The Two Paths to Knowledge: Definition and Proof
Introduces the two means of acquiring and conveying knowledge: definition and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between definition (mu'arraf) and proof (hujja) and identify which path is appropriate for concepts versus assents.

Core Idea
Concepts are conveyed by definition; assents are established by proof.

In this lesson, we explore the two fundamental paths that logic provides for acquiring and transmitting knowledge. When we have a concept in mind that we need to explain to others, we use definition. When we need to prove the truth or falsehood of a statement, we use proof. The passage clarifies the roles of these two tools, setting the stage for the detailed rules that follow.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two types of mental perceptions
The passage begins by reminding us that mental perceptions are of two kinds: concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). Identify which path corresponds to each.
This distinction is the basis for the two paths.
2
Identify the path for concepts
Find where the passage states that the path for concepts is called 'mu'arraf' or 'qawl sharih'. Note its function: to convey the form of a term to the mind or to distinguish it from others.
3
Identify the path for assents
Locate the description of the second path, which uses theoretical proof (hujja) to establish positive or negative assents or to persuade others.
This path is about proving statements, not defining terms.
4
Contrast the two paths
Explain how the two paths differ in purpose and application. Definition handles unknown concepts; proof handles unknown assents.
Both rely on rules (dawabit) that will be studied later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعرّف والحجّة عرفنا فيما سبق أن المدركات الذهنية منحصرة في التصورات والتصديقات، فإذا أردنا أن ننقل هذه المدركات الواقعة في أذهاننا إلى آخرين لا يعرفونها، ولم يكن لدينا وسائل حسيّة أو أدلة مادية نستطيع بها أن نجعلهم يدركونها كما أدركناها نحن، وإذا أردنا أيضاً أن نتعرف نحن على مجهولات تصورية أو تصديقية ليس لدينا معرفة بها، وليس لدينا وسائل حسيّة أو أدلة مادية نستطيع بها معرفتها، ونحن بحاجة إلى من يعرّفنا بها أو يثبتها لنا بالدليل، وكذلك إذا أردنا أن نلزم الآخرين بمدرك تصديقي غير مُسَلَّم به. فإنّ علمنا حينئذٍ لتحقيق هذه المطالب ينحصر في واحدٍ من طريقين: الطريق الأول: طريق التعريف بصور المفردات، وهذا الطريق من شأنه أن ينقل إلى الآخرين أمثلة التصورات الواقعة في أذهاننا. والطريق الموصل إلى التصورات يسمى (معرّفاً) ويسمى أيضاً (قولاً شارحاً) أي قولاً شارحاً للمفرد المطلوب حصول صورته في الذهن أو تمييزه عن غيره. وللمعرف ضوابط لا بد من التزامها فيه حتى يكون سليماً موصلاً إلى تصور صحيح أو تمييز واضح، ولهذه الضوابط مبحث خاصّ في هذا الفن. الطريق الثاني: طريق إقامة الأدلة النظرية للوصول إلى معرفة معلوم من المعلومات التصديقية الموجبة أو السالبة، أو لإقناع الآخرين به.
The Definition and the Proof. We have known previously that mental perceptions are confined to concepts and assents. So if we want to convey these perceptions occurring in our minds to others who do not know them, and we have no sensory means or material proofs by which we can make them perceive them as we perceived them, and if we also want to get to know unknown concepts or assents of which we have no knowledge, and we have no sensory means or material proofs by which we can know them, and we need someone to define them for us or prove them to us by proof, and likewise if we want to compel others to an assent not granted, then our knowledge for achieving these demands is confined to one of two paths: The first path: the path of definition by the forms of terms, and this path serves to convey to others examples of concepts occurring in our minds. And the path leading to concepts is called 'definition' (mu'arraf) and also called 'explanatory statement' (qawl sharih), i.e., an explanatory statement of the term whose form is sought to be obtained in the mind or distinguished from others. The definition has rules that must be adhered to for it to be sound, leading to a correct concept or clear distinction, and these rules have a special topic in this art. The second path: the path of providing theoretical proofs to arrive at knowledge of a known piece of information from the affirmative or negative assents, or to convince others of it.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage identifies the two fundamental ways we acquire and transmit knowledge in logic. It begins by recalling that mental perceptions are of two kinds: concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat). When we wish to communicate a concept we have in mind to someone else, and we lack direct sensory or material means to show it, we must use definition. The same holds if we ourselves seek to learn an unknown concept: we need someone to define it for us. The definition is called 'mu'arraf' or 'qawl sharih' (explanatory statement). Its function is to produce the form of the term in the mind or to distinguish it from other terms. The text notes that there are specific rules (dawabit) governing definitions, which will be discussed later in the art. The second path is for assents: when we need to establish a truth (positive or negative) or persuade others of it, we use theoretical proof (hujja). Thus, the science of logic provides tools for both defining concepts and proving statements. This lays the foundation for the rest of the course, which will examine the rules of definition and the forms of proof.

Key Terms
معرف
قول شارح
حجة

Checkpoint

A) To prove that a certain statement is true or false.
B) To transfer concepts from the mind to others or to distinguish a term from others.
C) To provide sensory evidence for an object.
D) To compel assent to a proposition.

Key Takeaway
Logic has two primary tools: definition for conveying concepts and proof for establishing assents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the statement: 'The world is created because it changes.' Is this a definition (mu'arraf) or a proof (hujja)? Explain your answer based on the passage.
?
2. Write a definition (mu'arraf) for 'wajib' (obligatory act) in usul al-fiqh. Ensure it conveys the concept and distinguishes it from other categories.

The Two Main Objectives of Logic: Definitions and Proofs
Author outlines the two main investigations of logic: definitions and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main investigations of logic (definitions and proofs) as outlined in the passage and explain the role of each in transferring concepts and establishing assents.

Core Idea
Logic has two complementary pillars: definitions for concepts and proofs for assents.

This passage from Dawabit al-Ma'rifa sets the stage for the entire study of logic. The author distills the purpose of logic into two fundamental tasks: transferring concepts to others through definitions (ta'rifat) and establishing theoretical assents through proofs (hujaj). Understanding this dual structure is essential before diving into the rules of each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Investigations
Locate in the Arabic where the author says the purpose of the art is confined to two investigations: definitions (التعريفات) and arguments/proofs (الحجج والأدلة).
This is the central thesis of the passage.
2
Understand the Function of Definitions
The passage says definitions are the verbal way to transfer tasawwurat (concepts) to others. Note that tasawwur is simple apprehension without judgment.
Definitions are for conceptual clarity.
3
Understand the Function of Proofs
Proofs lead to tasdiqat (assents). They are used to reach theoretical information beyond sensory evidence and to convince others dialectically.
Proofs are for establishing truth and compelling acceptance.
4
Recognize Key Terminology: Hujja and Dalil
The passage defines hujja/dalil as the way to a desired assent. In usul al-fiqh, dalil includes textual sources; here it is strictly logical argument.
Be aware of the shift in scope.
5
Outline the Pedagogical Plan
The passage states there are general principles common to both investigations, and specific principles for each. The lesson order covers common principles first, then each investigation's specific principles before delving into them.
This is a roadmap for the course.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الطريق من شأنه أن يوصل إلى التصديقات، والطريق الموصل إلى مطلوب تصديقي يسمى (حجّة) ويسمى أيضاً (دليلاً)، ولهذا الطريق ضوابط وقواعد لا بد من التزامها حتى تكون الحجة سليمة مقنعة ملزمة بالنتيجة، ولهذه الضوابط والقواعد مبحث خاص في هذا الفن. وبشيء من التأمل يظهر لنا أنّ المقصود الأساسي من هذا الفن ينحصر في مبحثين : المبحث الأول: التعريفات، إذ هي الطريق الكلامي الذي تُنقل به التصورات إلى الآخرين. المبحث الثاني: الحجج والأدلة، إذ هي طريق الوصول إلى معلومات تصديقية نظرية ليس لدينا وسائل حسية أو أدلة مادية للوصول إليها، وهي أيضاً طريق إقناع الآخرين بها، وإلزامهم جدلياً بقبولها والتسليم بها. ولكل من هذين المبحثين مبادىء عامة لا بد من معرفتها قبل الشروع فيه . إذن : فستتعرف على هذه المبادىء التي لا بد من التعرف عليها أولاً، ومن هذه المبادىء ما يحتاج إليه في كل من المبحثين، ومنها ما هو خاص بواحد منهما دون الآخر، لذلك فستتعرف على المبادىء العامة المشتركة، ثم نأتي بالمبادىء المتعلقة بكل مبحث منهما قبل الشروع فيه .
And this way is such that it leads to assents (tasdiqat), and the way that leads to a desired assent is called (hujja) and also called (dalil). This way has controls and rules that must be adhered to so that the argument be sound, convincing, and compelling in conclusion. For these controls and rules there is a special investigation in this art. With some reflection it appears to us that the fundamental purpose of this art is confined to two investigations: The first investigation: definitions, for they are the verbal way by which concepts are transferred to others. The second investigation: arguments and proofs, for they are the way to reach theoretical assent-based information for which we do not have sensory means or material evidence to reach them, and they are also a way to convince others of them and to compel them dialectically to accept and submit to them. For each of these two investigations there are general principles that must be known before embarking upon them. Therefore, you will become acquainted with these principles that must be recognized first. Some of these principles are needed in both investigations, and some are specific to one of them without the other. So you will become acquainted with the shared general principles, then we come to the principles related to each investigation before embarking upon it.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction explaining the two central purposes of logic: definitions (for conveying concepts) and proofs (for establishing assents). The author states that the way leading to a desired assent is called a hujja or dalil, and this way has specific controls and rules studied in logic. Reflection shows that the art of logic essentially comprises two investigations: the first is definitions (ta'rifat), which are the verbal means to transfer tasawwurat (concepts) to others; the second is arguments and proofs (hujaj wa adilla), which are the means to reach theoretical assents (tasdiqat) that cannot be attained through sensory or material evidence, and also serve to convince others dialectically. Both investigations require general principles that must be known beforehand. The author then outlines the pedagogical plan: first the common general principles, then the specific principles for each investigation before entering into it. Note that the passage uses 'tasawwurat' (concepts) and 'tasdiqat' (assents) without explicit definition, relying on the reader's prior familiarity. This mirrors the classical distinction in Islamic logic between conceptualization and assent.

Key Terms
حجة
دليل
تصورات
تصديقات
تعريفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Logic systematically studies both the transfer of concepts (definitions) and the establishment of assents (proofs), requiring distinct principles for each.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a real example from Usul al-Fiqh where a jurist first defines a term (e.g., 'what is qiyas?') and then uses a proof to establish a ruling in a new case. Describe how the definition and proof together illustrate the two investigations of logic.
?
In your own words, explain why the transfer of concepts (tasawwurat) via definitions is a separate investigation from the establishment of assents (tasdiqat) via proofs. Use the terminology of the passage.

Types of Indication: Natural, Conventional Non-Verbal, and Verbal
Author distinguishes natural from conventional indication and defines the scope of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish natural, conventional non-verbal, and conventional verbal indications, and identify examples of each from Islamic contexts.

Core Idea
Logic concerns itself only with verbal conventional indication, not natural signs or non-verbal symbols.

In this passage, the author categorizes indication (dalala) into natural and conventional types, then subdivides conventional into non-verbal and verbal. Understanding this classification is essential because logic (mantiq) focuses exclusively on verbal conventional indication—the way words convey meanings by linguistic convention. Let's break down the distinctions with examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of indication
Read the source arabic and locate where the author introduces natural indication (dalala tabi'iyya), conventional indication (dalala wad'iyya), and then subdivides conventional into non-verbal and verbal.
The passage begins with natural examples, then moves to conventional with a 'Third' marker.
2
Distinguish natural from conventional
Natural indication is based on causal or existential relations (e.g., rain indicating a fertile year). The intellect cannot guarantee its truth. Conventional indication is based on human agreement (e.g., traffic signs). Note the difference in certainty.
Natural indication is empirical and fallible; conventional is arbitrary but agreed upon.
3
Subdivide conventional indication
Within conventional, the author separates non-verbal (e.g., traffic signs, school bells) from verbal (words). Verbal conventional indication is further specified as the subject of logic.
Non-verbal conventional signs are not studied in logic; only verbal ones are.
4
Apply the classification to Islamic examples
Think of examples from Islamic sciences: the adhan sound indicating prayer time (conventional non-verbal), the word 'salat' indicating ritual prayer (verbal conventional), and smoke indicating fire (natural). Categorize each.
Use the criteria: is it based on agreement? Is it verbal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودلالة كثرة الأمطار على السنة المخصبة، ودلالة شحها على السنة المجدبة؛ فهذه وأشباهها دلالات طبيعية، لا يمنع العقل المجرد عن ملاحظة الواقع من تخلفها وعدم صدقها . ثالثاً : وإما أن تكون دلالة وضعية، وهي دلالة شيء ما تواضع الناس في اصطلاحهم على أن يكون دالاً على معنى معين، وقد يكون هذا الشيء مَعْلماً من المعالم، أو رسماً من الرسوم، أو لفظاً من الألفاظ . مثال ذلك : دلالات إشارات المرور، ودلالات الرسوم التي توضع على لوحات لإرشاد سائقي السيارات، ودلالة صورة السهم «←» على تحديد الاتجاه، ودلالة رنين الجرس في المدرسة على بداية الدرس أو نهايته، فهذه وما أشبهها دلالات وضعية غير لفظية، أما الدلالة الوضعية اللفظية فهي دلالة الألفاظ على المعاني بوساطة الوضع اللغوي، سواء كانت دلالة اللفظ على المعنى واردة على سبيل الحقيقة أو على سبيل المجاز، كدلالة خفض الجناح على معنى التواضع، وهذه الدلالة اللفظية الوضعية هي المقصودة في هذا الفن
And the indication of abundant rain on a fertile year, and the indication of its scarcity on a barren year; these and their likes are natural indications, and the bare intellect, without observing reality, cannot prevent them from being invalid and false. Third: It may be a conventional indication, which is the indication of something that people have agreed in their convention to be indicative of a specific meaning, and this thing may be a landmark, a drawing, or a word. For example: the indications of traffic signs, indications of drawings placed on boards to guide car drivers, the indication of an arrow image '←' for determining direction, and the indication of a school bell ringing for the beginning or end of a lesson. These and their likes are non-verbal conventional indications. As for the verbal conventional indication, it is the indication of words for meanings by means of linguistic convention, whether the word's indication of the meaning occurs literally or metaphorically, like the indication of lowering the wing for humility. And this verbal conventional indication is what is intended in this art.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies natural and conventional indication. The author first gives examples of natural indication (rain and fertility) and notes that the intellect cannot guarantee their truth. Then he defines conventional indication, providing non-verbal examples like traffic signs and school bells. Finally, he defines verbal conventional indication, which is the proper subject of logic, and notes it includes literal and metaphorical usage. The point is to distinguish the various types of indication and to clarify that logic concerns itself with verbal conventional indication.

Key Terms
دلالة طبيعية
دلالة وضعية
دلالة وضعية لفظية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Natural indication
B) Conventional non-verbal indication
C) Verbal conventional indication
D) Metaphorical indication

Key Takeaway
Verbal conventional indication is the sole subject of logic, as natural and non-verbal conventional signs are not studied in this art.

Research Methods in Logic: Division, Collection, Analysis, Synthesis
Author presents the second section outlining knowledge and its methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four operations of research (division, collection, analysis, synthesis) and explain their role in Islamic logical methodology.

Core Idea
The four operations transform raw sense data into defined concepts and valid arguments.

The author introduces the second section of his treatise, which moves from the general definition of logic to the specific methods by which knowledge is acquired. These methods are not mere techniques; they are the foundational intellectual operations that underlie every act of definition, classification, and proof in the Islamic sciences. In this lesson, we will parse each operation and see how it functions in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four operations in the Arabic
Find the terms 'al-taqsim' (division), 'al-jam‘' (collection), 'al-tahlil' (analysis), and 'al-tarkib' (synthesis) in the passage. Write them down.
2
Define each operation in your own words
For each operation, write a one-sentence definition based on the commentary provided. For example, division is partitioning a genus into species.
Use the key terms definitions for guidance.
3
Identify the opposites or complements
Notice that division and collection are opposites; analysis and synthesis are opposites. How do they complement each other?
In logic, division and collection help organize concepts; analysis and synthesis help construct definitions.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Choose one operation and give a concrete example from fiqh or kalam. For division, think of the classification of acts into wajib, mandub, etc. For analysis, think of analyzing the world into substances and accidents.
This shows the practical use of these operations in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسْمُ الثَّانِي
١ - المعرفة وطرق الوصول إليها .
٢ - وسائل البحث :
أ - عمليات التقسيم والجمع .
ب - عمليات التحليل والتركيب .
The Second Section: 1 - Knowledge and ways to reach it. 2 - Research methods: a - Operations of division and collection. b - Operations of analysis and synthesis.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage introduces the second section of the work, which covers knowledge and the methods to attain it. The author enumerates two main topics: first, knowledge itself and the paths to reach it; second, research methods, which are further subdivided into (a) operations of division and collection (al-taqsim wa al-jam‘) and (b) operations of analysis and synthesis (al-tahlil wa al-tarkib). These terms are foundational to the methodology of logic and epistemology. Division and collection refer to classifying things into categories and then bringing them together under general principles. Analysis and synthesis refer to breaking down complex concepts into simpler parts and then recombining them. In the Islamic intellectual tradition, these operations are essential for constructing definitions (hudud) and arguments. For example, in fiqh, division of acts into wajib, haram, etc., or in kalam, analysis of the world into atoms and accidents.

Key Terms
المعرفة
طرق الوصول
التقسيم
الجمع
التحليل
التركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) al-taqsim wa al-jam‘
C) al-ma‘rifa wa turuq al-wusul
D) al-mantiq wa al-jadal

Key Takeaway
The four operations of taqsim, jam‘, tahlil, and tarkib are the methodological backbone of Islamic logic, enabling systematic thought in all sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

External Senses: Their Role and Limitations
Author describes external senses as sources of knowledge and their limitations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantitative and qualitative limitations of the external senses described in the passage and explain why senses alone cannot yield certain knowledge.

Core Idea
External senses are necessary but insufficient; they are limited in range and depend on conditions.

The passage begins by explaining how external senses transmit attributes of objects to the intellect. However, it immediately reminds us that these senses are ‘very limited’ – both in how far they can reach (quantity) and what conditions they require (quality). Understanding these limitations is crucial because it shows why Islamic scholars have always insisted that sense perception must be supplemented by rational principles and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of limitations
The author divides sensory limitations into quantitative (distance) and qualitative (conditions like light for sight). Find the Arabic phrases that indicate these two categories.
Look for 'من جهة الكم' and 'من جهة الكيف'.
2
Give an example for each limitation
For quantity: when the distance is too great, senses fail. For quality: sight needs light, extreme smallness requires microscopes. Find these examples in the text.
3
Explain the epistemological implication
Because the senses are limited, the raw data they provide is incomplete. This means that knowledge based solely on sense perception cannot be certain. The intellect must step in with innate principles.
This connects to the later discussion of al-mawāzīn al-fiṭriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحسّ به وتنقله من صفات الأشياء إلى منطقة الإدراك الفكري، ثم تسجل الذاكرة لديها ما تؤكده الحواس بتكرار التجربة، وبعد ذلك يبدأ الفكر عمله فيما سجلته الذاكرة من صور وردت إليها عن طريق الحواس الظاهرة.
ولا يخفى علينا أن هذه الحواس محدودة جداً لا تدرك كل شيء يمكن إدراكه في الوجود، إنها منافذ قصيرة المدى محدودة كماً وكيفاً.
فمن جهة الكم نلاحظ أنه متى تجاوز البعد المسافة التي تسمح لنا بالإحساس ظهر عجز حواسنا عن إدراك الأشياء.
ومن جهة الكيف نلاحظ أنه لا بد من مرافقة شروط خاصة لكل حاسة فينا حتى نستطيع بوساطتها إدراك الأشياء المعروضة على حسّنا، فحاسة البصر مثلاً تحتاج إلى الضوء حتى ترى الأشياء، ومتى انعدم الضوء وحل الظلام الدامس انعدمت الرؤية تماماً، وكذلك متى صغرت الأشياء إلى المراتب الدنيا في الصغر لم نستطع رؤيتها إلا بوساطة المجاهر المكبرة إلى ملايين المرّات أحياناً، وهكذا تحتاج كل حاسة إلى توافر شروط خاصة حتى تدرك محسوسها.
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الظاهرة قوة وضعفاً.
ب ـ ونظير ما نشعر به بالحواس الظاهرة ما نشعر به بالحواس الباطنة، إذ يمر الإنسان فيها بخبرات كثيرة، فيدرك فكره هذه الخبرات، وتسجلها الذاكرة، ثم يبدأ الفكر عمله فيما هو مسجل لديه منها.
فمثلاً يمر الإنسان بخبرة حب، فيعرف من الحب بمقدار مشاعره حوله، ويمر بخبرة كراهية، فيعرف منها بمقدار مشاعره حولها، ويمر بخبرة ضيق صدر، فيعرف ذلك، ويمر بخبرة انشراح صدر، فيعرف ذلك، ويمر بخبرات حقد وحسد وبغضاء، فيعرف من صفات هذه الأشياء ما أحس به في داخل نفسه منها، وتلتقطها الذاكرة، ويضعها الفكر مواد بحث بين يديه.
والحواس الباطنة محدودة، كما أن الحواس الظاهرة محدودة.
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الباطنة قوةً وضعفاً، وبعض الحواس الباطنة يمتاز بها بعض الموهوبين امتيازاً فائقاً، وللأنبياء نصيب من امتياز الحواس الباطنة لا يوجد عند غيرهم.
وهكذا تغدو الخبرات التي يمارسها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة والباطنة أولى وسائل اكتسابه للمعارف.
جـ - والفكر يمارس أعماله بعد أن توجد لديه مجموعة من مواد المعرفة، التي وردت إليه عن طريق الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، ويضيف إليها موازين أساسية موجودة لديه في أصل فطرته، قد يطلق عليها قوانين الفكر العقلية، وبهذه الموازين الفطرية يستطيع العقل أن يحكم على كثير من الأفكار بالإثبات أو بالنفي.
وبهذه الموازين الفطرية العقلية يحكم العقل أحكاماً ذاتية فيما يلي:
١ - إنه يحكم بالتوحيد بين الفكرة وماهيتها المكونة لها، وهو المعبر عنه، بما هو هو.
٢ - إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض أو ما يؤدي إلى التناقض، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون عدم التناقض). ككون الشيء موجوداً ومعدوماً في آن واحد ومكان واحد، وكون جزء الشيء أكبر من كله، مع محافظة الجزء على كونه جزءاً، ومحافظة الكل على كونه كلاً لهذا الجزء.
٣ - ويحكم العقل بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون الامتناع) أو (قانون الوسط المرفوع) إذ ليس بين الوجود والعدم وسط يعتبر شيئاً ثالثاً غير الوجود والعدم، وهذا هو ما يعبر عنه باستحالة رفع النقيضين.
٤ - ويحكم العقل بواحد من أحكامه الثلاثة التي يحكم بها على كل ما يقع في تصوره، وهي: (واجب الوجود فلا يُتصوّر في العقل عدمه - واجب
You sense it and transfer it from the attributes of things to the area of intellectual perception, then the memory records what the senses confirm by repetition of experience, and after that the intellect begins its work on what the memory has recorded of images that came to it through the external senses. It is not hidden from us that these senses are very limited, they do not perceive everything that can be perceived in existence; they are short-range outlets limited in quantity and quality. In terms of quantity, we observe that whenever the distance exceeds the distance that allows us to sense, our senses become unable to perceive things. In terms of quality, we observe that there must be special conditions accompanying each of our senses so that we can perceive the things presented to our sense; for example, the sense of sight needs light to see things, and when light is absent and complete darkness sets in, vision is completely absent, and likewise, when things become extremely small, we cannot see them except with the help of magnifying microscopes sometimes to millions of times, and thus each sense needs specific conditions to perceive its object. People vary in the levels of their external senses, strength and weakness. B. Corresponding to what we feel with the external senses is what we feel with the internal senses, for a person experiences many experiences, his intellect perceives these experiences, the memory records them, then the intellect begins its work on what is recorded from them. For example, a person experiences the experience of love, so he knows love by the amount of his feelings about it, and he experiences the experience of hatred, so he knows it by the amount of his feelings about it, and he experiences the experience of anxiety, so he knows that, and he experiences the experience of elation, so he knows that, and he experiences experiences of rancor, envy, and enmity, so he knows from the qualities of these things what he feels within himself from them, and the memory captures them, and the intellect places them as research materials before it. The internal senses are limited, just as the external senses are limited. People vary in the levels of their internal senses, strength and weakness, and some internal senses are distinguished by some gifted people in an outstanding way, and the prophets have a share of the excellence of internal senses that is not found in others. Thus, the experiences that a person undergoes through his external and internal senses become the first means of his acquisition of knowledge. C. The intellect carries out its work after there exists for it a set of materials of knowledge that have come to it through the external and internal senses, and it adds to them fundamental balances that exist within it in the foundation of its innate constitution, which may be called the intellectual laws of thought. With these innate balances, the intellect can judge many ideas positively or negatively. And with these innate intellectual balances, the intellect makes self-evident judgments about the following: 1. It judges the identity between an idea and its constituting essence, which is expressed as 'what it is'. 2. It judges the impossibility of contradiction or what leads to contradiction, and this goes back to the intellectual principle called the 'law of non-contradiction', such as a thing being existent and non-existent at the same time and place, and a part of a thing being greater than the whole, while the part remains a part and the whole remains a whole for this part. 3. The intellect judges that a thing is either existent or non-existent, and this goes back to the intellectual principle called the 'law of exclusion' or the 'law of the excluded middle', for there is no middle between existence and non-existence that is considered a third thing other than existence and non-existence, and this is what is expressed as the impossibility of raising both negations. 4. The intellect judges one of its three judgments that it makes about everything that falls within its conceptualization: (necessarily existent, so its non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect - necessarily
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the sources of knowledge and the innate principles of the intellect. The author begins by describing how external senses transmit attributes of objects to the intellect, which then works on the data stored in memory. He then highlights the limitations of the external senses: they are restricted in both quantity (distance) and quality (conditions like light for sight, magnification for small objects). People vary in sensory strength. Next, the author introduces internal senses, which also provide experiences (love, hatred, anxiety, etc.) that are similarly limited and vary among individuals, with prophets having a special excellence. The author then moves to the intellect itself. After accumulating sensory data, the intellect adds innate fundamental balances or intellectual laws. Four specific self-evident principles are enumerated: (1) The law of identity: every idea is identical to its essence. (2) The law of non-contradiction: it is impossible for a thing to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, or for a part to be greater than the whole while retaining its status as a part. (3) The law of excluded middle: everything is either existent or non-existent with no third option. (4) The beginning of the fourth principle mentions one of three necessary judgments about everything conceived: necessary existence (whose non-existence is inconceivable). The passage emphasizes that sensory experience alone is insufficient for knowledge; rational principles are necessary for judgment. For example, in Islamic theology, the law of non-contradiction ensures that Allah cannot be both merciful and unjust in the same respect, and the law of excluded middle underpins the principle that a thing is either obligatory or not obligatory, with no middle ground in certain contexts.

Key Terms
الحواس الظاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is an example of a qualitative limitation of the sense of sight?
A) Sight can only see things within a certain distance.
B) Sight requires light; in darkness vision fails.
C) Sight cannot perceive the emotions of others.
D) Sight varies in strength from person to person.

Key Takeaway
External senses are essential but limited; they cannot provide complete knowledge without the aid of rational principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the context of Islamic theology, provide an example of a claim that cannot be verified by the external senses alone (e.g., the attributes of Allah, the existence of angels). Explain why the senses are insufficient.

Internal Senses: Emotions as Knowledge
Author introduces internal senses as a source of experiential knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external and internal senses and describe how internal senses contribute to knowledge, including their limitations and the special excellence of prophets.

Core Idea
Internal senses provide experiential knowledge of emotions and inner states, which are also limited but can be refined.

After discussing external senses, the author introduces the internal senses – the faculties that perceive inner experiences like love, hatred, and anxiety. These too are limited, but they vary among people, and prophets have a unique share of excellence. This expands our understanding of how humans acquire knowledge beyond the physical senses.

Guided Path
1
List the internal experiences mentioned
The author lists love, hatred, anxiety, elation, rancor, envy, enmity. Read the Arabic and find these words.
Look for 'خبرة حب', 'خبرة كراهية', etc.
2
Contrast with external senses
External senses perceive physical objects; internal senses perceive emotional and mental states. How does the author distinguish them?
The phrase 'نظير ما نشعر به بالحواس الظاهرة' sets up the parallel.
3
Note the special excellence of prophets
The author says prophets have a share of excellence in internal senses not found in others. Why might this be relevant to prophecy?
This connects to the idea that prophets receive inner experiences (wahy) that are verified by their internal senses.

Innate Intellectual Principles: The Bedrock of Rational Judgment
Author enumerates the innate intellectual principles that the intellect uses to judge ideas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three innate intellectual principles (law of identity, law of non-contradiction, law of excluded middle) and apply them to an example from Islamic legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh).

Core Idea
The intellect adds innate principles to sensory data, enabling self-evident judgments that are foundational for all rational thought.

In the final part of the passage, the author explains that after gathering sensory data, the intellect applies innate 'balances' (al-mawāzīn al-fiṭriyya) – the laws of thought. He lists three self-evident principles: the law of identity, the law of non-contradiction, and the law of excluded middle. These principles are not learned from experience but are hardwired into the human intellect, enabling it to judge with certainty. Understanding these principles is essential for anyone who wants to reason correctly in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the three principles in the Arabic text
The author numbers four points, but the fourth is incomplete. Focus on the first three: (1) al-tawḥīd bayna al-fikrah wa māhiyyatihā (law of identity), (2) istiḥālat al-tanāquḍ (law of non-contradiction), (3) al-shayʾ immā mawjūd aw maʿdūm (law of excluded middle).
The text: 'إنه يحكم بالتوحيد بين الفكرة وماهيتها...' for (1); 'إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض...' for (2); 'إن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً...' for (3).
2
Restate each principle in your own words
For (1): every idea is identical to its essence. For (2): a thing cannot be both existent and non-existent at the same time and place. For (3): everything is either existent or non-existent; there is no third option.
3
Apply the law of non-contradiction to a fiqh example
In usul al-fiqh, an act cannot be both obligatory (wajib) and prohibited (haram) at the same time. This would violate the law of non-contradiction. The example shows how the principle underpins legal consistency.
You could also use the law of excluded middle: an act is either wajib or not wajib (e.g., mandub or mubah or makruh or haram). There is no middle state like 'both wajib and not wajib'.
4
Explain why these principles are innate (fitriyya)
The author says these balances exist 'fī aṣl fiṭratihi' (in the foundation of its innate constitution). No one teaches them; every rational person recognizes them once concepts are formed.
This is why they are called 'badīhi' (self-evident) in mantiq.

Source Text

ولا يخفى علينا أن هذه الحواس محدودة جداً لا تدرك كل شيء يمكن إدراكه في الوجود، إنها منافذ قصيرة المدى محدودة كماً وكيفاً.
فمن جهة الكم نلاحظ أنه متى تجاوز البعد المسافة التي تسمح لنا بالإحساس ظهر عجز حواسنا عن إدراك الأشياء.
ومن جهة الكيف نلاحظ أنه لا بد من مرافقة شروط خاصة لكل حاسة فينا حتى نستطيع بوساطتها إدراك الأشياء المعروضة على حسّنا، فحاسة البصر مثلاً تحتاج إلى الضوء حتى ترى الأشياء، ومتى انعدم الضوء وحل الظلام الدامس انعدمت الرؤية تماماً، وكذلك متى صغرت الأشياء إلى المراتب الدنيا في الصغر لم نستطع رؤيتها إلا بوساطة المجاهر المكبرة إلى ملايين المرّات أحياناً، وهكذا تحتاج كل حاسة إلى توافر شروط خاصة حتى تدرك محسوسها.
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الظاهرة قوة وضعفاً.
ب ـ ونظير ما نشعر به بالحواس الظاهرة ما نشعر به بالحواس الباطنة، إذ يمر الإنسان فيها بخبرات كثيرة، فيدرك فكره هذه الخبرات، وتسجلها الذاكرة، ثم يبدأ الفكر عمله فيما هو مسجل لديه منها.
فمثلاً يمر الإنسان بخبرة حب، فيعرف من الحب بمقدار مشاعره حوله، ويمر بخبرة كراهية، فيعرف منها بمقدار مشاعره حولها، ويمر بخبرة ضيق صدر، فيعرف ذلك، ويمر بخبرة انشراح صدر، فيعرف ذلك، ويمر بخبرات حقد وحسد وبغضاء، فيعرف من صفات هذه الأشياء ما أحس به في داخل نفسه منها، وتلتقطها الذاكرة، ويضعها الفكر مواد بحث بين يديه.
والحواس الباطنة محدودة، كما أن الحواس الظاهرة محدودة.
ويتفاوت الناس في مستويات حواسهم الباطنة قوةً وضعفاً، وبعض الحواس الباطنة يمتاز بها بعض الموهوبين امتيازاً فائقاً، وللأنبياء نصيب من امتياز الحواس الباطنة لا يوجد عند غيرهم.
وهكذا تغدو الخبرات التي يمارسها الإنسان بحواسه الظاهرة والباطنة أولى وسائل اكتسابه للمعارف.
جـ - والفكر يمارس أعماله بعد أن توجد لديه مجموعة من مواد المعرفة، التي وردت إليه عن طريق الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، ويضيف إليها موازين أساسية موجودة لديه في أصل فطرته، قد يطلق عليها قوانين الفكر العقلية، وبهذه الموازين الفطرية يستطيع العقل أن يحكم على كثير من الأفكار بالإثبات أو بالنفي.
وبهذه الموازين الفطرية العقلية يحكم العقل أحكاماً ذاتية فيما يلي:
١ - إنه يحكم بالتوحيد بين الفكرة وماهيتها المكونة لها، وهو المعبر عنه، بما هو هو.
٢ - إنه يحكم باستحالة التناقض أو ما يؤدي إلى التناقض، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون عدم التناقض). ككون الشيء موجوداً ومعدوماً في آن واحد ومكان واحد، وكون جزء الشيء أكبر من كله، مع محافظة الجزء على كونه جزءاً، ومحافظة الكل على كونه كلاً لهذا الجزء.
٣ - ويحكم العقل بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً وإما أن يكون معدوماً، وهذا يرجع إلى الأصل العقلي المسمى (قانون الامتناع) أو (قانون الوسط المرفوع) إذ ليس بين الوجود والعدم وسط يعتبر شيئاً ثالثاً غير الوجود والعدم، وهذا هو ما يعبر عنه باستحالة رفع النقيضين.
٤ - ويحكم العقل بواحد من أحكامه الثلاثة التي يحكم بها على كل ما يقع في تصوره، وهي: (واجب الوجود فلا يُتصوّر في العقل عدمه - واجب
You sense it and transfer it from the attributes of things to the area of intellectual perception, then the memory records what the senses confirm by repetition of experience, and after that the intellect begins its work on what the memory has recorded of images that came to it through the external senses. It is not hidden from us that these senses are very limited, they do not perceive everything that can be perceived in existence; they are short-range outlets limited in quantity and quality. In terms of quantity, we observe that whenever the distance exceeds the distance that allows us to sense, our senses become unable to perceive things. In terms of quality, we observe that there must be special conditions accompanying each of our senses so that we can perceive the things presented to our sense; for example, the sense of sight needs light to see things, and when light is absent and complete darkness sets in, vision is completely absent, and likewise, when things become extremely small, we cannot see them except with the help of magnifying microscopes sometimes to millions of times, and thus each sense needs specific conditions to perceive its object. People vary in the levels of their external senses, strength and weakness. B. Corresponding to what we feel with the external senses is what we feel with the internal senses, for a person experiences many experiences, his intellect perceives these experiences, the memory records them, then the intellect begins its work on what is recorded from them. For example, a person experiences the experience of love, so he knows love by the amount of his feelings about it, and he experiences the experience of hatred, so he knows it by the amount of his feelings about it, and he experiences the experience of anxiety, so he knows that, and he experiences the experience of elation, so he knows that, and he experiences experiences of rancor, envy, and enmity, so he knows from the qualities of these things what he feels within himself from them, and the memory captures them, and the intellect places them as research materials before it. The internal senses are limited, just as the external senses are limited. People vary in the levels of their internal senses, strength and weakness, and some internal senses are distinguished by some gifted people in an outstanding way, and the prophets have a share of the excellence of internal senses that is not found in others. Thus, the experiences that a person undergoes through his external and internal senses become the first means of his acquisition of knowledge. C. The intellect carries out its work after there exists for it a set of materials of knowledge that have come to it through the external and internal senses, and it adds to them fundamental balances that exist within it in the foundation of its innate constitution, which may be called the intellectual laws of thought. With these innate balances, the intellect can judge many ideas positively or negatively. And with these innate intellectual balances, the intellect makes self-evident judgments about the following: 1. It judges the identity between an idea and its constituting essence, which is expressed as 'what it is'. 2. It judges the impossibility of contradiction or what leads to contradiction, and this goes back to the intellectual principle called the 'law of non-contradiction', such as a thing being existent and non-existent at the same time and place, and a part of a thing being greater than the whole, while the part remains a part and the whole remains a whole for this part. 3. The intellect judges that a thing is either existent or non-existent, and this goes back to the intellectual principle called the 'law of exclusion' or the 'law of the excluded middle', for there is no middle between existence and non-existence that is considered a third thing other than existence and non-existence, and this is what is expressed as the impossibility of raising both negations. 4. The intellect judges one of its three judgments that it makes about everything that falls within its conceptualization: (necessarily existent, so its non-existence is inconceivable in the intellect - necessarily
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the sources of knowledge and the innate principles of the intellect. The author begins by describing how external senses transmit attributes of objects to the intellect, which then works on the data stored in memory. He then highlights the limitations of the external senses: they are restricted in both quantity (distance) and quality (conditions like light for sight, magnification for small objects). People vary in sensory strength. Next, the author introduces internal senses, which also provide experiences (love, hatred, anxiety, etc.) that are similarly limited and vary among individuals, with prophets having a special excellence. The author then moves to the intellect itself. After accumulating sensory data, the intellect adds innate fundamental balances or intellectual laws. Four specific self-evident principles are enumerated: (1) The law of identity: every idea is identical to its essence. (2) The law of non-contradiction: it is impossible for a thing to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously, or for a part to be greater than the whole while retaining its status as a part. (3) The law of excluded middle: everything is either existent or non-existent with no third option. (4) The beginning of the fourth principle mentions one of three necessary judgments about everything conceived: necessary existence (whose non-existence is inconceivable). The passage emphasizes that sensory experience alone is insufficient for knowledge; rational principles are necessary for judgment. For example, in Islamic theology, the law of non-contradiction ensures that Allah cannot be both merciful and unjust in the same respect, and the law of excluded middle underpins the principle that a thing is either obligatory or not obligatory, with no middle ground in certain contexts.

Key Terms
الموازين الفطرية
قانون عدم التناقض
قانون الامتناع

A) Law of identity
B) Law of non-contradiction
C) Law of excluded middle
D) Law of sufficient reason

Key Takeaway
The intellect innately possesses the laws of identity, non-contradiction, and excluded middle, which are foundational for all rational judgment in the Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following statement: 'Zakat is both obligatory and recommended for someone who meets the nisab.' Using the law of non-contradiction, explain why this statement is impossible. Then, using the law of excluded middle, state what categories Zakat must fall into.

The Intellect's Extrapolative Abilities
Author describes the intellect's extrapolative abilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain three types of extrapolation the intellect performs: from perfection to deficiency, beauty to ugliness, and infinite variation in quantities and qualities.

Core Idea
The intellect can conceive opposite extremes and infinite variations without limit.

The author lists four examples of the intellect's ability to extrapolate from what it perceives. These abilities show that the mind is not passive but actively generates possibilities. Understanding these operations is crucial for grasping how the intellect forms concepts and judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four examples
Read points 9-12. The intellect (1) goes from perfection to deficiency, (2) from beauty to ugliness, (3) varies quantities infinitely, (4) varies qualities infinitely. Note that the first two are opposite extremes, the last two are infinite variations.
The wax example illustrates quality variation.
2
Distinguish the two types of extrapolation
The first two involve moving along a linear spectrum between opposites (perfect/deficient, beautiful/ugly). The last two involve changing a parameter (quantity or quality) without an inherent opposite, only degrees.
This distinction is important for understanding how the intellect forms different kinds of concepts.
3
Relate to Islamic epistemology
The intellect's ability to vary quantities is used in fiqh when determining legal measurements or penalties. Its ability to vary qualities is used in evaluating the strength of an illah (effective cause) in qiyas.
For example, the concept of 'intoxication' can be qualified by degree (weak vs. strong).

The ladder of perfection of this thing, knowing it degree by degree, within the laws of integration. 9 - And when the intellect perceives a perfect thing or one ascending in degrees of perfection, it can by itself proceed to conceive possibilities of its deficiency degree by degree down to its lowest. 10 - And when the intellect perceives beauty and senses its loci of beauty, it can by itself proceed to conceive the possibilities that distort and make it ugly so that it becomes not beautiful. 11 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of quantities, increasing and decreasing them to infinity; it increases and decreases quantities, increases and decreases spatial dimensions, increases and decreases temporal dimensions, increases and decreases measures of heat, increases and decreases measures of pressure, and so on for everything subject to measurement. 12 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of differences of qualities to infinity, changing and altering them without limit; if it holds a piece of pliable wax and begins to propose possibilities of changing its quality, its changes would not stop at a limit. And it is upon the intellect to classify its cognitions and place each of them in its rank and degree that it deserves, within its innate and acquired laws. Summary of methods of knowledge: If we wish to summarize the principles of methods of knowledge, it becomes clear to us that they go back to the following methods: The first method: sensory perception through the external or internal senses, and it includes experiential knowledge (mujarrabat), because its perception depends on observation of the senses, in addition to a mental generalization that comes through analogy (tamthil). The second method: perception of what is contained in the testimonies of others and their reports. The third method: perception of the inherent laws of the intellect, and perception of what the intellect deduces of cognitions from what has come to it through the previous two methods. And the intellect, with what it has of innate scales - which are further from error than the testimonies of the senses and the testimonies of other people who are not infallible - is considered a judge to be referred to in scrutinizing sensory perceptions and scrutinizing what is contained in the testimonies and reports of others; it affirms what it sees as true with certainty, accepts what is preponderant among them, discards what it doubts or lowers it below preponderance, and rejects what it judges as impossible and absurd based on its inherent laws. Not everything that enters the perceiving intellectual apparatus is on one level; rather it is on levels, the highest of which is truth with certainty and the lowest falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion. How do we distinguish between the levels of perception? 1 - When the sense presents its confirmed decisive testimony after repeated sensory processes, and the testimonies of other senses and testimonies of the senses of others agree with it, and it does not contradict a principle of the principles of the intellect and its laws, the knowledge becomes
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the intellect's operations and the sources and levels of knowledge. It begins by listing four abilities of the intellect: to conceive degrees of perfection, to conceive degrees of ugliness, to conceive infinite variations in quantities (such as heat, pressure, etc.), and to conceive infinite variations in qualities (exemplified by molding wax). The author then points out that the intellect works with primary materials from the senses, reports of others, and its own inherent laws and imagination. However, not every perception is certain; the intellect must classify its cognitions into five levels previously mentioned: truth with certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt, weak opinion (illusion), and falsehood with certainty. Then the author summarizes the three methods of knowledge: (1) sensory perception, including experiential knowledge obtained through repeated sensory observation with mental generalization via analogy (tamthil); (2) reports and testimonies of others; (3) the intellect's deduction from its own inherent laws and from materials obtained through the first two methods. The intellect, having innate scales that are more reliable than fallible senses and fallible human testimony, acts as a judge to test perceptions: it affirms certain truth, accepts probable opinion, discards doubt or weak opinion, and rejects what is impossible according to its laws. The passage ends with an incomplete discussion of how to distinguish levels of perception, starting with the condition of consistent sense testimony.

The Five Levels of Perception
Author lists the five levels of perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five levels of perception and give a real example from Islamic knowledge classification for each.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not monolithic; it spans five levels from certain truth to certain falsehood.

After explaining the intellect's active nature, the author reminds us that not every perception is certain. He lists five categories: truth with certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt, weak opinion (illusion), and falsehood with certainty. This classification is essential for the intellect to correctly assign epistemic weight to its cognitions.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five levels in order
From highest to lowest: al-haqq bi-yaqin (truth with certainty), al-zann al-rajih (preponderant opinion), al-shakk (doubt), al-zann al-marjuh (weak opinion/illusion), al-batil bi-yaqin (falsehood with certainty).
The author had previously explained these; here he repeats them.
2
Distinguish preponderant from weak opinion
Preponderant opinion (zann rajih) is when one side is more likely, weak opinion (zann marjuh) is when the weaker side is considered, often called 'wahm' (illusion).
In usul al-fiqh, zann rajih is the basis for many practical rulings.
3
Classify an example
Take the statement 'The Friday prayer is obligatory.' According to the sources, this is truth with certainty. The statement 'The minimum dowry is 10 dirhams' (based on weak evidence) would be weak opinion or illusion if contradicted by stronger evidence.
This mirrors how jurists rank evidence: mutawatir gives certainty, ahad gives preponderant opinion, etc.

سُلَّم كمال هذا الشيء متعرفاً على درجاته درجة فدرجة، ضمن قوانين التكامل. ۹ - ومتى أدرك العقل الشيء الكامل أو المرتقي في درجات الكمال، فإنه يستطيع بنفسه أن ينطلق فيتصور احتمالات نقصه دركة فدركة حتى أسفلها . ۱۰ - ومتى أدرك العقل الجميل وأحسّ بمواطن جماله استطاع أن ينطلق بنفسه فيتصور الاحتمالات التي تشوّهه وتقبّحه فتجعله غير جميل . ۱۱ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات المقادير فيزيد فيها وينقص إلى ما لا نهاية له ؛ فيزيد الكميات وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد المكانية وينقصها، ويزيد الأبعاد الزمانية وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الحرارة وينقصها، ويزيد مقادير الضغط وينقصها، وهكذا إلى كل شيء خاضع لحساب المقادير . ۱۲ - ويستطيع العقل بنفسه أن ينطلق في إدراك احتمالات اختلاف الكيفيات إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيغير فيها ويبدّل بشكل لا حصر له، فلو أمسك عجينة من الشمع المطاوع، وانطلق يطرح احتمالات تغيير كيفيتها لما وقفت تغييراته عند حد . وهكذا يتضح لنا كيف يقوم العقل بأعماله التي لا حصر لها، ضمن المواد الأولى التي تصل إليه عن طريق الحسّ الظاهر أو الباطن، أو التي تصل إليه عن طريق أخبار الآخرين وشهاداتهم، مضافاً إليها ما لديه من قوانين ذاتية، وما لديه من قدرة على التخيل . ولكن ينبغي أن نعلم أنه ليس كل ما يدركه الفكر هو من قبيل اليقين، بل هو ينقسم إلى أقسام الإدراك التي سبق بيانها : [الحق بيقين - الظن الراجح - الشك - الظن المرجوح (الوهم) - الباطل بيقين]. وعلى العقل أن يصنف معارفه، ويضع كلاً منها في مرتبته ودرجته التي يستحقها، ضمن قوانينه الفطرية والمكتسبة. خلاصة طرق المعرفة: وإذا أردنا أن نلخص أصول طرق المعرفة تبين لنا أنها ترجع إلى الطرق التالية : الطريق الأول: الإدراك الحسيّ بالحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة، ويدخل فيه المجربات، لأن إدراكها يعتمد على ملاحظة الحسّ، مضافاً إليها تعميم عقلي يأتي عن طريق التمثيل. الطريق الثاني: إدراك ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف . الطريق الثالث: إدراك قوانين العقل الذاتية، وإدراك ما يستنبطه العقل من معارف مما ورد إليه عن الطريقين السابقين. والعقل بما لديه من موازين فطرية - هي أبعد عن الخطأ من شهادات الحسّ، وشهادات الآخرين من الناس غير المعصومين - يعتبر حكماً يرجع إليه في تمحيص المدركات بالحسّ، وتمحيص ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف، فيجزم بما يراه حقاً يقيناً، ويقبل ما يترجح لديه منها، ويطرح ما يشك به أو ينزله إلى ما دون الرجحان، ويرفض ما يحكم بامتناعه واستحالته بناءً على قوانينه الذاتية . فليس كل وارد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك هو على مرتبة واحدة، بل هو على مراتب أعلاها الحق بيقين، وأدناها الباطل بيقين، وبينهما الظن الراجح والشك والظن المرجوح . كيف نميز بين مراتب الإدراك؟ : ١ - حين يقدم الحس شهادته المؤكدة القاطعة بعد عمليات الإدراك الحسيّ المتواترة، وتتفق معها شهادات الحواس الأخرى، وشهادات حواس الآخرين، ولا تتعارض مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه، تغدو المعرفة
The ladder of perfection of this thing, knowing it degree by degree, within the laws of integration. 9 - And when the intellect perceives a perfect thing or one ascending in degrees of perfection, it can by itself proceed to conceive possibilities of its deficiency degree by degree down to its lowest. 10 - And when the intellect perceives beauty and senses its loci of beauty, it can by itself proceed to conceive the possibilities that distort and make it ugly so that it becomes not beautiful. 11 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of quantities, increasing and decreasing them to infinity; it increases and decreases quantities, increases and decreases spatial dimensions, increases and decreases temporal dimensions, increases and decreases measures of heat, increases and decreases measures of pressure, and so on for everything subject to measurement. 12 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of differences of qualities to infinity, changing and altering them without limit; if it holds a piece of pliable wax and begins to propose possibilities of changing its quality, its changes would not stop at a limit. Thus it becomes clear to us how the intellect performs its unlimited actions, within the primary materials that reach it through the external or internal senses, or that reach it through the reports and testimonies of others, in addition to what it has of inherent laws and what it has of imaginal capacity. But we should know that not everything perceived by thought is of the category of certainty; rather it divides into the types of perception previously explained: [truth with certainty - preponderant opinion - doubt - weak opinion (illusion) - falsehood with certainty]. And it is upon the intellect to classify its cognitions and place each of them in its rank and degree that it deserves, within its innate and acquired laws. Summary of methods of knowledge: If we wish to summarize the principles of methods of knowledge, it becomes clear to us that they go back to the following methods: The first method: sensory perception through the external or internal senses, and it includes experiential knowledge (mujarrabat), because its perception depends on observation of the senses, in addition to a mental generalization that comes through analogy (tamthil). The second method: perception of what is contained in the testimonies of others and their reports. The third method: perception of the inherent laws of the intellect, and perception of what the intellect deduces of cognitions from what has come to it through the previous two methods. And the intellect, with what it has of innate scales - which are further from error than the testimonies of the senses and the testimonies of other people who are not infallible - is considered a judge to be referred to in scrutinizing sensory perceptions and scrutinizing what is contained in the testimonies and reports of others; it affirms what it sees as true with certainty, accepts what is preponderant among them, discards what it doubts or lowers it below preponderance, and rejects what it judges as impossible and absurd based on its inherent laws. Not everything that enters the perceiving intellectual apparatus is on one level; rather it is on levels, the highest of which is truth with certainty and the lowest falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion. How do we distinguish between the levels of perception? 1 - When the sense presents its confirmed decisive testimony after repeated sensory processes, and the testimonies of other senses and testimonies of the senses of others agree with it, and it does not contradict a principle of the principles of the intellect and its laws, the knowledge becomes
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the intellect's operations and the sources and levels of knowledge. It begins by listing four abilities of the intellect: to conceive degrees of perfection, to conceive degrees of ugliness, to conceive infinite variations in quantities (such as heat, pressure, etc.), and to conceive infinite variations in qualities (exemplified by molding wax). The author then points out that the intellect works with primary materials from the senses, reports of others, and its own inherent laws and imagination. However, not every perception is certain; the intellect must classify its cognitions into five levels previously mentioned: truth with certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt, weak opinion (illusion), and falsehood with certainty. Then the author summarizes the three methods of knowledge: (1) sensory perception, including experiential knowledge obtained through repeated sensory observation with mental generalization via analogy (tamthil); (2) reports and testimonies of others; (3) the intellect's deduction from its own inherent laws and from materials obtained through the first two methods. The intellect, having innate scales that are more reliable than fallible senses and fallible human testimony, acts as a judge to test perceptions: it affirms certain truth, accepts probable opinion, discards doubt or weak opinion, and rejects what is impossible according to its laws. The passage ends with an incomplete discussion of how to distinguish levels of perception, starting with the condition of consistent sense testimony.

Key Terms
اليقين
الظن
الشك
الوهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which level is directly above 'doubt' in the hierarchy?
A) Truth with certainty
B) Preponderant opinion
C) Weak opinion (illusion)
D) Falsehood with certainty

Key Takeaway
Perception spans five levels; the intellect must classify each cognition accurately to avoid epistemic confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following Islamic knowledge statements into one of the five levels: 'The Quran is the word of Allah' (truth with certainty), 'The Prophet recommended wearing white on Fridays' (doubt or preponderant opinion depending on evidence).
?
Give an example from fiqh where a ruling is based on preponderant opinion (zann rajih) rather than certainty.

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
The Three Sources of Knowledge
Author summarizes the three methods of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three sources of knowledge and give an example from Islamic sciences for each.

Core Idea
All knowledge comes from three sources: sensory experience, testimony, and rational deduction.

The author summarizes the methods of knowledge into three categories. The first is sensory perception, which includes experiential knowledge (mujarrabat) that involves a mental generalization via analogy (tamthil). The second is testimony from others. The third is the intellect's own inherent laws and deductions from the previous two sources. This tripartite division is foundational for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sources
Source 1: Sensory perception (external/internal senses) including mujarrabat. Source 2: Testimonies and reports of others. Source 3: Intellect's innate laws and deductions from sources 1 and 2.
Mujarrabat (experiential knowledge) is a subcategory of sensory; it includes generalization via tamthil.
2
Analyze the role of analogy (tamthil)
In mujarrabat, the sense observes repeatedly, and the intellect uses analogy to generalize. For example, repeatedly seeing fire burn yields the general rule that fire burns. This is not pure sense but sense plus reason.
Tamthil here is analogical reasoning, not the formal qiyas of usul al-fiqh.
3
Distinguish the third source
The third source is not independent of the first two; it works on data from senses and testimony. But the intellect also has innate laws (like the principle of non-contradiction) that are not derived from experience.
This distinguishes rational knowledge from empirical.

Method of Learning

The Intellect as Judge of Perceptions
Author describes the intellect's role as judge with innate scales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how the intellect uses its innate scales to evaluate perceptions and assign them to the appropriate level of certainty.

Core Idea
The intellect is a judge that uses innate scales, more reliable than senses or others' testimony, to accept, doubt, or reject perceptions.

After listing the three sources, the author explains that the intellect possesses innate scales (mawazin fitriyya) that are less prone to error than the senses or the testimony of non-infallible people. The intellect acts as a judge (hakam) to scrutinize sensory and testimonial knowledge: it affirms certain truths, accepts preponderant opinions, discards doubts, and rejects impossibilities. This is how we distinguish between the levels of perception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of material the intellect judges
The intellect judges: (1) sensory perceptions, (2) testimonies of others, (3) its own deductions. For each, it applies its innate scales.
The innate scales include principles like non-contradiction and causality.
2
Understand the four outcomes of judgment
The intellect can: (a) affirm with certainty (haqq bi-yaqin), (b) accept as preponderant (zann rajih), (c) discard or downgrade due to doubt, or (d) reject as impossible (mustahil).
This corresponds to the five levels: certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt/weak opinion, falsehood.
3
Apply to a concrete Islamic case
Consider a hadith: if sensory experience (e.g., seeing the Prophet) is impossible due to time, and testimony is weak, the intellect uses its scale to judge it as doubtful or false. If testimony is mutawatir, intellect accepts with certainty.
The intellect's judgment does not contradict revelation; it works with the data.

الظن الراجح - الشك - الظن المرجوح (الوهم) - الباطل بيقين]. وعلى العقل أن يصنف معارفه، ويضع كلاً منها في مرتبته ودرجته التي يستحقها، ضمن قوانينه الفطرية والمكتسبة. خلاصة طرق المعرفة: وإذا أردنا أن نلخص أصول طرق المعرفة تبين لنا أنها ترجع إلى الطرق التالية : الطريق الأول: الإدراك الحسيّ بالحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة، ويدخل فيه المجربات، لأن إدراكها يعتمد على ملاحظة الحسّ، مضافاً إليها تعميم عقلي يأتي عن طريق التمثيل. الطريق الثاني: إدراك ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف . الطريق الثالث: إدراك قوانين العقل الذاتية، وإدراك ما يستنبطه العقل من معارف مما ورد إليه عن الطريقين السابقين. والعقل بما لديه من موازين فطرية - هي أبعد عن الخطأ من شهادات الحسّ، وشهادات الآخرين من الناس غير المعصومين - يعتبر حكماً يرجع إليه في تمحيص المدركات بالحسّ، وتمحيص ما تتضمنه شهادات الآخرين وأخبارهم من معارف، فيجزم بما يراه حقاً يقيناً، ويقبل ما يترجح لديه منها، ويطرح ما يشك به أو ينزله إلى ما دون الرجحان، ويرفض ما يحكم بامتناعه واستحالته بناءً على قوانينه الذاتية . فليس كل وارد إلى الجهاز الفكري المدرك هو على مرتبة واحدة، بل هو على مراتب أعلاها الحق بيقين، وأدناها الباطل بيقين، وبينهما الظن الراجح والشك والظن المرجوح . كيف نميز بين مراتب الإدراك؟ : ١ - حين يقدم الحس شهادته المؤكدة القاطعة بعد عمليات الإدراك الحسيّ المتواترة، وتتفق معها شهادات الحواس الأخرى، وشهادات حواس الآخرين، ولا تتعارض مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه، تغدو المعرفة
The ladder of perfection of this thing, knowing it degree by degree, within the laws of integration. 9 - And when the intellect perceives a perfect thing or one ascending in degrees of perfection, it can by itself proceed to conceive possibilities of its deficiency degree by degree down to its lowest. 10 - And when the intellect perceives beauty and senses its loci of beauty, it can by itself proceed to conceive the possibilities that distort and make it ugly so that it becomes not beautiful. 11 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of quantities, increasing and decreasing them to infinity; it increases and decreases quantities, increases and decreases spatial dimensions, increases and decreases temporal dimensions, increases and decreases measures of heat, increases and decreases measures of pressure, and so on for everything subject to measurement. 12 - And the intellect can by itself proceed in conceiving possibilities of differences of qualities to infinity, changing and altering them without limit; if it holds a piece of pliable wax and begins to propose possibilities of changing its quality, its changes would not stop at a limit. Thus it becomes clear to us how the intellect performs its unlimited actions, within the primary materials that reach it through the external or internal senses, or that reach it through the reports and testimonies of others, in addition to what it has of inherent laws and what it has of imaginal capacity. But we should know that not everything perceived by thought is of the category of certainty; rather it divides into the types of perception previously explained: [truth with certainty - preponderant opinion - doubt - weak opinion (illusion) - falsehood with certainty]. And it is upon the intellect to classify its cognitions and place each of them in its rank and degree that it deserves, within its innate and acquired laws. Summary of methods of knowledge: If we wish to summarize the principles of methods of knowledge, it becomes clear to us that they go back to the following methods: The first method: sensory perception through the external or internal senses, and it includes experiential knowledge (mujarrabat), because its perception depends on observation of the senses, in addition to a mental generalization that comes through analogy (tamthil). The second method: perception of what is contained in the testimonies of others and their reports. The third method: perception of the inherent laws of the intellect, and perception of what the intellect deduces of cognitions from what has come to it through the previous two methods. And the intellect, with what it has of innate scales - which are further from error than the testimonies of the senses and the testimonies of other people who are not infallible - is considered a judge to be referred to in scrutinizing sensory perceptions and scrutinizing what is contained in the testimonies and reports of others; it affirms what it sees as true with certainty, accepts what is preponderant among them, discards what it doubts or lowers it below preponderance, and rejects what it judges as impossible and absurd based on its inherent laws. Not everything that enters the perceiving intellectual apparatus is on one level; rather it is on levels, the highest of which is truth with certainty and the lowest falsehood with certainty, and between them are preponderant opinion, doubt, and weak opinion. How do we distinguish between the levels of perception? 1 - When the sense presents its confirmed decisive testimony after repeated sensory processes, and the testimonies of other senses and testimonies of the senses of others agree with it, and it does not contradict a principle of the principles of the intellect and its laws, the knowledge becomes
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the intellect's operations and the sources and levels of knowledge. It begins by listing four abilities of the intellect: to conceive degrees of perfection, to conceive degrees of ugliness, to conceive infinite variations in quantities (such as heat, pressure, etc.), and to conceive infinite variations in qualities (exemplified by molding wax). The author then points out that the intellect works with primary materials from the senses, reports of others, and its own inherent laws and imagination. However, not every perception is certain; the intellect must classify its cognitions into five levels previously mentioned: truth with certainty, preponderant opinion, doubt, weak opinion (illusion), and falsehood with certainty. Then the author summarizes the three methods of knowledge: (1) sensory perception, including experiential knowledge obtained through repeated sensory observation with mental generalization via analogy (tamthil); (2) reports and testimonies of others; (3) the intellect's deduction from its own inherent laws and from materials obtained through the first two methods. The intellect, having innate scales that are more reliable than fallible senses and fallible human testimony, acts as a judge to test perceptions: it affirms certain truth, accepts probable opinion, discards doubt or weak opinion, and rejects what is impossible according to its laws. The passage ends with an incomplete discussion of how to distinguish levels of perception, starting with the condition of consistent sense testimony.

Why We Need Structured Arguments: Direct and Indirect Inference
Author states the need to present mental works as arguments and previews the section.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct and indirect inference and explain why arguments must be formulated as proofs in Islamic scholarly discourse.

Core Idea
Proofs convince others; inference is either immediate or mediated by a middle term.

In this opening of the third section, the author tells us that presenting our intellectual works to others requires proper argumentation. Just as a jurist must present evidence for a ruling, any mental product must be formed as a proof (ḥujja) to gain acceptance. This lesson introduces the two fundamental methods of inference—direct and indirect—that underpin all Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of argumentation
The author states that mental works (a'mal al-'aql) must be formulated according to a method of proof so that others are convinced (yaqtani'u). In Islamic sciences, conviction requires evidence—whether in kalam, fiqh, or tafsir.
The term iqna' (conviction) is key: arguments aim to produce certainty or strong belief.
2
Distinguish direct and indirect inference
Direct inference (istidlal mubashir) derives a conclusion without an intermediate term; indirect inference (istidlal ghayr mubashir) uses a middle term in a syllogism. For example, converting 'Every haram is prohibited' to 'Some prohibited things are haram' is direct; using a middle term to prove a new ruling is indirect.
The distinction hinges on the presence or absence of a middle term linking two premises.
3
Locate the content of the third section
The passage explicitly states that this is the content of the third section, titled 'Methods of Inference' (Turuq al-Istidlal). This sets the stage for detailed study of immediate inference and syllogistic reasoning.
Understanding this preview helps you situate the upcoming lessons on conversion, syllogism, and argument forms.

Source Text

The author explains that the intellectual products ('a'mal al-'aql) must be presented as structured arguments (hujaj) to persuade others. This necessitates studying both direct (mubashir) and indirect (ghayr mubashir) inference methods, as well as how to construct convincing proofs. The author explicitly states that this is the content of the third section, titled 'Methods of Inference' (Turuq al-Istidlal). This sets the stage for what follows: the student is about to learn the formal mechanisms of argumentation.

Inference is either immediate (direct) or mediated by a middle term (indirect); both are essential for persuasive argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Division of Universals into Particulars
Author describes the process of dividing universals into particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of dividing a universal into its varieties and individuals, as demonstrated in the passage.

Core Idea
Division breaks the universal into manageable parts for thorough study.

This lesson focuses on the first operation described in the passage: dividing a universal into its particulars. The author gives numerous examples from human classification, such as division by color, geography, intelligence, health, and religion. Understanding this operation helps in organizing knowledge systematically and studying each part thoroughly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal being divided
Find the term that is being divided. In the passage, 'human' (insan) is a universal that is divided into varieties and individuals.
2
Determine the type of division
Decide whether the division is into species, varieties, or individuals. The passage shows division of genus into species, species into varieties, and species into individuals.
Example: dividing human into white, black, etc. is division into varieties.
3
Note the different criteria for division
The author lists various criteria: color, geography, intelligence, health, religion, morality. Each criterion yields a different classification.
Divisions can be almost unlimited based on different considerations.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
Think of how this operation is used in Fiqh: for example, dividing human acts into wajib, mandub, mubah, makruh, haram is a division of the universal 'act' into its varieties based on legal ruling.
This facilitates detailed study of each category.
