Intellectual vs. Material Experimental Composition
Author distinguishes intellectual composition from material experimental composition with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish intellectual composition from material experimental composition and identify their roles in Islamic scholarly practice.

Core Idea
Intellectual composition precedes and guides material experimental composition in all structured inquiry.

This passage from a modern methodology textbook clarifies two fundamental cognitive operations: the mental act of combining scattered elements into a coherent whole (intellectual composition), and the physical act of testing that mental construct in the laboratory (material experimental composition). Understanding this distinction is crucial for any researcher in Islamic sciences who must organize knowledge or invent new solutions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the passage and identify the definitions of 'intellectual composition' (al-tarkīb al-fikrī) and 'material experimental composition' (al-tarkīb al-māddī al-tajrībī). Note that the author gives separate sections for each.
2
Extract the examples
The author provides two examples of intellectual composition: a poet creating a simile by combining a boat and ambergris to describe a crescent, and an engineer inventing a land-sea-air vehicle by assembling parts from memory according to natural laws. List the key elements combined in each case.
3
Determine the logical priority
Notice that the passage states intellectual composition comes before material experimental composition. The researcher first builds a mental model, then tests it physically.
4
Recognize broader applications
The passage says intellectual composition is not only for invention but also for organizing knowledge into books, chapters, sections, etc. This applies directly to how Islamic scholars structure their works.
5
Understand the testing role
Material experimental composition can confirm, modify, or reject the intellectual composition. However, the intellectual composition remains a compositional act regardless of the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المجتمعة التي توصل الفكر إلى تركيبها والتنسيق بينها. يأتي الشاعر مثلاً بفكرة زورق، ثم يأتي بفكرة حمولة من عنبر، ثم يؤلف منهما جميعاً كلاً في صورة، ويشبه بها الهلال. فعالية الشاعر قبل أن يصل إلى التشبيه قامت على تركيب فكري، جمع فيه أجزاء متفرقة كانت حاضرة في ذاكرته، ونسق بينها، وأقام منها صورة جديدة. ويأتي المهندس الميكانيكي مثلاً، فيجمع في مخيلته أجزاء متفرقة مما وعت ذاكرته من عناصر الأشياء، ويؤلف بينها تأليفاً مترابطاً منطقياً، بحسب ما يعلم من قوانين الطبيعة وسننها، ثم ينتهي إلى ابتكار هيكل فكري مترابط، مادته الأجزاء التي جمعها، وصورته الهيئة الفكرية الهندسية التي توصل إليها ابتكاراً. وليكن ما توصل إليه في عملية التركيب الفكري مركبة «برية بحرية جوية» تغوص في الماء وتطير في الجو، وهي ذات شكل خاص، وصفات خاصة، وخصائص تختلف عما سبق أن ابتكره الإنسان في مجال المركبات. ثم يأتي التركيب المادي التجريبي المعملي فينفذ العمل التركيبي الذي قام به الفكر، وقد تُصَدِّق التجربة عمل الفكر، أو تُعدّل منه، أو تكذبه وترفضه، كل هذا لا يؤثر في الموضوع، لأن ما قام به الفكر على كل حال إنما هو عمل تركيبي ما، صدقه الواقع التجريبي أو كذبه. وعملية التركيب العقلي هذه هي وسيلة الباحث إلى الاختراع والابتكار، كما هي طريقة العالم في جمع وحدات المعرفة منسقة في كتب وأبواب وفصول وأقسام وأصناف وأنواع ومباحث ومسائل، ونحو ذلك من دوائر تجتمع فيها أجزاء ذات ترابط، فيتألف منها كل ذو هيئة منطقية. د ـ والتركيب المادي التجريبي : هو عملية مادية تقوم بالتأليف بين العناصر المادية الجاهزة في الطبيعة أو التي انتهت إليها أعمال التحليل المادي. وحين يقوم الباحث بإعادة تركيب مادة ما كان قد حَلَّلها سابقاً إلى عناصرها هو أو غيره، فإنه يمتحن بعمليته التركيبية دقة العملية التحليلية وصوابها، أو يكتشف عدم الدقة فيها، أو خطأها وعدم صوابها. أما حينما يقوم الباحث باختبار مُركبات جديدة فإنه يحاول اكتشافاً جديداً ذا ظواهر وآثار وشروط وخصائص لا تعرف على وجهها الصحيح إلا بعد التجربة والامتحان. وكم اكتشف الباحثون مُركبات جديدة نافعة لم تكن معروفة من قبل، وازدهرت بها الحضارة المادية الحديثة، وكانت الوسيلة إليها عمليات التركيب التي قام بها الفكر العلمي. وقد لا يمارس الباحث أعمال التركيب المادي قبل أن يقوم بأعمال التركيب الفكري، إذ يكون العمل الفكري في التركيب سابقاً للعمل المادي التجريبي.
The collected [parts] that the intellect brings to their composition and coordination. The poet, for example, brings an idea of a boat, then brings an idea of a cargo of ambergris, then composes from both a whole in a form, and compares it to the crescent. The poet's operation before reaching the simile was based on intellectual composition, in which he collected scattered parts that were present in his memory, coordinated them, and established from them a new form. The mechanical engineer, for example, gathers in his imagination scattered parts of what his memory has stored of the elements of things, and composes between them a logically coherent composition, according to what he knows of the laws of nature and its customs, then arrives at the invention of a coherent intellectual structure, its matter being the parts he gathered, and its form being the intellectual engineering configuration he arrived at by invention. Let what he arrived at in the process of intellectual composition be a 'land-sea-air' vehicle that dives into water and flies in the air, having a special shape, special qualities, and characteristics different from what humans had previously invented in the field of vehicles. Then comes material experimental laboratory composition, which implements the compositional work that the intellect performed. The experiment may verify the work of the intellect, or modify it, or falsify it and reject it. All this does not affect the subject, because what the intellect did in any case is merely some compositional work, whether empirical reality confirms it or falsifies it. And this process of intellectual composition is the researcher's means to invention and innovation, just as it is the scholar's method of gathering units of knowledge organized in books, sections, chapters, divisions, categories, types, topics, and questions, and similar circles in which interconnected parts come together, forming a whole with a logical structure. D – Material experimental composition: it is a material operation that composes between material elements ready in nature or that have resulted from material analysis operations. And when a researcher recomposes a material that he or someone else had previously analyzed into its elements, he tests by his compositional operation the accuracy and correctness of the analytical operation, or discovers its inaccuracy, or its error and incorrectness. As for when a researcher tests new compounds, he attempts a new discovery possessing phenomena, effects, conditions, and characteristics that are not known correctly except after experiment and testing. How many new useful compounds have researchers discovered that were not known before, and modern material civilization flourished with them, and the means to them were the compositional operations carried out by scientific thought. And the researcher may not practice material composition operations before performing intellectual composition operations, since intellectual work in composition precedes material experimental work.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion distinguishing two types of composition: intellectual composition (al-tarkīb al-fikrī) and material experimental composition (al-tarkīb al-māddī al-tajrībī). The author illustrates intellectual composition through a poet composing a simile (tashbīh) from two ideas—a boat and a cargo of ambergris—and an engineer inventing a land-sea-air vehicle by combining parts from memory according to natural laws. The key is that the intellect assembles scattered elements into a coherent new whole. This intellectual composition is the means of invention and of organizing knowledge into books, chapters, sections, etc., forming a logical structure. Then the material experimental composition is the physical implementation of the intellectual composition. It tests the intellectual work—either confirming, modifying, or rejecting it—but the intellectual work remains a legitimate compositional act regardless of empirical outcome. The passage emphasizes that material composition often follows intellectual composition, and it serves to verify analysis or discover new compounds. Throughout, the author stresses the primacy of intellectual composition as a creative and organizational tool.

Key Terms
التركيب الفكري
التركيب المادي التجريبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Intellectual composition is the constructive mental act that generates new ideas and organizes knowledge, while material experimental composition tests those ideas in practice.

Synthesis as a Test of Analysis in Islamic Inquiry
Author describes how synthesis tests analysis and enables discovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the intellectual operation of synthesis tests the validity of an earlier analysis, using examples from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Synthesis reveals whether your analysis was correct.

This passage describes a key methodological principle: when you reconstruct what you have broken down, you verify the accuracy of your breakdown. In Islamic sciences, this principle applies when a jurist recombines the components of a legal ruling to check his reasoning. Understanding this relationship helps you ensure that your analytical work is sound and that you are building on a correct foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two uses of synthesis: (1) recomposing a previously analyzed material to test the analysis, and (2) creating entirely new compounds for discovery. Note the different purposes.
2
Understand the testing function
Synthesis acts as a check: if you can reconstruct the whole from its parts without contradiction or missing elements, your analysis is likely correct. If the reconstruction fails, the analysis was flawed.
This parallels how a faqih recombines rulings to verify his breakdown of a legal principle.
3
Recognize the priority of intellectual synthesis
The passage states that intellectual synthesis must precede material synthesis. You must think through the composition before executing it physically. This ensures that experimental work is guided by reason.
In Islamic thought, this mirrors the primacy of rational analysis over blind practice.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Consider a jurist analyzing the ruling on gharar (uncertainty) into its conditions and exceptions. By reconstructing the ruling intellectually, he tests whether his analysis captures all necessary elements.
This application shows how the principle extends beyond physical sciences to Islamic legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحين يقوم الباحث بإعادة تركيب مادة ما كان قد حَلَّلها سابقاً إلى عناصرها هو أو غيره، فإنه يمتحن بعمليته التركيبية دقة العملية التحليلية وصوابها، أو يكتشف عدم الدقة فيها، أو خطأها وعدم صوابها. أما حينما يقوم الباحث باختبار مُركبات جديدة فإنه يحاول اكتشافاً جديداً ذا ظواهر وآثار وشروط وخصائص لا تعرف على وجهها الصحيح إلا بعد التجربة والامتحان. وكم اكتشف الباحثون مُركبات جديدة نافعة لم تكن معروفة من قبل، وازدهرت بها الحضارة المادية الحديثة، وكانت الوسيلة إليها عمليات التركيب التي قام بها الفكر العلمي. وقد لا يمارس الباحث أعمال التركيب المادي قبل أن يقوم بأعمال التركيب الفكري، إذ يكون العمل الفكري في التركيب سابقاً للعمل المادي التجريبي.
And when the researcher undertakes the recomposition of a material that he or someone else had previously analyzed into its elements, he tests, through his synthetic operation, the accuracy and correctness of the analytical operation, or discovers its inaccuracy, error, or incorrectness. As for when the researcher tests new compounds, he attempts a new discovery possessing phenomena, effects, conditions, and characteristics that are not known correctly except after experiment and testing. How many new beneficial compounds have researchers discovered that were not known before, and modern material civilization flourished through them, and the means to them were the synthetic operations carried out by scientific thought. The researcher may not practice material synthetic operations before carrying out intellectual synthetic operations, for intellectual work in synthesis precedes material experimental work.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between analysis and synthesis in the research process, emphasizing the roles of both in testing and discovery. The author begins by describing two scenarios: First, when a researcher recomposes a material that was previously analyzed by himself or others, the synthetic operation serves as a test of the accuracy and correctness of the earlier analysis. It may confirm the analysis or reveal its inaccuracies. Second, when the researcher works with entirely new compounds, synthesis becomes a tool for discovery, leading to new knowledge about phenomena, effects, and characteristics that can only be properly understood through experimentation. The author then remarks on the historical success of such synthetic operations in producing beneficial compounds that advanced modern material civilization. Finally, the passage highlights a methodological priority: intellectual synthesis must precede material synthesis. The researcher must first conceive and plan the compound conceptually before physically constructing it. This order ensures that the experimental work is guided by thought rather than being haphazard.

Key Terms
تحليل
تركيب
باحث
امتحان
تجربة

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Synthesis is not just a creative act but a critical tool for validating analysis; intellectual synthesis must precede any practical application.

Outline of Inference: Direct and Indirect Types
Author outlines the third section on inference, dividing into direct and indirect types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the main types of inference into direct (immediate) and indirect (mediated) categories according to the logical tradition.

Core Idea
Inference splits into direct (no middle term) and indirect (needs a middle term like syllogism).

This lesson presents the skeletal structure of inference as it appears in classical Islamic logic. The author divides inference into two fundamental kinds: those that require no middle term (direct) and those that do (indirect). Understanding this division is essential because it determines how arguments are evaluated and what rules apply. We will walk through the outline step by step, fixing each category in your mind so that later lessons on opposition, conversion, syllogism, and so on have a clear mental home.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
Look at the outline: inference (al-istidlal) is first divided into direct (mubashir) and indirect (ghayr mubashir). Direct inference yields a conclusion from a single proposition without a middle term; indirect inference requires two premises and a middle term.
Direct inference includes opposition (taqabul) and conversion ('aks). Indirect includes induction (istiqra'), syllogism (qiyas), and analogy (tamthil).
2
List the subtypes of direct inference
Under direct inference, there are two topics: opposition (al-taqabul) and conversion (al-'aks). Opposition studies the logical relationships between propositions (e.g., contradictory, contrary). Conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth.
Remember that direct inference is often called 'immediate inference' in English logic texts.
3
List the subtypes of indirect inference
Under indirect inference, there are three types: induction (from particulars to a universal), syllogism (the canonical deductive form), and analogy (from one case to another based on a shared attribute).
Syllogism (qiyas) here refers to the categorical syllogism, not to legal analogy (qiyas fiqhi), which is closer to tamthil.
4
Notice the final chapter: Ranks of Arguments
The outline ends with a third chapter on the ranks (maratib) of arguments, which evaluates arguments by their strength and certainty (e.g., necessary vs. probable). This will use the earlier categories to assign epistemic weight.
This chapter ties the logical forms back to the goal of certain knowledge (yaqin).
5
Memorize the structure using a diagram
Draw a tree diagram with 'Inference' at the top branching into 'Direct' (with branches Opposition and Conversion) and 'Indirect' (with branches Induction, Syllogism, and Analogy). Then add a third branch for 'Ranks of Arguments' (though it is a separate chapter).
Using visual diagrams is a traditional memorization aid (taswir). Keep this diagram as a mental map.

The Third Section: Inference. A - Introduction. B - Chapter One: Direct Inference. 1 - Opposition. 2 - Conversion. C - Chapter Two: Indirect Inference. 1 - Induction. 2 - Syllogism. 3 - Analogy. D - Chapter Three: Ranks of Arguments.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the outline of the third section of the book, which covers inference (al-istidlal). The author first introduces the section with an overview. He divides inference into two main categories: direct inference (al-istidlal al-mubashir) and indirect inference (al-istidlal ghayr al-mubashir). Direct inference includes two topics: opposition (al-taqabul) and conversion (al-'aks). Opposition deals with the relationships between propositions, such as contradiction and contrariety. Conversion is the immediate inference that swaps the subject and predicate while preserving truth. Indirect inference includes three types: induction (al-istiqra'), syllogism (al-qiyas), and analogy (al-tamthil). Induction is reasoning from particular cases to a general rule. Syllogism is the canonical deductive argument. Analogy is reasoning from one case to another based on a shared attribute. Finally, the author lists a third chapter on the ranks of arguments (maratib al-hujaj), which likely evaluates the strength and certainty of different argument types. This outline serves as a roadmap for the student to understand the structure of logic before delving into details.

Material and Psychological Factors Causing Error in Observation
Enumerates material and psychological factors causing errors in observation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between material and psychological factors causing error in observation and identify which specific factor is at play in a given example.

Core Idea
Error in observation arises from both external conditions and internal bias—knowing them safeguards your inquiry.

In this passage, the author systematically lists the pitfalls that can corrupt scientific observation and experiment. By categorizing them into material and psychological factors, he provides a diagnostic tool for anyone seeking reliable knowledge. Understanding these factors is essential for evaluating the soundness of experimental evidence in fields like medicine, fiqh, and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two main categories
The passage divides factors into material (al-‘awāmil al-mādiyyah) and psychological (al-‘awāmil al-nafsiyyah). Material factors relate to external conditions or the observer's physical/mental state; the psychological factor is specifically about personal bias. Identify which factor is material and which is psychological.
Note that factor F (health) is listed under material, not psychological.
2
Identify each material factor's specific type
Examine each material factor (B to G): B is sensory error, C is misinterpretation, D is incomplete experiment, E is faulty inference, F is poor health, G is defective instruments. Recognize what each emphasizes.
These are not mutually exclusive but each highlights a distinct source.
3
Analyze the psychological factor (H)
Factor H is psychological: the observer is not objective and seeks only evidence that confirms a preconceived idea. Note the vivid description of 'night blindness' (al-‘ashā) that veils the senses from contrary evidence.
This factor is especially important in debates and ijtihad.
4
Apply the anecdote as a case study
The author's anecdote about blood pressure readings illustrates factor G (defective instruments). Use it to practice: identify which factor caused the error and explain why.
The passage states that two devices were defective (fāsidah), so it's clearly instrument failure.

ب ـ ومن العوامل المادية تعرض الملاحظ لخطأ في الحس، ويدخل في هذا أخطاء الحس وأوهامه المعروفة .
جـ ـ ومن العوامل المادية فهمه لما شهده فهماً خاطئاً على غير وجهه الصحيح .
د ـ ومن العوامل المادية أن لا تكون تجربته مستوفية عناصرها اللازمة .
هـ ـ ومن العوامل المادية أن يخطىء في الاستنتاج، فيرى التجربة أو يشاهد الظاهرة مشاهدة صحيحة ولكن يستدل استدلالاً غير مستند إلى أساس صحيح .
و ـ ومن العوامل المادية كون المجرب في حالة صحية جسدية أو نفسية أو فكرية غير سليمة، الأمر الذي قد ينتج عنه نتائج غير سليمة .
ز ـ ومن العوامل المادية أن تكون الآلات أو المواد التي يستخدمها في التجربة فاسدة غير صالحة لتقديم نتائج صحيحة .
وأذكر بهذه المناسبة أنني قست ضغطي عند عدد من الأطباء في يوم واحد، فكانت النتيجة مختلفة اختلافاً كبيراً، إذ أعطاني بعضهم ما يقتضي تنزيل الضغط، وأعطاني بعضهم ما يقتضي رفعه، وكان ذلك بسبب فساد الأجهزة، إذ تبين لي فيما بعد أن جهازي اثنين منهم فاسدان .
ح ـ ومن العوامل النفسية أن لا يكون المجرّب الملاحظ متجرداً عن الغرض الخاص، فهو حينئذ لا يبحث بحثاً موضوعياً، وإنما يبحث ليثبت وجهة نظر معينة مرتكزة في نفسه قبل البحث، لذلك فهو لا يفكر إلا بتصيّد ما يدعم فكرته السابقة، وكل ما يرد عليه مما يخالفها يتجاوز عنه فلا يسجله، أو لا يشاهده أصلاً بسبب العشى الذي يُجلّل حسّه من نفسه .
B) Among the material factors: the observer is subject to error in sense perception, and this includes errors of the senses and their known illusions. C) Among the material factors: his understanding of what he witnessed is mistaken, not in its correct form. D) Among the material factors: that his experiment does not fulfill its necessary elements. E) Among the material factors: that he errs in inference, so he sees the experiment or observes the phenomenon correctly but draws a conclusion not based on a sound foundation. F) Among the material factors: the experimenter being in an unsound physical, psychological, or intellectual health condition, which may produce unsound results. G) Among the material factors: that the instruments or materials he uses in the experiment are defective, not suitable for providing correct results. And I mention on this occasion that I had my blood pressure measured by a number of doctors on one day, and the result differed greatly, as some gave me what required lowering the pressure, and some gave me what required raising it, and that was due to defective devices, as it later became clear to me that the devices of two of them were defective. H) Among the psychological factors: that the experimenting observer is not free from personal bias; then he does not search objectively, but rather searches to prove a certain viewpoint entrenched in himself before the research. Therefore, he only thinks of seeking out what supports his previous idea, and whatever comes to him contrary to it, he passes over it and does not record it, or does not observe it at all due to the night blindness that veils his sense from his own self.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage enumerates eight factors—seven material and one psychological—that cause errors in scientific observation and experimentation. The author begins with factor B: the observer's sense perception can be faulty, including known sensory illusions. Factor C points to misinterpretation of what was witnessed. Factor D emphasizes that an experiment must include all necessary elements; an incomplete experiment leads to error. Factor E addresses correct observation but faulty inference—a logical error in reasoning from the data. Factor F notes that the experimenter's physical, mental, or psychological state can distort results. Factor G concerns defective instruments or materials, illustrated by an anecdote where the author's blood pressure readings varied widely due to faulty devices. Finally, factor H is psychological: the observer lacks objectivity and seeks only evidence that confirms his preconceived notion, ignoring contrary evidence or even failing to perceive it due to 'blindness' caused by his bias. The list underscores that empirical knowledge is subject to multiple sources of error, both in the external conditions and in the observer's internal state.

العوامل النفسية
الملاحظة

A) Factor D: incomplete experiment
B) Factor G: defective instruments
C) Factor B: sensory errors

Key Takeaway
Observation errors arise from both material conditions (senses, inference, instruments, health) and psychological bias; a rigorous observer must guard against all these pitfalls.

Condition One: Precision (al-Diqah)
First condition of valid scientific observation: Precision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of precision in scientific observation and apply them to an Islamic science scenario.

Core Idea
Precision transforms raw observation into reliable knowledge by specifying object, place, time, and instruments.

The passage begins with precision as the first condition for valid observation. Precision is not mere carefulness; it involves a checklist: the observed thing must be exactly determined, its location and time noted, and all tools verified. This condition distinguishes scientific observation from casual looking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of precision
Look for the four elements mentioned: the thing observed, its place, its time, and the instruments. Write them down.
2
Explain the purpose of each component
Why is it important to specify the object? To avoid ambiguity. Why place and time? Context affects observation. Why instruments? To ensure reliability.
3
Apply precision to an Islamic science example
Consider the observation of the crescent moon for Ramadan. What would precision require? Determine the moon's position, the exact time of sunset, and verify the telescope or naked eye conditions.
This demonstrates how precision is used in ʿilm al-falāk (astronomy) for religious purposes.

شروط التجربة والملاحظة
وليضمن المجرب الملاحظ الابتعاد ما أمكن عن مواقع الخطأ فعليه أن يلتزم بالشروط التالية :
أولاً: الدقة، فهي أول الصفات التي يجب أن تتصف بها الملاحظة العلمية، حتى تتميز بذلك عن الملاحظة غير العلمية.
وتكون الدقة بتحديد الشيء الملاحظ وحصره، وتحديد مكان الظاهرة وزمانها، والتأكد التام من سلامة الأجهزة والأدوات والمواد المستعملة في التجربة والملاحظة.
ثانياً: تحديد الظاهرة التي هي موضع البحث، وعزلها عزلاً تاماً عن كل ما سواها من الظواهر المشابهة، كلما أمكن ذلك، لأن اختلاط الظاهرة بغيرها من شأنه أن يسيء إلى إدراك الظاهرة إدراكاً صحيحاً تاماً.
فحين نريد مثلاً دراسة حركة جسم من الأجسام، فالمفروض أن نعزل هذه الحركة عن المقاومة التي يلاقيها الجسم المتحرك من السطح الذي يتحرك عليه، والجو الذي يتحرك فيه، ونجري حركته في فراغ مطلق، ولكن قد يبدو تحقيق هذا الشرط عسيراً جداً لدى دراسة ظواهر الأشياء، لأن الظواهر لا تخلو من مختلطات بها، وقد لا يكون التمييز متيسراً، وعلى كل حال فعزل الظاهرة وحصر الانتباه في ناحية من نواحيها، واجب الباحث ما أمكنه الأمر.
ثالثاً: التكرار، فلا يكفي دراسة الظاهرة في حالة من حالاتها، بل لا بد من دراستها في أحوالها المختلفة، والتأكد - عن طريق إجراء التجربة والملاحظة مرات مختلفات - من صحة النتائج التي سجلت بالملاحظة.
فقد تختلف النتائج باختلاف الأحوال، وقد يصحح بالتكرار ما دخل من خطأ في التجارب الأولى.
رابعاً: تبسيط الظاهرة، فيجب تبسيط الظاهرة، وذلك بتحديد جزء بسيط غير مركب من صفات الشيء الموضوع للبحث، وبهذا التبسيط تتضح دلالة الظاهرة للمجرب، وتتضح صلتها بغيرها من الأفكار.
خامساً: تسجيل الظاهرة الملاحظة في الحال، ويجب هذا التسجيل
Conditions of Experiment and Observation
And for the experimenting observer to ensure avoidance of places of error as much as possible, he must adhere to the following conditions:
First: Precision, for it is the first quality that scientific observation must possess, so that it is distinguished thereby from non-scientific observation.
And precision is by determining the observed thing and confining it, determining the place of the phenomenon and its time, and fully ascertaining the soundness of the instruments, tools, and materials used in the experiment and observation.
Second: Determining the phenomenon that is the subject of research, and isolating it completely from all other similar phenomena, as much as possible, because the mixing of the phenomenon with others will harm the correct and complete perception of the phenomenon.
For example, when we want to study the motion of a body, we should isolate this motion from the resistance that the moving body encounters from the surface it moves on and the air in which it moves, and conduct its motion in a perfect vacuum. But achieving this condition may seem very difficult when studying phenomena of things, because phenomena are not free from admixtures, and discrimination may not be easy. In any case, isolating the phenomenon and focusing attention on one of its aspects is the duty of the researcher as much as possible.
Third: Repetition; it is not sufficient to study the phenomenon in one of its states, but rather it must be studied in its different states, and to ascertain—by conducting the experiment and observation multiple times—the correctness of the results recorded by observation.
For results may differ according to circumstances, and repetition may correct the error that entered in the first experiments.
Fourth: Simplifying the phenomenon; the phenomenon must be simplified by determining a simple, non-composite part of the properties of the thing under investigation. By this simplification, the significance of the phenomenon becomes clear to the experimenter, and its connection to other ideas becomes clear.
Fifth: Recording the observed phenomenon immediately; this recording must
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents five essential conditions for scientific observation and experimentation, aiming to minimize errors. The conditions are listed under the heading 'شروط التجربة والملاحظة'. The author states that to avoid errors as much as possible, the observer must adhere to these conditions. Condition one is precision (الدقة): the observed object must be precisely determined and confined, its place and time specified, and the instruments and materials must be verified as sound. Condition two is isolation (العزل): the phenomenon under study must be separated from all similar phenomena to avoid confusion. The author gives an example from physics: to study motion, one should ideally isolate it from resistance by using a vacuum, but notes this is often difficult; nevertheless, the researcher must strive to isolate to the extent possible. Condition three is repetition (التكرار): studying the phenomenon in different states and repeating the experiment multiple times to ensure results are correct, as initial errors may be corrected through repetition. Condition four is simplification (التبسيط): breaking the phenomenon down into a simple, non-composite part to clarify its significance and connections. Condition five, which cuts off, is immediate recording (التسجيل في الحال) of the observation. These conditions reflect a sophisticated understanding of empirical methodology, familiar to modern scientific practice but rooted in the Islamic tradition's concern for sound knowledge acquisition.

Key Terms
الدقة (al-diqah): Precision: the first quality of scientific observation, involving precise determination of the object, place, time, and verification of instruments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does precision in scientific observation include?
A) Only determining the object
B) Determining object, place, time, and verifying instruments
C) Repeating the experiment
D) Simplifying the phenomenon

Key Takeaway
Precision in observation requires specifying the object, place, time, and instrument soundness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Choose a phenomenon in Islamic law that depends on observation (e.g., sighting the new moon for Ramadan, determining the time of Fajr, identifying the direction of Qibla). List the four components of precision from the passage and explain how each would be applied in that context.

Condition Two: Isolation (al-ʿAzl)
Second condition: Isolation of the phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why isolation is necessary in observation and apply it to an Islamic science example.

Core Idea
Isolation of a phenomenon prevents confusion with similar phenomena, ensuring that what is observed belongs solely to the subject of study.

The second condition addresses the need to isolate the phenomenon under study from all similar phenomena. The passage gives the example of studying motion by isolating it from resistance using a vacuum. In the Islamic tradition, this principle is crucial for identifying causes and distinguishing between similar entities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reason for isolation
Read the passage and find why isolation is necessary: mixing with others harms correct perception.
2
Analyze the motion example
The author describes ideal isolation (vacuum) but notes it is often difficult. Understand that isolation is a striving goal.
3
Apply isolation to Islamic jurisprudence
In identifying the ʿillah (effective cause) of a ruling, one must isolate the specific attribute from other characteristics. For example, the prohibition of khamr due to intoxication must be isolated from color or taste.

Conditions of Experiment and Observation
And for the experimenting observer to ensure avoidance of places of error as much as possible, he must adhere to the following conditions:
First: Precision, for it is the first quality that scientific observation must possess, so that it is distinguished thereby from non-scientific observation.
And precision is by determining the observed thing and confining it, determining the place of the phenomenon and its time, and fully ascertaining the soundness of the instruments, tools, and materials used in the experiment and observation.
Second: Determining the phenomenon that is the subject of research, and isolating it completely from all other similar phenomena, as much as possible, because the mixing of the phenomenon with others will harm the correct and complete perception of the phenomenon.
For example, when we want to study the motion of a body, we should isolate this motion from the resistance that the moving body encounters from the surface it moves on and the air in which it moves, and conduct its motion in a perfect vacuum. But achieving this condition may seem very difficult when studying phenomena of things, because phenomena are not free from admixtures, and discrimination may not be easy. In any case, isolating the phenomenon and focusing attention on one of its aspects is the duty of the researcher as much as possible.
Third: Repetition; it is not sufficient to study the phenomenon in one of its states, but rather it must be studied in its different states, and to ascertain—by conducting the experiment and observation multiple times—the correctness of the results recorded by observation.
For results may differ according to circumstances, and repetition may correct the error that entered in the first experiments.
Fourth: Simplifying the phenomenon; the phenomenon must be simplified by determining a simple, non-composite part of the properties of the thing under investigation. By this simplification, the significance of the phenomenon becomes clear to the experimenter, and its connection to other ideas becomes clear.
Fifth: Recording the observed phenomenon immediately; this recording must
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage presents five essential conditions for scientific observation and experimentation, aiming to minimize errors. The conditions are listed under the heading 'شروط التجربة والملاحظة'. The author states that to avoid errors as much as possible, the observer must adhere to these conditions. Condition one is precision (الدقة): the observed object must be precisely determined and confined, its place and time specified, and the instruments and materials must be verified as sound. Condition two is isolation (العزل): the phenomenon under study must be separated from all similar phenomena to avoid confusion. The author gives an example from physics: to study motion, one should ideally isolate it from resistance by using a vacuum, but notes this is often difficult; nevertheless, the researcher must strive to isolate to the extent possible. Condition three is repetition (التكرار): studying the phenomenon in different states and repeating the experiment multiple times to ensure results are correct, as initial errors may be corrected through repetition. Condition four is simplification (التبسيط): breaking the phenomenon down into a simple, non-composite part to clarify its significance and connections. Condition five, which cuts off, is immediate recording (التسجيل في الحال) of the observation. These conditions reflect a sophisticated understanding of empirical methodology, familiar to modern scientific practice but rooted in the Islamic tradition's concern for sound knowledge acquisition.

Key Terms
العزل (al-‘azl): Isolation: separating the phenomenon under study from all similar phenomena to avoid confusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must the phenomenon be isolated from similar phenomena?
A) To make it more complex
B) Because mixing harms correct perception
C) To avoid repetition
D) To ensure instruments are sound

Key Takeaway
Isolation prevents confusion with similar phenomena and is essential for accurate observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In the example of studying motion, the author suggests isolating motion from resistance by using a vacuum. Similarly, in Islamic law, when determining the cause (ʿillah) of a ruling, isolation is needed. Suppose a ruling forbids a transaction due to ribā. How would you isolate the element that constitutes ribā from other aspects of the transaction? Write a brief explanation.

Condition Three: Repetition (al-Takrār)
Third condition: Repetition of observation and experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of repetition in observation and apply it to an Islamic science context.

Core Idea
Repetition across different states validates observations and corrects initial errors.

The third condition emphasizes that studying a phenomenon only once is insufficient. The observer must study it in different states and repeat the experiment multiple times. This allows for correction of errors and ensures results are reliable, a principle well-known in both empirical science and Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the insufficiency of one study
The passage states that studying a phenomenon in one state is not enough; different states may yield different results.
2
Recognize the role of repetition in error correction
Repetition can correct errors from the first experiments by providing more data.
3
Apply repetition to Islamic sciences
In hadith studies, the repetition of a narration through multiple chains (tawātur) confirms its authenticity. In moon sighting, multiple witnesses serve the same purpose.

وليضمن المجرب الملاحظ الابتعاد ما أمكن عن مواقع الخطأ فعليه أن يلتزم بالشروط التالية :
أولاً: الدقة، فهي أول الصفات التي يجب أن تتصف بها الملاحظة العلمية، حتى تتميز بذلك عن الملاحظة غير العلمية.
وتكون الدقة بتحديد الشيء الملاحظ وحصره، وتحديد مكان الظاهرة وزمانها، والتأكد التام من سلامة الأجهزة والأدوات والمواد المستعملة في التجربة والملاحظة.
ثانياً: تحديد الظاهرة التي هي موضع البحث، وعزلها عزلاً تاماً عن كل ما سواها من الظواهر المشابهة، كلما أمكن ذلك، لأن اختلاط الظاهرة بغيرها من شأنه أن يسيء إلى إدراك الظاهرة إدراكاً صحيحاً تاماً.
فحين نريد مثلاً دراسة حركة جسم من الأجسام، فالمفروض أن نعزل هذه الحركة عن المقاومة التي يلاقيها الجسم المتحرك من السطح الذي يتحرك عليه، والجو الذي يتحرك فيه، ونجري حركته في فراغ مطلق، ولكن قد يبدو تحقيق هذا الشرط عسيراً جداً لدى دراسة ظواهر الأشياء، لأن الظواهر لا تخلو من مختلطات بها، وقد لا يكون التمييز متيسراً، وعلى كل حال فعزل الظاهرة وحصر الانتباه في ناحية من نواحيها، واجب الباحث ما أمكنه الأمر.
ثالثاً: التكرار، فلا يكفي دراسة الظاهرة في حالة من حالاتها، بل لا بد من دراستها في أحوالها المختلفة، والتأكد - عن طريق إجراء التجربة والملاحظة مرات مختلفات - من صحة النتائج التي سجلت بالملاحظة.
فقد تختلف النتائج باختلاف الأحوال، وقد يصحح بالتكرار ما دخل من خطأ في التجارب الأولى.
رابعاً: تبسيط الظاهرة، فيجب تبسيط الظاهرة، وذلك بتحديد جزء بسيط غير مركب من صفات الشيء الموضوع للبحث، وبهذا التبسيط تتضح دلالة الظاهرة للمجرب، وتتضح صلتها بغيرها من الأفكار.
خامساً: تسجيل الظاهرة الملاحظة في الحال، ويجب هذا التسجيل
Conditions of Experiment and Observation
And for the experimenting observer to ensure avoidance of places of error as much as possible, he must adhere to the following conditions:
First: Precision, for it is the first quality that scientific observation must possess, so that it is distinguished thereby from non-scientific observation.
And precision is by determining the observed thing and confining it, determining the place of the phenomenon and its time, and fully ascertaining the soundness of the instruments, tools, and materials used in the experiment and observation.
Second: Determining the phenomenon that is the subject of research, and isolating it completely from all other similar phenomena, as much as possible, because the mixing of the phenomenon with others will harm the correct and complete perception of the phenomenon.
For example, when we want to study the motion of a body, we should isolate this motion from the resistance that the moving body encounters from the surface it moves on and the air in which it moves, and conduct its motion in a perfect vacuum. But achieving this condition may seem very difficult when studying phenomena of things, because phenomena are not free from admixtures, and discrimination may not be easy. In any case, isolating the phenomenon and focusing attention on one of its aspects is the duty of the researcher as much as possible.
Third: Repetition; it is not sufficient to study the phenomenon in one of its states, but rather it must be studied in its different states, and to ascertain—by conducting the experiment and observation multiple times—the correctness of the results recorded by observation.
For results may differ according to circumstances, and repetition may correct the error that entered in the first experiments.
Fourth: Simplifying the phenomenon; the phenomenon must be simplified by determining a simple, non-composite part of the properties of the thing under investigation. By this simplification, the significance of the phenomenon becomes clear to the experimenter, and its connection to other ideas becomes clear.
Fifth: Recording the observed phenomenon immediately; this recording must
Source pages p. 207

Condition four is simplification (التبسيط): breaking the phenomenon down into a simple, non-composite part to clarify its significance and connections. Condition five, which cuts off, is immediate recording (التسجيل في الحال) of the observation. These conditions reflect a sophisticated understanding of empirical methodology, familiar to modern scientific practice but rooted in the Islamic tradition's concern for sound knowledge acquisition.

Condition Four: Simplification (al-Tabsīṭ)
Fourth condition: Simplification of the phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how simplification aids understanding and apply it to an Islamic theological concept.

Core Idea
Simplifying a phenomenon by focusing on a non-composite part clarifies its significance and connections.

The fourth condition calls for simplifying the phenomenon by isolating a simple, non-composite part of its properties. This makes the phenomenon's meaning clearer and reveals its relationship to other ideas. In Islamic thought, simplification is used in kalam to analyze divine attributes and in fiqh to break down complex rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand what simplification means
The passage says to determine a simple, non-composite part of the properties. This means breaking the phenomenon down into its fundamental components.
2
Identify the purpose of simplification
Simplification clarifies the significance of the phenomenon and its connection to other ideas.
3
Apply simplification to an Islamic concept
In kalam, the attribute of 'knowledge' (ʿilm) is simplified by focusing on its definition as that which necessitates grasping the known. This clarifies its relation to other attributes like power.

Condition Five: Immediate Recording (al-Tasjīl)
Fifth condition: Immediate recording of the observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the importance of immediate recording and apply it to an Islamic legal context.

Core Idea
Immediate recording preserves the observation accurately and prevents loss or distortion of data.

The fifth condition demands that the observer record the phenomenon immediately. Although the passage is cut off, the principle is clear: delay in recording can lead to forgotten details or distortion. In Islamic practice, immediate recording is crucial for witnessing contracts, medical observations, and transmitting knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of immediacy
The passage states that recording must be done in the moment (fi al-ḥāl).
2
Reason why immediacy matters
Immediate recording preserves accuracy; delay can introduce memory errors or bias.
3
Apply immediate recording to Islamic sciences
In fiqh, witnesses to a contract must record or testify immediately. In hadith transmission, the Companion would narrate soon after the event to maintain accuracy.

Scientific Hypotheses in Inductive Research
Author defines scientific hypothesis and explains its role and testing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition, conditions, and testing process of a scientific hypothesis within the inductive methodology.

Core Idea
A scientific hypothesis is a grounded, testable explanation that drives the inductive search for truth.

In this passage, the author outlines the second stage of inductive research: forming scientific hypotheses. A hypothesis is not a wild guess but a reasoned possibility rooted in prior knowledge. It must be tested through evidence, leading to acceptance, rejection, or refinement. The example of Archimedes' discovery shows how a hypothesis emerges from observation and becomes a law.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of a scientific hypothesis
From the passage, identify the key elements: a hypothesis is an opinion or idea proposed as a hypothetical possibility to explain a phenomenon, show its cause, or explore its consequences.
2
Identify the conditions for a valid hypothesis
The passage states that the researcher must base the hypothesis on prior knowledge and experience in the relevant or related fields. This distinguishes a hypothesis from a random guess.
The condition ensures rigor in scientific inquiry.
3
Describe the testing process
After proposing the hypothesis, the researcher gathers evidence to support or refute it. If evidence is insufficient, the hypothesis is rejected and a new one is formed, continuing until sufficient conviction is reached.
This iterative process is central to induction.
4
Analyze the Archimedes example
Archimedes had a hypothesis that water displacement could measure volume. He tested it by observing his bath and then applied it to the crown problem. The hypothesis was verified and later became the scientific law of buoyancy.
The example shows hypothesis leading to a scientific law.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرحلة الثانية من مراحل الاستقراء : وهي مرحلة الفروض العلمية . والفرض العلمي : رأي أو فكرة يطرحها الباحث على سبيل الاحتمال الافتراضي ، فيفسر بها ظاهرة من الظواهر ، ويبين بها سبب حدوث الظاهرة أو علتها ، أو يبين بها ما يترتب عليها باعتبار الظاهرة سبباً لشيء آخر، أو علة لشيء آخر . ولا بد أن يستند الباحث في طرحه لهذه الفكرة الاحتمالية الافتراضية ، إلى حصيلة ما لديه من معارف وخبرات سابقة في مجال التجربة التي يجريها ، أو في مجالات أخرى لها صلة ما بمجال التجربة التي يجريها . وبعد طرح الفكرة الافتراضية ، يبدأ بالتدليل عليها لقبولها ، أو رفضها والانتقال إلى فكرة افتراضية أخرى ، وهكذا حتى تقوم لديه القناعة الكافية بصحة الفكرة التي طرحها ، أو برجحانها على الفروض الأخرى ، وذلك بالاستناد إلى الأدلة التي قامت لديه أو ظهرت له . ومن أمثلة الفروض التي صارت فيما بعد قانوناً علمياً قانون (أرخميدس) : «كل جسم يملأ حيزاً من الفراغ بمقدار حجمه لا وزنه» . وقصة استخراج هذا القانون تتلخص بأن حاكم (سراقوسه) في القرن الثالث قبل الميلاد ، أعطى صائغه قطعة من الذهب الخالص ليصوغها له تاجاً ، فلما جاء الصائغ بالتاج وزنه الحاكم فوجده مساوياً لوزن قطعة الذهب التي كان أعطاه إياها ، ولكن الحاكم شك في هذا التاج ، هل هو من الذهب الخالص فعلاً أو هو مغشوش بالفضة ، وقد سرق الصائغ مقدارها من قطعة الذهب ، فأرسل إلى (أرخميدس) وأعطاه التاج وكلفه أن يبحث الأمر . واحتار أرخميدس ولم يجد حيلة يعرف بها الحقيقة مع بقاء التاج على وضعه ، حتى اتفق له أن ذهب إلى الحمام ليغتسل ، فلما نزل في الماء وشاهد الماء يرتفع بمقدار حجم جسمه ، لفتت انتباهه هذه الظاهرة البدهية ،
The second stage of the stages of induction: It is the stage of scientific hypotheses. The scientific hypothesis: an opinion or idea that the researcher proposes as hypothetical possibility, by which he explains a phenomenon, and shows the cause of its occurrence or its reason, or shows what follows from it considering the phenomenon as a cause for something else, or a reason for something else. The researcher must, in proposing this hypothetical idea, rely on the outcome of his previous knowledge and experiences in the field of the experiment he is conducting, or in other fields related to the field of the experiment he is conducting. After proposing the hypothetical idea, he begins to provide evidence for it for acceptance, or rejection and moving to another hypothetical idea, and so on until he has sufficient conviction in the correctness of the idea he proposed, or its preponderance over other hypotheses, based on the evidence that he has or that appears to him. Among the examples of hypotheses that later became a scientific law is Archimedes' law: 'Every body occupies a space of void equal to its volume, not its weight.' The story of this law's discovery is summarized as follows: The ruler of Syracuse in the third century BC gave his jeweler a piece of pure gold to fashion a crown for him. When the jeweler brought the crown, the ruler weighed it and found it equal to the weight of the gold piece he had given him. But the ruler suspected the crown—whether it was really pure gold or alloyed with silver, and the jeweler had stolen an amount from the gold piece. So he sent for Archimedes, gave him the crown, and tasked him to investigate the matter. Archimedes was perplexed and found no trick to know the truth while keeping the crown intact, until it happened that he went to the bath to bathe. When he descended into the water and saw the water rise by the volume of his body, this obvious phenomenon caught his attention.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage introduces the second stage of inductive research: forming scientific hypotheses. The author defines a hypothesis as a tentative idea proposed to explain a phenomenon, identify its cause, or explore its consequences. The researcher must ground the hypothesis in prior knowledge and experience. After proposing it, the hypothesis is tested through evidence, leading to its acceptance, rejection, or modification until sufficient confidence is achieved. The passage then provides a classical example: Archimedes' principle. The story illustrates how a banal observation (water displacement) led to a breakthrough law. The author uses this to show how hypotheses emerge from careful observation and lead to scientific laws.

Key Terms
الفرض العلمي
الاستقراء

A) To prove a theory conclusively
B) To explain a phenomenon or show its cause
C) To dismiss previous knowledge
D) To create a scientific law immediately

Key Takeaway
A scientific hypothesis is a tentative, evidence-based explanation that drives the inductive process from observation to law.

Identify the hypothesis in the following Kalam argument: 'The universe displays consistent natural laws. These laws could be due to inherent properties of matter or due to a legislator. The inherent properties hypothesis fails to explain why laws are uniform. Therefore, the legislator hypothesis is stronger.' Explicitly state the hypothesis and how it is tested.

Method of Learning
intermediate
10 min
The Indirect Syllogistic Method for Hypothesis Testing
Author defines the third stage of induction and distinguishes indirect and direct methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a conditional syllogism to test a hypothesis in an Islamic science context.

Core Idea
The indirect method uses deduction to verify hypotheses where experimentation is impossible.

The third stage of induction involves verifying hypotheses. The indirect method uses logical syllogism to deduce consequences from a hypothesis. This method is crucial in Islamic sciences like kalam, where empirical testing is often impossible. We will learn how to apply conditional syllogisms to test theological hypotheses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothesis
Locate the tentative causal explanation (al-fard) that requires verification. In kalam, this could be 'the world is temporally originated'.
2
Formulate the conditional syllogism
Express the hypothesis as an 'if-then' statement: If H is true, then consequence C follows. For example: If the world is temporally originated, then it must have a cause.
3
Check the consequence's verifiability
Determine whether C can be verified by observation or rational proof. In kalam, the consequence 'it has a cause' is accepted by reason.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
If C is verified, the hypothesis is supported (modus ponens). If C is false, the hypothesis is refuted (modus tollens). This yields probabilistic or certain confirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرحلة الثالثة من مراحل الاستقراء وهي مرحلة تحقيق الفروض أو ترجيحها واعتبارها نظرية مقبولة علمياً . وللباحث منهجان لتحقيق فروضه العلمية أو ترجيحها أو رفضها : ١ - منهج غير مباشر . ٢ - منهج مباشر، وهو الأقوم ما أمكن تحقيقه . أ - أما المنهج غير المباشر : فهو المنهج الذي لا تستخدم فيه التجربة لإثبات الفرض أو ترجيحه، ولكن تُستخدم فيه الطريقة القياسية، وهي طريقة القياس المنطقي القائم على اللزوم الذي تصاغ منه الأقيسة الشرطية، أو القائم على التضمن أو المطابقة اللذين تصاغ منهما الأقيسة الحملية، ولا يشترط التصريح بصيغ القياس الشرطي أو الحملي . والأصل في استخدام هذا المنهج القياسي أن يكون في المجالات التي يتعذر فيها إخضاع الظاهرة للتجربة، كمجالات الدراسات الفلكية، وطبقات الأرض وأغوارها العميقة، وكظاهرة المد والجزر المرتبطة بجاذبية القمر، وكالأحداث التاريخية الماضية، والوقائع المنتهية، وأشباه ذلك . هذا هو الأصل فيها، ولكن قد تستخدم أيضاً في المجالات التي لا تتعذر فيها التجربة . فبعد الاستنباط الافتراضي للعلاقة السببية للظاهرة من مجموعة عناصرها، وجميع ما يتعلق بها، والآثار التي تترتب عليها، يقوم الباحث بالتأكد من صحة الفرض أو رجحانه، بدراسة ما يتعلق به من أمور تلزم عنه، أو يلزم هو عنها، أو يدخل في ضمنها أو ينطبق عليها . ومن أمثلة استخدام هذا المنهج القياسي : لما فسر «نيوتن» حركة القمر حول الأرض بأنها تنشأ بسبب جاذبية الأرض للقمر، أراد أن يتأكد من صحة هذا الفرض، فاستعان بمعلوماته الفلكية السابقة وبالقوانين الرياضية، فأجرى القياس التالي : إذا كانت الأرض تجذب القمر نحوها، فلا بد أن ينحرف القمر في مداره ستة عشر قدماً تقريباً في الدقيقة الواحدة . أي يلزم من صحة هذا الفرض وجود هذه النتيجة، لأنها لازمة عنه . وكان باستطاعته أن يتأكد من صحة هذه النتيجة أو عدم صحتها بطريقة مباشرة، وهي الملاحظة الفلكية . وبهذا المنهج القياسي غير المباشر يُعلّل المؤرخ أسباب ظاهرة من الظواهر التاريخية، ويستنتج أسباب حادثة من الحوادث، كحادثة سرقة، أو حادثة قتل، أو نحو ذلك . ب - وأما المنهج المباشر : فهو المنهج الذي يستخدم فيه الباحث التجربة، لتأكيد الفروض أو تحقيقها . وقد أحصى الفيلسوف الإنكليزي «جون ستيوارت ميل» لهذا المنهج المباشر خمس طرائق اعتبرت من مبتكراته، وأطلق عليها اسم «قواعد الاستقراء» . الطريقة الأولى : النظر في الاتفاق، أي : في التلازم في الوقوع (إذا وجد وجد) . الطريقة الثانية : النظر في الاختلاف، أي : في التلازم في التخلف، (إذا لم يوجد لم يوجد) . الطريقة الثالثة : النظر في الاتفاق والاختلاف معاً، أي : في التلازم في الوقوع والتخلف (إذا وجد وجد، وإذا لم يوجد لم يوجد) . الطريقة الرابعة : النظر في التغير النسبي، أي : في التلازم في نسبة
The third stage of the stages of induction: it is the stage of verifying hypotheses or weighting them and considering them an accepted scientific theory. The researcher has two methods to verify his scientific hypotheses or weight them or reject them: 1- Indirect method. 2- Direct method, which is the more sound if it can be achieved. A- As for the indirect method: it is the method in which experiment is not used to prove the hypothesis or weight it, but the syllogistic method is used, which is the method of logical syllogism based on implication from which conditional syllogisms are formulated, or based on inclusion or correspondence from which categorical syllogisms are formulated, and it is not required to state explicitly the forms of conditional or categorical syllogism. The principle in using this syllogistic method is that it is in fields where it is impossible to subject the phenomenon to experiment, such as the fields of astronomical studies, the layers of the earth and its deep depths, and the phenomenon of tides linked to the moon's gravity, and past historical events, concluded occurrences, and the like. This is the principle in it, but it may also be used in fields where experiment is not impossible. After the hypothetical deduction of the causal relationship of the phenomenon from its set of elements, and all that relates to it, and the effects that follow from it, the researcher proceeds to verify the correctness of the hypothesis or its weight, by studying matters related to it that are necessary from it, or that it is necessary from them, or that enter into its scope or correspond to it. Examples of using this syllogistic method: when Newton interpreted the moon's motion around the earth as arising from the earth's attraction of the moon, he wanted to verify the correctness of this hypothesis, so he used his previous astronomical information and mathematical laws, and conducted the following syllogism: If the earth attracts the moon towards it, then the moon must deviate in its orbit by approximately sixteen feet per minute. That is, from the correctness of this hypothesis, this result must follow, because it is necessary from it. And he was able to verify the correctness of this result or its incorrectness by a direct method, namely astronomical observation. With this indirect syllogistic method, the historian explains the causes of a historical phenomenon, and deduces the causes of an incident, such as a theft incident, or a murder incident, or the like. B- As for the direct method: it is the method in which the researcher uses experiment to confirm hypotheses or verify them. The English philosopher John Stuart Mill enumerated for this direct method five methods that were considered his innovations, and he called them 'the rules of induction'. First method: observation of agreement, i.e., concurrence in occurrence (if it exists, it exists). Second method: observation of difference, i.e., concurrence in absence (if it does not exist, it does not exist). Third method: observation of agreement and difference together, i.e., concurrence in occurrence and absence (if it exists, it exists, and if it does not exist, it does not exist). Fourth method: observation of relative change, i.e., concurrence in proportion
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage describes the third stage of induction, which is the verification of hypotheses. The author presents two methods: indirect and direct. The indirect method uses logical syllogism, either conditional (based on implication) or categorical (based on inclusion or correspondence), and is primarily applied in fields where experimentation is impossible, such as astronomy, geology, tides, and history. The author illustrates with Newton's hypothesis that the moon's motion is due to Earth's gravity; Newton deduced that if true, the moon should deviate 16 feet per minute, which was later confirmed by observation. The direct method involves experiment, and the author introduces John Stuart Mill's five rules of induction, listing the first four: method of agreement, method of difference, joint method of agreement and difference, and method of concomitant variation (the fourth is cut off). The passage emphasizes that both methods aim to confirm or refute hypotheses, with the direct method being more sound when feasible.

Key Terms
استقراء
فرض
منهج قياسي

B) Experiment is impossible
C) Hypothesis is already proven
D) Only for historical events

Key Takeaway
The indirect syllogistic method is a powerful tool for testing hypotheses in non-empirical Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a conditional syllogism to test the hypothesis that 'the Quran is a miracle (mu'jiza)'. Assume that if it is a miracle, then no human can produce its like. Deduce the consequence and verify it using historical evidence.
?

Applying Mill's Methods of Induction in Islamic Jurisprudence
Author defines the third stage of induction and distinguishes indirect and direct methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Mill's method of agreement to identify the 'illah (effective cause) of a fiqh ruling.

Core Idea
Mill's direct methods, though modern, align with classical juristic reasoning for causal inference.

The direct method uses experiment to verify hypotheses. John Stuart Mill formulated five methods. In Islamic jurisprudence, these methods can be applied to identify the 'illah of a ruling. We will focus on the method of agreement, which looks for a common factor in all instances of a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Collect instances of the ruling
Gather cases where the ruling (hukm) applies. For example, the prohibition of riba applies to gold, silver, wheat, barley, dates, salt.
2
Identify common factors
Look for a factor present in all cases. In the riba example, all items are measured by weight or volume.
3
Formulate the hypothesis of 'illah
Propose that the common factor is the effective cause. For riba, the hypothesis: 'the 'illah is measurement by weight or volume'.
4
Test for disconfirming cases
Check if there is any case where the factor is present but the ruling is absent. If not, the hypothesis is strengthened. This is the method of difference.

The Indirect Method: Conditional Syllogism in Inquiry
Author explains indirect method through a scientific example and extends it to history.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the indirect method as a conditional syllogism and apply it to verify a hypothesis in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Test hypotheses by deriving necessary consequences, then verify them directly.

This passage introduces the indirect method, a powerful reasoning tool for when direct observation is unavailable. The author illustrates it with a scientific hypothesis about the moon's orbit and extends it to historical explanation. You will learn to identify the conditional structure and the crucial verification step that distinguishes sound reasoning from speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hypothesis (antecedent)
Find the initial claim being tested. In the passage, the hypothesis is that Earth's gravity pulls the moon.
In Islamic sciences, the hypothesis might be a proposed 'illah (cause) for a ruling.
2
Derive a necessary consequence (consequent)
From the hypothesis, deduce what must be observable if the hypothesis is true. Here, the moon must deviate 16 feet per minute in its orbit.
The consequence must be logically necessary, not just probable.
3
Formulate the conditional syllogism
Set up the 'if...then' structure: If hypothesis, then consequence. This is a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i muttasil).
The passage uses this structure: 'If the Earth pulls the moon, then the moon must deviate...'
4
Verify the consequence through direct observation
Check whether the predicted consequence actually occurs. In the passage, this is done via astronomical observation.
This verification step distinguishes the indirect method from mere speculation.
5
Apply the same structure to historical reasoning
For historical events, treat the event as the consequent and infer a hypothetical cause as antecedent. Verify by checking whether the expected traces of that cause are present.
The historian uses indirect evidence, not direct observation of the past.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حول الأرض بأنها تنشأ بسبب جاذبية الأرض للقمر، أراد أن يتأكد من صحة هذا الفرض، فاستعان بمعلوماته الفلكية السابقة وبالقوانين الرياضية، فأجرى القياس التالي :
إذا كانت الأرض تجذب القمر نحوها، فلا بد أن ينحرف القمر في مداره ستة عشر قدماً تقريباً في الدقيقة الواحدة .
أي يلزم من صحة هذا الفرض وجود هذه النتيجة، لأنها لازمة عنه .
وكان باستطاعته أن يتأكد من صحة هذه النتيجة أو عدم صحتها بطريقة مباشرة، وهي الملاحظة الفلكية .
وبهذا المنهج القياسي غير المباشر يُعلّل المؤرخ أسباب ظاهرة من الظواهر التاريخية، ويستنتج أسباب حادثة من الحوادث، كحادثة سرقة، أو حادثة قتل، أو نحو ذلك .
About the Earth that it arises because of the Earth's gravity pulling the moon. He wanted to verify the correctness of this hypothesis, so he used his prior astronomical knowledge and mathematical laws, and performed the following syllogism: If the Earth pulls the moon towards it, then the moon must deviate in its orbit about sixteen feet per minute. That is, the correctness of this hypothesis necessitates the existence of this result, because it is a necessary consequence of it. He was able to verify the correctness of this result or its incorrectness in a direct way, which is astronomical observation. And with this indirect syllogistic method, the historian explains the causes of a historical phenomenon, and deduces the causes of an event, such as a theft, a murder, and so on.

Commentary
This passage introduces the indirect method (al-manhaj al-qiyasi ghayr al-mubashir), a form of reasoning used when direct observation is not immediately possible. It begins with a scientific example: a researcher hypothesizes that the Earth's gravity causes the moon's orbital deviation. He formulates a conditional syllogism: if Earth pulls the moon, then the moon must deviate at a specific rate. From the hypothesis (the antecedent), the conclusion (the consequent) follows necessarily. The researcher then can verify the conclusion directly via astronomical observation. The author notes that the same indirect method is applied by historians: given an observed event (e.g., a murder), the historian infers its cause through a similar conditional reasoning, where the event is the consequent and the hypothetical cause is the antecedent. Crucially, the direct verification step distinguishes this from pure speculation; the hypothesis is tested by checking the predicted observation.

The Direct Method and the Law of Causality
Defining the direct method and its foundation in causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the direct method and explain how it depends on the principle of causality.

Core Idea
Every inductive method presupposes causality.

The passage introduces the direct method (al-manhaj al-mubashir) as an experimental approach to confirm hypotheses. It then emphasizes that all inductive methods rely on the universal law of causality—no phenomenon occurs without a cause. This principle is foundational for both scientific inquiry and Islamic legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh), where causality is essential for identifying 'illal (effective causes).

Guided Path
1
Identify the direct method's definition
Find the phrase 'المنهج المباشر' and note that it involves using experiment to confirm hypotheses.
2
Locate the law of causality statement
The author says all methods depend on the law of causality. Underline that principle.
3
Consider the epistemological implication
Why is causality necessary? Without it, no inference from observed instances to general laws is valid.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, the principle of causality underlies the identification of 'illah for qiyas. For example, intoxication is the effective cause for the prohibition of khamr, and by causality, it extends to all intoxicants.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب - وأما المنهج المباشر : فهو المنهج الذي يستخدم فيه الباحث التجربة، لتأكيد الفروض أو تحقيقها .
وقد أحصى الفيلسوف الإنكليزي «جون ستيوارت ميل» لهذا المنهج المباشر خمس طرائق اعتبرت من مبتكراته، وأطلق عليها اسم «قواعد الاستقراء» .
الطريقة الأولى : النظر في الاتفاق، أي : في التلازم في الوقوع (إذا وجد وجد) .
الطريقة الثانية : النظر في الاختلاف، أي : في التلازم في التخلف، (إذا لم يوجد لم يوجد) .
الطريقة الثالثة : النظر في الاتفاق والاختلاف معاً، أي : في التلازم في الوقوع والتخلف (إذا وجد وجد، وإذا لم يوجد لم يوجد) .
الطريقة الرابعة : النظر في التغير النسبي، أي : في التلازم في نسبة التغير، تزايداً في جانب الوجود، وتناقصاً في جانب العدم.
الطريقة الخامسة : النظر في البواقي .
وقبل أن نعالج هذه الطرائق الخمس بالشرح، نشير إلى أنها جميعاً تعتمد على قانون السببية العام في الوجود، فما من ظاهرة إلا لها سبب، وأنها جميعاً تستخدم في الكشف عن العلل والأسباب، كما تستخدم في تأكيد الفروض أو التحقق من صحتها .
B - As for the direct method: it is the method in which the researcher uses experiment to confirm or verify hypotheses. The English philosopher John Stuart Mill counted for this direct method five ways that were considered his innovations, and he called them 'the rules of induction'. The first method: consideration of agreement, i.e., the correlation in occurrence (if it exists, it exists). The second method: consideration of difference, i.e., the correlation in absence (if it does not exist, it does not exist). The third method: consideration of agreement and difference together, i.e., the correlation in occurrence and absence (if it exists, it exists, and if it does not exist, it does not exist). The fourth method: consideration of concomitant variation, i.e., the correlation in the proportion of change, increasing on the side of existence and decreasing on the side of non-existence. The fifth method: consideration of residues. And before we deal with these five methods by explanation, we point out that they all depend on the general law of causality in existence, for no phenomenon is without a cause, and that they are all used in discovering causes and reasons, as well as in confirming hypotheses or verifying their correctness.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage defines the direct method (al-manhaj al-mubashir) as one that employs experiment to confirm or verify hypotheses. It then attributes to John Stuart Mill the formulation of five specific methods within this direct approach, collectively called 'the rules of induction' (qawa'id al-istiqra'). The author enumerates them: (1) Method of Agreement – look for a circumstance that is always present when the phenomenon occurs; (2) Method of Difference – look for a circumstance that is always absent when the phenomenon is absent; (3) Joint Method of Agreement and Difference – combine both criteria; (4) Method of Concomitant Variation – look for a circumstance that varies in proportion to the phenomenon; (5) Method of Residues – subtract known causes to infer remaining ones. Before detailing these methods, the author notes a crucial epistemological prerequisite: all these methods presuppose the universal law of causality – every phenomenon has a cause. Their purpose is both to discover causes and to test hypotheses. The enumeration is clear, but the author will explain each in subsequent sections.

Key Terms
منهج مباشر
قواعد الاستقراء
قانون السببية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principle of non-contradiction.
B) The general law of causality in existence.
C) The law of identity.
D) The principle of sufficient reason.

Key Takeaway
All inductive methods presuppose that every phenomenon has a cause, which is a precondition for any empirical inquiry.

Mill's Five Inductive Canons
Enumerating Mill's five canons of induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list Mill's five inductive methods and describe the core idea of each.

Core Idea
Mill's five methods provide systematic ways to identify causes.

After defining the direct method, the passage enumerates the five rules of induction (qawa'id al-istiqra') formulated by John Stuart Mill: Method of Agreement, Method of Difference, Joint Method, Method of Concomitant Variation, and Method of Residues. These are standard tools for causal inference in both modern science and traditional Islamic reasoning patterns.

Guided Path
1
List the five methods in order
Write down the Arabic names: الإتفاق، الإختلاف، الإتفاق والإختلاف معاً، التغير النسبي، البواقي.
2
Understand the logic of each
For each, note how it relates to presence/absence or variation of a potential cause.
3
Identify the unifying principle
All methods depend on causality, as stated in the passage.
4
Relate to Islamic induction (istiqra')
While classical Islamic logic did not formalize these canons, the underlying reasoning appears in juristic methods for identifying 'illah, such as sabr wa taqsim (division) which resembles the method of difference.

The Method of Agreement: Identifying Causes by Common Circumstances
Author explains the method of agreement with a medical example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Method of Agreement to isolate a probable cause from multiple cases in an Islamic science context.

Core Idea
The cause is the sole circumstance common to all cases of the phenomenon.

This lesson introduces the first of Mill's inductive methods, the Method of Agreement. The author explains how to identify the cause by finding the one factor present in every instance of the phenomenon. We will work through a medical example and then connect it to Islamic sciences like uṣūl al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon and cases
Start with a phenomenon you want to explain (e.g., a disease). Collect multiple cases where it occurs. In the passage, the phenomenon is a sudden disease affecting several patients.
2
List all circumstances for each case
For each case, record all relevant circumstances (e.g., what they ate, where they were, their age). The passage mentions the doctor surveyed the circumstances of each patient.
3
Find the common circumstance
Compare the lists. Identify the circumstance that is present in every case, while all others vary. In the example, only the herb was common to all patients.
This step is the core of the method – it eliminates variable factors.
4
Conclude probable cause
The common circumstance is the probable cause. The author uses 'yatarajjaḥ' (becomes inclined) indicating probability, not certainty.
Induction does not prove causation; it yields a hypothesis for further testing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعالج بعد هذا شرح هذه الطرائق .
۱ - شرح الطريقة الأولى : وهي النظر في الاتفاق، أي: النظر إلى حالات الظاهرة المدروسة، واستخراج الظرف الوحيد الذي اتفقت عليه الحالات، فلم يفارق في أية حالة منها، مع أن الظروف الأخرى وجدت في بعض الحالات، وتخلفت في الأخرى دون أن يؤثر ذلك على الظاهرة شيئاً .
يقول «ستيوارت ميل» في تحديد هذه الطريقة :
«وإذا اتفقت حالتان أو أكثر للظاهرة المطلوب بحثها في ظرف واحد فقط، واختلفت هذه الحالات فيما عداه، فإن هذا الظرف الوحيد الذي تتفق فيه هو سبب الظاهرة» .
مثال ذلك : عرض على طبيب مجموعة من المصابين فجأة بمرض معين، فافترض أن سبب المرض تناولهم لشيء معين، فأحصى ظروف كل منهم، فلم يجد ظرفاً اتفق فيه الجميع إلا ظرفاً واحداً، هو أنهم أكلوا من عشبة معينة .
