And after this we will deal with the explanation of these methods.
1 - Explanation of the first method: it is the consideration of agreement, i.e., examining the cases of the studied phenomenon and extracting the single circumstance that the cases agreed upon, so that it did not leave any case, while other circumstances were present in some cases and absent in others without affecting the phenomenon at all.
Stuart Mill says in defining this method:
'If two or more cases of the phenomenon under investigation agree only in one circumstance, and differ in all others, then that sole circumstance in which they all agree is the cause of the phenomenon.'
An example: a doctor was presented with a group of people suddenly afflicted with a specific disease. He hypothesized that the cause was their consumption of a certain thing. He surveyed the circumstances of each of them and found no circumstance that all agreed upon except one: they had eaten a certain herb.
Here, the doctor becomes inclined that the consumption of this herb was indeed the cause of contracting this disease.
The process of verifying the hypothesis was accomplished through induction, and the agreement of the occurrence of the phenomenon in a specific circumstance, which indicated that this circumstance is
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage explains the first inductive method: the Method of Agreement (ṭarīqat al-ittifāq). The author begins by stating that after the enumeration, he will now explain the methods. The first method involves examining multiple cases of the phenomenon in question and isolating the single circumstance that is present in all cases, while other circumstances vary. The key is that only one circumstance is common; if other circumstances were present in some cases but absent in others, they are ruled out as causes. Mill's definition is quoted: if two or more cases of the phenomenon agree only in one circumstance and differ in all others, that sole common circumstance is the cause. The example given: a doctor sees several patients with the same disease. He hypothesizes that they all consumed something. After checking their circumstances, the only common factor is that they all ate a particular herb. Therefore, the herb is likely the cause. The author concludes that this process of verification is induction, based on the agreement of the phenomenon's occurrence with a specific circumstance. However, the text cuts off mid-sentence at the page break. The passage is incomplete; the next pages would presumably continue the explanation or provide further examples.

Key Terms
طريقة الاتفاق (ṭarīqat al-ittifāq): The first of Mill's inductive methods: the cause is the only circumstance common to all instances of the phenomenon. [Note: This is a modern formulation; classical mantiq does not have a separate method named 'agreement' in this sense.]
ظرف (ẓarf): A circumstance or factor that may be a candidate for the cause of the phenomenon. [Note: In classical mantiq, 'ẓarf' is a category (time, place); here it is broadened to any relevant condition.]
سبب (sabab): The cause of the phenomenon, identified as the common circumstance. [Note: Stable term; in kalam it often means 'means' or 'occasion', but here it is the direct cause.]

The Method of Co-extensiveness and Co-exclusiveness (al-Tard wa al-'Aks)
Author describes the method of al-tard wa al-'aks for extracting 'illa, with example and response to its validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of al-tard wa al-'aks to identify the 'illa (effective cause) in a given legal ruling and assess whether it yields certain or probable knowledge.

Core Idea
Checking both presence and absence reveals the 'illa, but only with probability in legal matters.

In usul al-fiqh, jurists need to extract the 'illa behind a ruling to extend it to new cases. One key method is al-tard wa al-'aks, which examines the co-occurrence of a ruling with a proposed attribute. This passage explains how it works and clarifies its epistemological weight—it gives only probable knowledge in legal matters, yet that is sufficient for practical theorizing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions of the method
The method requires both tard (co-extensiveness: the ruling is present when the attribute is present) and 'aks (co-exclusiveness: the ruling is absent when the attribute is absent). Only when both hold does the attribute qualify as an 'illa.
Some mistakenly think only one condition is sufficient; both are necessary.
2
Examine the example from khamr
The example: prohibition (tahrim) exists when intoxicating intensity (al-shidda al-muskrira) exists, and prohibition does not exist when intensity does not exist. This indicates that intensity is the 'illa of prohibition.
Note that the 'illa is not the substance itself but the property of intoxication.
3
Understand the epistemological claim
The passage says that in rational matters this method gives certainty, but in legal matters the 'illa is only an indicator (amara), not a necessitating cause. Therefore the method yields only probable knowledge (ghalabat al-zann).
Probable knowledge is still considered valid for legal reasoning in the absence of stronger evidence.
4
Evaluate the author's conclusion
The author affirms that this method is sufficient for extracting theories and benefiting from them in life, as long as nothing stronger comes that refutes or nullifies it. This reflects the pragmatic approach to legal evidence.
Think about what could refute: a counterexample where the attribute is present but no ruling, or absent but ruling still applies.

وتدخل هذه الطريقة ضمن أنواع «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة في علم أصول الفقه . واستخراج العلة بهذه الطريقة استخراج لها عن طريق الطرد والعكس معاً . ومن أمثلتها عندهم : وجود التحريم عند وجود الشدة المسكرة في الخمر، وعدم وجود التحريم عند عدم وجود الشدة المسكرة في الخمر، وهذا يدلّ على أن الشدّة هي علة التحريم . قالوا : وهذا دليل على صحة العلة في العقليات وهي فيها موجبة، فأولى أن يكون دليلاً على صحة العلة في الشرعيات، والعلة فيها من قبيل الأمارة لا من قبيل الأمر الموجب، واستدلوا أيضاً بأنه يغلب على الظن ثبوت الحكم مستنداً إلى ذلك الوصف . ونحن نقول : إنّ هذا الدليل يفيد في الأسباب الطبيعية غلبة الظن، وهذا يكفي لاستخراج النظريات والانتفاع بها في الحياة، ما لم يأت ما هو أقوى منها، أو ما ينقضها ويلغيها .
And this method enters under the types of 'takhrij manat al-hukm' known in the science of usul al-fiqh. Extracting the 'illa by this method is extracting it by way of co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness together. Among their examples: the existence of prohibition when the intoxicating intensity exists in khamr, and the non-existence of prohibition when the intoxicating intensity does not exist in khamr. This indicates that the intensity is the 'illa of prohibition. They said: This is evidence for the validity of the 'illa in rational matters, and it is affirmative there, so it is more appropriate to be evidence for the validity of the 'illa in legal matters, and the 'illa there is of the type of indication, not of the type of necessitating cause. They also argued that it gives probable knowledge of the ruling based on that attribute. And we say: This evidence benefits probable knowledge in natural causes, and this is sufficient for extracting theories and benefiting from them in life, as long as nothing stronger comes, or something that refutes and nullifies it.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines the third method of extracting the legal cause ('illa) known as al-tard wa al-'aks (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness). The author explains that this method works by checking two things: the presence of the ruling when the attribute is present, and the absence of the ruling when the attribute is absent. The example given is from the prohibition of khamr (wine): when the intoxicating intensity (shidda muskrira) is present, prohibition exists; when it is absent, prohibition does not exist. This indicates that the intensity is the cause ('illa) of prohibition. The author then presents a claim by some that this method is valid in rational matters and even more so in legal matters, though in legal matters the 'illa is only an indicator (amara) not a necessitating cause. The author responds by affirming that this method yields only probable knowledge (ghalabat al-zann) in natural causes, which is sufficient for practical theorizing as long as no stronger contrary evidence appears. The passage thus clarifies the epistemological status of this method, emphasizing its probabilistic nature.

The Fourth Method: Examining Relative Variation (al-Taghyir al-Nisbi)
Author defines the fourth method of causal inference with furnace and Pasteur examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth method of causal inference (al-taghyir al-nisbi) and apply it to a hypothetical fiqh scenario to confirm an 'illa.

Core Idea
Proportional variation strengthens causal proof beyond mere presence and absence.

The previous method (agreement and difference) established causality by noting that when the cause is present, the effect is present, and when absent, the effect is absent. But what if we can vary the cause in degree? The fourth method, called examining relative variation (al-taghyir al-nisbi), adds a powerful quantitative dimension. By confirming that increasing the cause increases the effect, and decreasing it decreases the effect, we gain stronger evidence that the hypothesized cause is genuine. This method is used not only in modern science but also in Islamic legal theory to verify effective causes ('ilal) for rulings.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of concomitant variation
The fourth method requires that changes in the supposed cause correspond proportionally to changes in the effect, both in increase and decrease. This goes beyond simple presence/absence.
2
Identify the phenomenon and hypothesized cause
Clearly specify what you are trying to explain (the effect) and what you suspect is the cause. In the furnace example, the phenomenon is the level of ignition, and the hypothesized cause is the position of a particular valve.
Ensure the cause is quantifiable or at least gradable.
3
Verify existence and non-existence
First, confirm that when the cause is present, the effect is present, and when the cause is absent, the effect is absent. This is the baseline from the third method.
Without this, proportional variation alone might be coincidental.
4
Test proportional increase and decrease
Increase the cause in degree and observe if the effect increases proportionally. Then decrease the cause and observe if the effect decreases proportionally. This provides a 'new indicator' as the author says.
The more precise the correlation, the stronger the evidence.
5
Eliminate alternative causes
Check that changing other potential causes does not produce the same effect. In the furnace, moving other valves should not alter the flame. This is part of the method.
This is essentially controlled experimentation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - شرح الطريقة الرابعة : وهي النظر في التغيّر النسبي، أي: في التلازم في نسبة التغير وجوداً وعدماً . وهذه الطريقة تزيد على الطريقة الثالثة بالتحقق من نسبة التغير في الظاهرة : ارتفاعاً في جانب الوجود مع ارتفاع كمية الشيء المفروض أنه هو السبب في الظاهرة، وانخفاضاً في اتجاه العدم بالتناقص الجزئي المساوي لتناقص كمية الشيء المفروض أنه هو السبب في الظاهرة . مثال ذلك : لو وجد فرن مشتعل بوقود من البترول وفي الخارج عدد من المفاتيح اللولبية التي تتناقص وتتزايد في العطاء، وهذه المفاتيح مجهولة الوظائف بالنسبة إلينا، فبعضها للوقود، وبعضها للهواء، وبعضها للماء، وبعضها له وظائف أخرى، ونحن نريد أن نعرف مفتاح الوقود لنتحكم بنسبة إمداد الفرن بالطاقة الكافية . فإذا فرضنا أن أحدها هو مفتاح الوقود، كان باستطاعتنا أن نجري طريقة التأكد من التغير النسبي . فإذا ارتبطت زيادة الاشتعال ونقصه إلى حدّ الانطفاء بالمفتاح المفروض، ولم تحدث هذه الظاهرة عن طريق تحريك المفاتيح الأخرى، كان ذلك دليلاً على أنه هو السبب الموصل لطاقة الوقود . وفي هذه الطريقة يتبين لنا وجود الظاهرة عند وجود السبب، وانعدام الظاهرة عند انعدام السبب، ونسبة تكاثر وجود كمية الظاهرة موازية لنسبة تكاثر وجود كمية السبب، ونسبة تناقص وجود كمية الظاهرة موازية لنسبة تناقص وجود كمية السبب . ففي هذه الطريقة إضافة شاهد مرجّح جديد على طريقة الاتفاق والاختلاف معاً، هي طريقة التغير النسبي . ومن أمثلة تطبيق هذه الطريقة ما أجراه العالم الفرنسي «باستير» حين أراد التحقق من نظريته في الجراثيم . وقصة ذلك أن «باستير» أعلن أن كلّ كائن حي إنما يتولد من كائن حيّ آخر، وأنّ التعفّن يرجع إلى وجود حيوانات دقيقة جداً تتناثر مع الذرات الصغيرة، ويحملها الهواء فتقع على الأجسام والسوائل فتتغذى بها وتتكاثر . وقد نقض «باستير» بنظريته هذه، الفكرة التي كانت سائدة من قبل، والتي كانت ترى أنه قد تتولد كائنات حيّة دون أن تشتق من كائنات حية . وليحقق «باستير» ما افترضه، أخذ في أول الأمر أنبوبتين ووضع في
4 - Explanation of the fourth method: It is the examination of relative variation, i.e., the concomitance in the proportion of variation in existence and non-existence. This method adds to the third method by verifying the proportion of variation in the phenomenon: an increase in the side of existence with an increase in the quantity of the thing assumed to be the cause of the phenomenon, and a decrease in the direction of non-existence through partial decrease equal to the decrease in the quantity of the thing assumed to be the cause of the phenomenon. An example of that: Suppose there is a furnace burning with petroleum fuel, and outside there are a number of screw valves that decrease and increase in output, and these valves are unknown in function to us: some are for fuel, some for air, some for water, some for other functions, and we want to know the fuel valve to control the rate of fuel supply to the furnace with sufficient energy. So if we assume that one of them is the fuel valve, we can conduct the method of verifying relative variation. If the increase and decrease of ignition, up to the point of extinguishing, are linked to the assumed valve, and this phenomenon does not occur by moving the other valves, then that is evidence that it is the cause that delivers the fuel energy. In this method, we see the existence of the phenomenon when the cause exists, the non-existence of the phenomenon when the cause is absent, the proportion of increase in the quantity of the phenomenon parallel to the proportion of increase in the quantity of the cause, and the proportion of decrease in the quantity of the phenomenon parallel to the proportion of decrease in the quantity of the cause. So in this method there is an additional supporting indicator over the method of agreement and difference together: it is the method of relative variation. Among the examples of the application of this method is what the French scientist Pasteur did when he wanted to verify his theory of germs. The story is that Pasteur announced that every living organism is generated only from another living organism, and that putrefaction is due to the existence of very tiny animals that scatter with small atoms, carried by air, falling on bodies and liquids, feeding on them and multiplying. With this theory, Pasteur refuted the previously prevalent idea which held that living organisms could be generated without deriving from living organisms. To verify his assumption, Pasteur first took two test tubes and placed in
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth method of extracting the 'illa, called 'examining relative variation' (al-taghyir al-nisbi). The author explains that it builds on the third method by adding a quantitative dimension: not only does one check the presence and absence of the cause and effect, but also the proportion of variation. If increasing the cause increases the effect, and decreasing the cause decreases the effect, then the hypothesized cause is confirmed. The example of a furnace with multiple valves illustrates this: by assuming one valve controls the fuel, one observes that turning it up increases the flame (and turning it down decreases it), while other valves have no such effect. This confirms the cause. The author then summarizes that this method involves four indicators: existence with existence, absence with absence, proportional increase, and proportional decrease. Finally, Pasteur's germ theory is mentioned as an application, though the description is cut off. The method is essentially the 'method of concomitant variation' known in modern logic, but framed within Islamic juristic methodology for discovering causal relationships.

The Method of Remainder (Ṭarīqat al-Bawāqī)
Author defines and illustrates the method of remainder with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of remainder to isolate the cause of a phenomenon by subtracting known causes.

Core Idea
Subtract known causes; the remainder reveals the unknown cause.

The method of remainder is a natural reasoning tool used daily. In this lesson, we will examine its structure through the author's examples, including a poetic story and cases from crime investigation and revolution analysis. You will learn to identify the steps of subtractive elimination and the crucial requirement of exhaustive enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon and its components
Determine the overall phenomenon and break it down into parts that may be assigned to different causes.
In the food prohibition example, the phenomenon is 'prohibition' with possible causes: harm, impurity, filth, ownership.
2
List known causes and their effects
Identify which parts of the phenomenon are already explained by known causes.
Known effects: if food is harmful, it is prohibited; if impure, prohibited; etc.
3
Subtract the effects of known causes
Remove the parts explained by known causes from the total phenomenon.
If the food is not harmful and not impure, subtract those causes.
4
Attribute the remainder to the remaining cause(s)
The unexplained part must be caused by the remaining cause.
Since only 'ownership by another' remains, that is the cause of prohibition.
5
Ensure the survey of causes was exhaustive
The method only works if all possible causes are enumerated before subtraction.
If a cause is missing, the conclusion may be wrong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريقة الطرح بربط المعلوم بالمعلوم، وحصر الباقي بالباقي، طريق مستخدمة في الفكر اليومي عند الإنسان، لذلك نجد النصوص البليغة كثيراً ما تترك بيان ربط البواقي من الأسباب بمسبباتها، اعتماداً على فهم الفكر له من غير جهد، وإيجازاً في الكلام. وقد ذكرتني هذه الطريقة بقصة بعض الشعراء العاديين، إذ أراد أن يسخر من شاعر آخر فقال له: الشعراء ثلاثة: شاعر، وشعرور، وسخيف (على سبيل الشتيمة)، فأي الشعراء أنت؟ فأجابه صاحبه على الفور: أما أنا فشعرور، واقتسم أنت والمتنبي الباقي. ومعلوم لكليهما أن المتنبي سيأخذ في القسمة وصف شاعر، فبقي الباقي للباقي، وبهذا ردّ الشتيمة عليه، فكان من أراد السخرية هو المسخور منه. وقد عرّف «ستيوارت ميل» هذه الطريقة بقوله: «اطرح من أي ظاهرة الأشياء المعروف أنها معلولة لبعض علل معروفة فيكون الباقي من الظاهرة معلولاً للبعض الآخر من تلك العلل». أمثلة على تطبيق هذه الطريقة: ١ - ظهرت عدة جرائم، وقُبض المجرمون، واعترفوا إلا واحداً منهم لم يعترف بجريمته، فإننا سنحكم بأنه صاحب الجريمة الباقية، سواء اعترف أو لم يعترف، لأن العلم بكونه مجرماً متحقق لدينا، والذي نبحث عنه إنما هو تعيين الجريمة التي ارتكبها هو. ٢ - قامت ثورة على السلطة الحاكمة في بلد ما، فعلمنا أن بعض المشتركين فيها طامعون بالحكم، وأن بعض المشتركين مستأجرون بالمال، وعلمنا أن النقمة على سياسة السلطة الحاكمة من أسباب الثورة، عندئذ نعلم - عن طريق حصر البواقي - أن الثائرين الذين ليسوا طامعين بالحكم ولا مستأجرين بالمال، هم ناقمون على سياسة السلطة الحاكمة. ٣ - ومن أمثلة استخدام هذه الطريقة في الشرعيات ما يلي: علمنا أن السبب في تحريم بعض الأطعمة إما كونها ضارة، وإما كونها مستخبثة، وإما كونها نجسة، وإما كونها ملك الغير. لكن هذا الطعام الشهي ليس ضاراً، ولا مستخبثاً، ولا نجساً، فما السبب في تحريمه؟ سندرك الجواب من غير جهد فكري، إن السبب في تحريمه كونه ملك الغير، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به، وقد حصلنا على هذه النتيجة إذ طرحنا من الأسباب ما نعلم أنه متعلق بطعام آخر غير هذا، وحصرنا الأمر بالسبب الأخير، إذن فهو سبب التحريم. ويشترط هنا أن يكون سبر الأسباب حاصراً لجميع ما يُعلَّل به. مقابل هذه الطريقة في الفكر الإسلامي: يقابل طريقة البواقي هذه طريقة السبر والتقسيم في الفكر الإسلامي، والفارق بين الطريقتين: أن طريقة البواقي توزع المسببات في الظاهرة على أسبابها، وتحصر الباقي من الظاهرة في الباقي من الأسباب، أما طريقة السبر والتقسيم فتقوم على حصر جميع الأسباب المحتملة، وإسقاط واحد بعد آخر بالدليل، وحصر الأمر في السبب الأخير. ويستخدم السبر والتقسيم في استخراج علة الحكم في الفقه الإسلامي، وله عندهم أصول وضوابط، ويشترط في السبر أن يكون حاصراً، وفي الإسقاط أن يكون صحيحاً، والسبر والتقسيم طريق من طرق «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة أنواعه في علم أصول الفقه. وربما كانت طريقة السبر والتقسيم أكثر ضبطاً من طريقة البواقي، وبإمكان طريقة السبر والتقسيم أن تشتمل على طريقة البواقي إذا كانت طريقة البواقي حاصرة ومنضبطة. وقد تبدو طريقة البواقي أيسر ـ في العمليات المادية ـ من طريقة السبر
The method of subtraction by linking the known to the known, and restricting the remainder to the remainder, is a method used in daily human thinking. Therefore we find eloquent texts often leave out the explanation of linking the remaining causes to their effects, relying on the mind's understanding of it without effort, and for brevity in speech. This method reminded me of a story of some ordinary poets; one wanted to mock another poet and said: 'Poets are three: a poet, a poetaster, and a fool (by way of insult). Which of the poets are you?' His companion immediately replied: 'As for me, I am a poetaster. You and al-Mutanabbi divide the rest.' It was known to both that al-Mutanabbi would take the description of poet in the division, so the remainder remained for the remainder, and thus the insult was returned, so the one who intended mockery became the mocked. Stuart Mill defined this method by saying: 'Subtract from any phenomenon the parts known to be caused by certain known causes, and the remainder of the phenomenon is the effect of the remaining causes.' Examples of applying this method: 1 - Several crimes appeared, the criminals were arrested, and they confessed except one who did not confess his crime. We will judge that he is the perpetrator of the remaining crime, whether he confesses or not, because the knowledge that he is a criminal is established for us, and what we are seeking is only to determine which crime he committed. 2 - A revolution arose against the ruling authority in a country. We knew that some participants were ambitious for power, and some participants were hired for money, and we knew that resentment against the policy of the ruling authority was among the causes of the revolution. Then we know - by way of restricting the remainder - that the revolutionaries who are neither ambitious for power nor hired for money are those resentful of the policy of the ruling authority. 3 - Among examples of using this method in religious matters are the following: We knew that the cause of the prohibition of some foods is either that they are harmful, or that they are impure, or that they are owned by another. But this delicious food is not harmful, nor impure, nor ritually impure. So what is the cause of its prohibition? We will realize the answer without mental effort: the cause of its prohibition is that it is owned by another, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. We obtained this result because we subtracted from the causes what we knew pertains to another food other than this, and we restricted the matter to the last cause. Therefore, it is the cause of prohibition. It is required here that the survey of causes encompasses all that can be reasoned. Corresponding to this method in Islamic thought: This method of remainder is met by the method of sabr and taqsim in Islamic thought. The difference between the two methods is that the method of remainder distributes the effects in the phenomenon to their causes and restricts the remainder of the phenomenon to the remainder of the causes. As for the method of sabr and taqsim, it is based on enumerating all possible causes, eliminating one after another with evidence, and restricting the matter to the last cause. Sabr and taqsim is used in deriving the effective cause (ʿilla) of a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence, and it has its own principles and regulations. It is required that the survey be exhaustive and the elimination be correct. Sabr and taqsim is one of the methods of 'extracting the basis of the ruling' whose types are known in the science of uṣūl al-fiqh. Perhaps the method of sabr and taqsim is more precise than the method of remainder, and it is possible for the method of sabr and taqsim to encompass the method of remainder if the method of remainder is exhaustive and disciplined. The method of remainder may appear easier – in material operations – than the method of sabr.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage discusses two logical methods: the method of remainder (ṭarīqat al-bawāqī) and the Islamic method of sabr wa taqsim. The author begins by describing the method of remainder as a natural thinking process where one subtracts known causes to identify the unknown cause. He illustrates with a story of poets: when one poet divides poets into three categories (poet, poetaster, fool) and asks which the other is, the respondent quickly claims to be the poetaster, leaving the category 'poet' for al-Mutanabbi and 'fool' for the insulter. This is an example of reasoning by elimination. The author then quotes Stuart Mill's definition: subtract from any phenomenon the parts caused by known causes; the remainder is caused by the remaining causes. Three examples follow: (1) identifying which crime a confessed criminal among several committed; (2) deducing that revolutionaries not motivated by power or money are driven by resentment; (3) a fiqh example: if the possible causes of food prohibition are harm, impurity, being filthy, or being owned by another, and the food is not harmful, filthy, or impure, then the prohibition must be due to ownership. The author notes the condition that the survey of causes must be exhaustive. Next, the author contrasts this with the Islamic method of sabr wa taqsim. The key difference: the method of remainder distributes effects to causes and restricts the remaining effect to the remaining cause, whereas sabr wa taqsim enumerates all possible causes and eliminates them one by one with evidence until only one remains. In Islamic jurisprudence, sabr wa taqsim is used to extract the effective cause (ʿilla) of a ruling, with the requirement that the survey be exhaustive and the elimination correct. The author suggests sabr wa taqsim may be more precise, but the method of remainder may be easier in material matters. This passage is a comparative methodological analysis, highlighting the structure of inductive reasoning and its application in both general and Islamic contexts.

Key Terms
طريقة البواقي
حصر

In a revolution scenario, suppose you know that some participants are motivated by ambition, some by money, and some by resentment. If you can identify that a particular group is neither ambitious nor hired for money, what does the method of remainder tell you about their motivation? Explain.

Sabr wa Taqsim: The Islamic Method of Causation Analysis
Author contrasts sabr wa taqsim with the method of remainder, explaining its use in fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the method of remainder with sabr wa taqsim and apply the latter to identify the 'illa of a ruling by enumerating and eliminating possibilities with evidence.

Core Idea
Sabr wa taqsim eliminates causes one by one with evidence, not just subtraction.

Sabr wa taqsim is the Islamic counterpart to the method of remainder, but it is more rigorous. In this lesson, we will examine the key differences: while the method of remainder subtracts known causes, sabr wa taqsim requires enumerating all possible causes and eliminating each with specific evidence. This method is foundational in usul al-fiqh for deriving the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate all possible causes exhaustively
List every plausible cause that could account for the phenomenon (e.g., prohibition). The list must be complete.
In the food example, the list is harm, impurity, filth, ownership.
2
Eliminate one cause at a time with evidence
For each cause, present a clear argument or evidence that shows it does not apply in this case.
E.g., 'This food is not harmful because it does not cause harm.'
3
Restrict the matter to the remaining cause
After eliminating all but one, the remaining cause is the true 'illa.
Ownership remains after eliminating harm, impurity, and filth.
4
Verify the elimination is correct
Each elimination must be based on sound evidence, not mere opinion.
The author states: 'al-isqāṭ an yakūna ṣaḥīḥan' (the elimination must be correct).
5
Contrast with method of remainder
Note that sabr wa taqsim does not simply subtract known effects; it systematically examines each cause and eliminates it with proof. This makes it more precise for legal reasoning.
The author suggests sabr wa taqsim can encompass the method of remainder if the latter is also exhaustive and disciplined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطريقة الطرح بربط المعلوم بالمعلوم، وحصر الباقي بالباقي، طريق مستخدمة في الفكر اليومي عند الإنسان، لذلك نجد النصوص البليغة كثيراً ما تترك بيان ربط البواقي من الأسباب بمسبباتها، اعتماداً على فهم الفكر له من غير جهد، وإيجازاً في الكلام. وقد ذكرتني هذه الطريقة بقصة بعض الشعراء العاديين، إذ أراد أن يسخر من شاعر آخر فقال له: الشعراء ثلاثة: شاعر، وشعرور، وسخيف (على سبيل الشتيمة)، فأي الشعراء أنت؟ فأجابه صاحبه على الفور: أما أنا فشعرور، واقتسم أنت والمتنبي الباقي. ومعلوم لكليهما أن المتنبي سيأخذ في القسمة وصف شاعر، فبقي الباقي للباقي، وبهذا ردّ الشتيمة عليه، فكان من أراد السخرية هو المسخور منه. وقد عرّف «ستيوارت ميل» هذه الطريقة بقوله: «اطرح من أي ظاهرة الأشياء المعروف أنها معلولة لبعض علل معروفة فيكون الباقي من الظاهرة معلولاً للبعض الآخر من تلك العلل». أمثلة على تطبيق هذه الطريقة: ١ - ظهرت عدة جرائم، وقُبض المجرمون، واعترفوا إلا واحداً منهم لم يعترف بجريمته، فإننا سنحكم بأنه صاحب الجريمة الباقية، سواء اعترف أو لم يعترف، لأن العلم بكونه مجرماً متحقق لدينا، والذي نبحث عنه إنما هو تعيين الجريمة التي ارتكبها هو. ٢ - قامت ثورة على السلطة الحاكمة في بلد ما، فعلمنا أن بعض المشتركين فيها طامعون بالحكم، وأن بعض المشتركين مستأجرون بالمال، وعلمنا أن النقمة على سياسة السلطة الحاكمة من أسباب الثورة، عندئذ نعلم - عن طريق حصر البواقي - أن الثائرين الذين ليسوا طامعين بالحكم ولا مستأجرين بالمال، هم ناقمون على سياسة السلطة الحاكمة. ٣ - ومن أمثلة استخدام هذه الطريقة في الشرعيات ما يلي: علمنا أن السبب في تحريم بعض الأطعمة إما كونها ضارة، وإما كونها مستخبثة، وإما كونها نجسة، وإما كونها ملك الغير. لكن هذا الطعام الشهي ليس ضاراً، ولا مستخبثاً، ولا نجساً، فما السبب في تحريمه؟ سندرك الجواب من غير جهد فكري، إن السبب في تحريمه كونه ملك الغير، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به، وقد حصلنا على هذه النتيجة إذ طرحنا من الأسباب ما نعلم أنه متعلق بطعام آخر غير هذا، وحصرنا الأمر بالسبب الأخير، إذن فهو سبب التحريم. ويشترط هنا أن يكون سبر الأسباب حاصراً لجميع ما يُعلَّل به. مقابل هذه الطريقة في الفكر الإسلامي: يقابل طريقة البواقي هذه طريقة السبر والتقسيم في الفكر الإسلامي، والفارق بين الطريقتين: أن طريقة البواقي توزع المسببات في الظاهرة على أسبابها، وتحصر الباقي من الظاهرة في الباقي من الأسباب، أما طريقة السبر والتقسيم فتقوم على حصر جميع الأسباب المحتملة، وإسقاط واحد بعد آخر بالدليل، وحصر الأمر في السبب الأخير. ويستخدم السبر والتقسيم في استخراج علة الحكم في الفقه الإسلامي، وله عندهم أصول وضوابط، ويشترط في السبر أن يكون حاصراً، وفي الإسقاط أن يكون صحيحاً، والسبر والتقسيم طريق من طرق «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة أنواعه في علم أصول الفقه. وربما كانت طريقة السبر والتقسيم أكثر ضبطاً من طريقة البواقي، وبإمكان طريقة السبر والتقسيم أن تشتمل على طريقة البواقي إذا كانت طريقة البواقي حاصرة ومنضبطة. وقد تبدو طريقة البواقي أيسر ـ في العمليات المادية ـ من طريقة السبر
The method of subtraction by linking the known to the known, and restricting the remainder to the remainder, is a method used in daily human thinking. Therefore we find eloquent texts often leave out the explanation of linking the remaining causes to their effects, relying on the mind's understanding of it without effort, and for brevity in speech. This method reminded me of a story of some ordinary poets; one wanted to mock another poet and said: 'Poets are three: a poet, a poetaster, and a fool (by way of insult). Which of the poets are you?' His companion immediately replied: 'As for me, I am a poetaster. You and al-Mutanabbi divide the rest.' It was known to both that al-Mutanabbi would take the description of poet in the division, so the remainder remained for the remainder, and thus the insult was returned, so the one who intended mockery became the mocked. Stuart Mill defined this method by saying: 'Subtract from any phenomenon the parts known to be caused by certain known causes, and the remainder of the phenomenon is the effect of the remaining causes.' Examples of applying this method: 1 - Several crimes appeared, the criminals were arrested, and they confessed except one who did not confess his crime. We will judge that he is the perpetrator of the remaining crime, whether he confesses or not, because the knowledge that he is a criminal is established for us, and what we are seeking is only to determine which crime he committed. 2 - A revolution arose against the ruling authority in a country. We knew that some participants were ambitious for power, and some participants were hired for money, and we knew that resentment against the policy of the ruling authority was among the causes of the revolution. Then we know - by way of restricting the remainder - that the revolutionaries who are neither ambitious for power nor hired for money are those resentful of the policy of the ruling authority. 3 - Among examples of using this method in religious matters are the following: We knew that the cause of the prohibition of some foods is either that they are harmful, or that they are impure, or that they are owned by another. But this delicious food is not harmful, nor impure, nor ritually impure. So what is the cause of its prohibition? We will realize the answer without mental effort: the cause of its prohibition is that it is owned by another, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. We obtained this result because we subtracted from the causes what we knew pertains to another food other than this, and we restricted the matter to the last cause. Therefore, it is the cause of prohibition. It is required here that the survey of causes encompasses all that can be reasoned. Corresponding to this method in Islamic thought: This method of remainder is met by the method of sabr and taqsim in Islamic thought. The difference between the two methods is that the method of remainder distributes the effects in the phenomenon to their causes and restricts the remainder of the phenomenon to the remainder of the causes. As for the method of sabr and taqsim, it is based on enumerating all possible causes, eliminating one after another with evidence, and restricting the matter to the last cause. Sabr and taqsim is used in deriving the effective cause (ʿilla) of a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence, and it has its own principles and regulations. It is required that the survey be exhaustive and the elimination be correct. Sabr and taqsim is one of the methods of 'extracting the basis of the ruling' whose types are known in the science of uṣūl al-fiqh. Perhaps the method of sabr and taqsim is more precise than the method of remainder, and it is possible for the method of sabr and taqsim to encompass the method of remainder if the method of remainder is exhaustive and disciplined. The method of remainder may appear easier – in material operations – than the method of sabr.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage discusses two logical methods: the method of remainder (ṭarīqat al-bawāqī) and the Islamic method of sabr wa taqsim. The author begins by describing the method of remainder as a natural thinking process where one subtracts known causes to identify the unknown cause. He illustrates with a story of poets: when one poet divides poets into three categories (poet, poetaster, fool) and asks which the other is, the respondent quickly claims to be the poetaster, leaving the category 'poet' for al-Mutanabbi and 'fool' for the insulter. This is an example of reasoning by elimination. The author then quotes Stuart Mill's definition: subtract from any phenomenon the parts caused by known causes; the remainder is caused by the remaining causes. Three examples follow: (1) identifying which crime a confessed criminal among several committed; (2) deducing that revolutionaries not motivated by power or money are driven by resentment; (3) a fiqh example: if the possible causes of food prohibition are harm, impurity, being filthy, or being owned by another, and the food is not harmful, filthy, or impure, then the prohibition must be due to ownership. The author notes the condition that the survey of causes must be exhaustive. Next, the author contrasts this with the Islamic method of sabr wa taqsim. The key difference: the method of remainder distributes effects to causes and restricts the remaining effect to the remaining cause, whereas sabr wa taqsim enumerates all possible causes and eliminates them one by one with evidence until only one remains. In Islamic jurisprudence, sabr wa taqsim is used to extract the effective cause (ʿilla) of a ruling, with the requirement that the survey be exhaustive and the elimination correct. The author suggests sabr wa taqsim may be more precise, but the method of remainder may be easier in material matters. This passage is a comparative methodological analysis, highlighting the structure of inductive reasoning and its application in both general and Islamic contexts.

The Method of Residues in Islamic Legal Reasoning
Author illustrates method of residues with a food prohibition example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of residues to identify the cause of a legal ruling by eliminating known causes.

Core Idea
Eliminate known causes to find the remaining cause—a logical shortcut to discovery.

The method of residues is an inductive procedure where, after accounting for known causes of parts of a phenomenon, the remaining part is attributed to the remaining cause. This lesson unpacks how this method works through a concrete legal example from the passage: determining why a particular food is prohibited. You will learn to identify the steps—identifying possible causes, eliminating those that do not apply, and concluding the remaining cause—and see how this method is related to but distinct from the Islamic method of exploration and division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon and its possible causes
In the example, the phenomenon is the prohibition of certain foods. The passage lists four possible causes: harmfulness, impurity, filthiness, or being the property of another. List them exactly as given.
The causes must be mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive for the method to work.
2
Determine which causes are absent in the specific case
For the specific delicious food, we know it is not harmful, not impure, and not filthy. Eliminate those from the list.
Elimination is based on knowledge—either from direct evidence or from previous rulings.
3
Conclude that the remaining cause is the actual cause
After eliminating three causes, only 'being the property of another' remains. Therefore, the prohibition of this food is due to ownership.
This conclusion assumes that the original list was comprehensive. The passage stresses that the survey must be 'hāsir' (comprehensive).
4
Recognize the relationship to the method of exploration and division
The method of residues distributes known effects among known causes; the method of exploration and division starts with a comprehensive enumeration of all possible causes and then eliminates them one by one. Note that the method of residues can be subsumed under exploration and division if it is comprehensive and disciplined.
This comparison helps situate the method of residues within the broader Islamic methodological tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علمنا أن السبب في تحريم بعض الأطعمة إما كونها ضارة، وإما كونها مستخبثة، وإما كونها نجسة، وإما كونها ملك الغير. لكن هذا الطعام الشهي ليس ضاراً، ولا مستخبثاً، ولا نجساً، فما السبب في تحريمه؟ سندرك الجواب من غير جهد فكري، إن السبب في تحريمه كونه ملك الغير، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به، وقد حصلنا على هذه النتيجة إذ طرحنا من الأسباب ما نعلم أنه متعلق بطعام آخر غير هذا، وحصرنا الأمر بالسبب الأخير، إذن فهو سبب التحريم. ويشترط هنا أن يكون سبر الأسباب حاصراً لجميع ما يُعلَّل به. مقابل هذه الطريقة في الفكر الإسلامي: يقابل طريقة البواقي هذه طريقة السبر والتقسيم في الفكر الإسلامي، والفارق بين الطريقتين: أن طريقة البواقي توزع المسببات في الظاهرة على أسبابها، وتحصر الباقي من الظاهرة في الباقي من الأسباب، أما طريقة السبر والتقسيم فتقوم على حصر جميع الأسباب المحتملة، وإسقاط واحد بعد آخر بالدليل، وحصر الأمر في السبب الأخير. ويستخدم السبر والتقسيم في استخراج علة الحكم في الفقه الإسلامي، وله عندهم أصول وضوابط، ويشترط في السبر أن يكون حاصراً، وفي الإسقاط أن يكون صحيحاً، والسبر والتقسيم طريق من طرق «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة أنواعه في علم أصول الفقه. وربما كانت طريقة السبر والتقسيم أكثر ضبطاً من طريقة البواقي، وبإمكان طريقة السبر والتقسيم أن تشتمل على طريقة البواقي إذا كانت طريقة البواقي حاصرة ومنضبطة. وقد تبدو طريقة البواقي أيسر ـ في العمليات المادية ـ من طريقة السبر والتقسيم القائمة على الترديد الفكري بين الاحتمالات الممكنة كلها، وإسقاط غير الصالح منها للتعليل بالدليل، وحصر العلة بالباقي منها. قيمة طريقة البواقي : يرى «ستيوارت ميل» مبتكر هذه الطريقة أنها من أعظم وسائل الاكتشافات، وأنها نافعة في الاكتشاف أكثر منها في البرهنة، لذلك فهي لا تستخدم في البرهنة على التحقق من صحة الفروض. قالوا : وقد تمكن علماء الطبيعة بوساطة هذه الطريقة من اكتشاف الكوكب (نبتون)، ومن اكتشاف غاز (الأرغون) في الهواء، ومن اكتشاف عنصر (الراديوم) في بعض المعادن. ويقول «هرشل» : إن أكثر الاكتشافات ذات الشأن الخطير التي تمت في علم الفلك، إنما كانت من ثمار استخدام هذه الطريقة.
We know that the reason for prohibiting some foods is either because they are harmful, or because they are impure, or because they are filthy, or because they are the property of another. But this delicious food is not harmful, nor impure, nor filthy, so what is the reason for its prohibition? We will realize the answer without mental effort: the reason for its prohibition is that it is the property of another, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. We have obtained this result when we eliminated from the causes what we know pertains to another food other than this, and we confined the matter to the last cause; thus it is the cause of prohibition. It is required here that the survey of causes be comprehensive of everything that can be used as a cause. Opposite to this method in Islamic thought: The method of residues is contrasted by the method of exploration and division in Islamic thought. The difference between the two methods is that the method of residues distributes the effects in the phenomenon among its causes, and confines the remainder of the phenomenon to the remainder of the causes. As for the method of exploration and division, it is based on enumerating all possible causes, dropping one after another by evidence, and confining the matter to the last cause. The method of exploration and division is used in extracting the effective cause of a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence, and they have principles and controls for it. It is required in the exploration that it be comprehensive, and in the dropping that it be correct. Exploration and division is one of the methods of 'delineating the basis of the ruling' (takhrij manat al-hukm), whose types are well-known in the science of principles of jurisprudence. Perhaps the method of exploration and division is more precise than the method of residues, and it is possible for the method of exploration and division to include the method of residues if the method of residues is comprehensive and disciplined. The method of residues may seem easier — in material operations — than the method of exploration and division, which is based on intellectual consideration among all possible probabilities, dropping those unsuitable for causation by evidence, and confining the cause to the remaining one. The value of the method of residues: John Stuart Mill, the originator of this method, considers it among the greatest means of discoveries, and that it is more useful in discovery than in proof. Therefore, it is not used in proof to verify the correctness of hypotheses. They said: Natural scientists, by means of this method, were able to discover the planet Neptune, and to discover the gas argon in the air, and to discover the element radium in some minerals. Herschel says: Most of the momentous discoveries made in astronomy were the fruits of using this method.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage compares two methods for identifying the cause of a phenomenon: the 'method of residues' (طريقة البواقي) attributed to John Stuart Mill, and the 'method of exploration and division' (طريقة السبر والتقسيم) used in Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh). The author begins with a concrete example from Islamic law: the reason for prohibiting certain foods can be harmfulness, impurity, filthiness, or being the property of another. When a particular delicious food is known not to be harmful, impure, or filthy, the only remaining possible cause is that it belongs to someone else, leading to the conclusion that its prohibition is due to ownership. This illustrates the method of residues: eliminating known causes until only one remains. The author emphasizes that the survey of causes must be comprehensive (حاصر). The passage then introduces the Islamic method of exploration and division (السبر والتقسيم), which systematically enumerates all possible causes (حصر), then eliminates each one with evidence (إسقاط بالدليل) until only the valid cause remains. The key difference is that the method of residues proceeds by distributing known effects among known causes and focusing on the remainder, while the method of exploration and division starts with a comprehensive list of all possible causes and eliminates them one by one. The method of exploration and division is used in fiqh to extract the 'illa (effective cause) of a legal ruling, and it has detailed principles. The author notes that exploration and division is more precise and can subsume the method of residues if the latter is comprehensive and disciplined. However, in material (physical) operations, the method of residues may seem easier because it requires less intellectual deliberation. Finally, the passage acknowledges Mill's high valuation of his method for discovery, citing examples like Neptune, argon, and radium, and quoting Herschel on its importance in astronomy. All examples in this commentary, including the legal example, are drawn from the Islamic tradition as required.

The Method of Exploration and Division (Sabr wa Taqsim) for Identifying the 'Illa
Author describes the method of exploration and division and contrasts it with the method of residues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of exploration and division to systematically identify the effective cause ('illa) of a legal ruling.

Core Idea
Enumerate all possible causes, then eliminate each with evidence until only the true cause remains.

The method of exploration and division (sabr wa taqsim) is a rigorous procedure used in usul al-fiqh to isolate the effective cause ('illa) of a ruling. Unlike the method of residues, which distributes known effects, sabr wa taqsim starts with a comprehensive enumeration of all possible attributes or causes, then eliminates each one with evidence until only one remains. This lesson guides you through the steps of this method, using the same food prohibition example but focusing on the procedural rigor of sabr wa taqsim.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate all possible causes (sabr)
List every conceivable attribute or cause that could be the basis for the ruling. In the example, the possible causes are harmfulness, impurity, filthiness, and being the property of another. The list must be exhaustive (ḥāṣir).
The comprehensiveness of the list is essential; missing a possible cause could lead to a false conclusion.
2
Eliminate each cause with evidence (isqāt)
For each possible cause, provide evidence that it is not applicable to the specific case. In the example, the food is known not to be harmful, not impure, and not filthy based on sensory knowledge or prior rulings. Each elimination must be supported by a valid proof (dalīl).
The evidence must be clear and relevant; the passage states that the elimination (isqāt) must be correct (ṣaḥīḥ).
3
Conclude that the remaining cause is the 'illa
After eliminating all but one cause, that remaining cause is established as the effective cause for the ruling. The conclusion is that the prohibition is due to ownership.
This step is analogous to the method of residues, but sabr wa taqsim requires the initial enumeration to be comprehensive, making it more rigorous.
4
Compare with method of residues
The method of exploration and division is more systematic and precise than the method of residues because it does not rely on prior knowledge of which causes are linked to which effects. It can even subsume the method of residues if the latter's survey is comprehensive.
The author suggests that sabr wa taqsim is 'more precise' (akthar ḍabṭan) and can include the method of residues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علمنا أن السبب في تحريم بعض الأطعمة إما كونها ضارة، وإما كونها مستخبثة، وإما كونها نجسة، وإما كونها ملك الغير. لكن هذا الطعام الشهي ليس ضاراً، ولا مستخبثاً، ولا نجساً، فما السبب في تحريمه؟ سندرك الجواب من غير جهد فكري، إن السبب في تحريمه كونه ملك الغير، فلا يجوز الانتفاع به، وقد حصلنا على هذه النتيجة إذ طرحنا من الأسباب ما نعلم أنه متعلق بطعام آخر غير هذا، وحصرنا الأمر بالسبب الأخير، إذن فهو سبب التحريم. ويشترط هنا أن يكون سبر الأسباب حاصراً لجميع ما يُعلَّل به. مقابل هذه الطريقة في الفكر الإسلامي: يقابل طريقة البواقي هذه طريقة السبر والتقسيم في الفكر الإسلامي، والفارق بين الطريقتين: أن طريقة البواقي توزع المسببات في الظاهرة على أسبابها، وتحصر الباقي من الظاهرة في الباقي من الأسباب، أما طريقة السبر والتقسيم فتقوم على حصر جميع الأسباب المحتملة، وإسقاط واحد بعد آخر بالدليل، وحصر الأمر في السبب الأخير. ويستخدم السبر والتقسيم في استخراج علة الحكم في الفقه الإسلامي، وله عندهم أصول وضوابط، ويشترط في السبر أن يكون حاصراً، وفي الإسقاط أن يكون صحيحاً، والسبر والتقسيم طريق من طرق «تخريج مناط الحكم» المعروفة أنواعه في علم أصول الفقه. وربما كانت طريقة السبر والتقسيم أكثر ضبطاً من طريقة البواقي، وبإمكان طريقة السبر والتقسيم أن تشتمل على طريقة البواقي إذا كانت طريقة البواقي حاصرة ومنضبطة. وقد تبدو طريقة البواقي أيسر ـ في العمليات المادية ـ من طريقة السبر والتقسيم القائمة على الترديد الفكري بين الاحتمالات الممكنة كلها، وإسقاط غير الصالح منها للتعليل بالدليل، وحصر العلة بالباقي منها. قيمة طريقة البواقي : يرى «ستيوارت ميل» مبتكر هذه الطريقة أنها من أعظم وسائل الاكتشافات، وأنها نافعة في الاكتشاف أكثر منها في البرهنة، لذلك فهي لا تستخدم في البرهنة على التحقق من صحة الفروض. قالوا : وقد تمكن علماء الطبيعة بوساطة هذه الطريقة من اكتشاف الكوكب (نبتون)، ومن اكتشاف غاز (الأرغون) في الهواء، ومن اكتشاف عنصر (الراديوم) في بعض المعادن. ويقول «هرشل» : إن أكثر الاكتشافات ذات الشأن الخطير التي تمت في علم الفلك، إنما كانت من ثمار استخدام هذه الطريقة.
We know that the reason for prohibiting some foods is either because they are harmful, or because they are impure, or because they are filthy, or because they are the property of another. But this delicious food is not harmful, nor impure, nor filthy, so what is the reason for its prohibition? We will realize the answer without mental effort: the reason for its prohibition is that it is the property of another, so it is not permissible to benefit from it. We have obtained this result when we eliminated from the causes what we know pertains to another food other than this, and we confined the matter to the last cause; thus it is the cause of prohibition. It is required here that the survey of causes be comprehensive of everything that can be used as a cause. Opposite to this method in Islamic thought: The method of residues is contrasted by the method of exploration and division in Islamic thought. The difference between the two methods is that the method of residues distributes the effects in the phenomenon among its causes, and confines the remainder of the phenomenon to the remainder of the causes. As for the method of exploration and division, it is based on enumerating all possible causes, dropping one after another by evidence, and confining the matter to the last cause. The method of exploration and division is used in extracting the effective cause of a ruling in Islamic jurisprudence, and they have principles and controls for it. It is required in the exploration that it be comprehensive, and in the dropping that it be correct. Exploration and division is one of the methods of 'delineating the basis of the ruling' (takhrij manat al-hukm), whose types are well-known in the science of principles of jurisprudence. Perhaps the method of exploration and division is more precise than the method of residues, and it is possible for the method of exploration and division to include the method of residues if the method of residues is comprehensive and disciplined. The method of residues may seem easier — in material operations — than the method of exploration and division, which is based on intellectual consideration among all possible probabilities, dropping those unsuitable for causation by evidence, and confining the cause to the remaining one. The value of the method of residues: John Stuart Mill, the originator of this method, considers it among the greatest means of discoveries, and that it is more useful in discovery than in proof. Therefore, it is not used in proof to verify the correctness of hypotheses. They said: Natural scientists, by means of this method, were able to discover the planet Neptune, and to discover the gas argon in the air, and to discover the element radium in some minerals. Herschel says: Most of the momentous discoveries made in astronomy were the fruits of using this method.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage compares two methods for identifying the cause of a phenomenon: the 'method of residues' (طريقة البواقي) attributed to John Stuart Mill, and the 'method of exploration and division' (طريقة السبر والتقسيم) used in Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh). The author begins with a concrete example from Islamic law: the reason for prohibiting certain foods can be harmfulness, impurity, filthiness, or being the property of another. When a particular delicious food is known not to be harmful, impure, or filthy, the only remaining possible cause is that it belongs to someone else, leading to the conclusion that its prohibition is due to ownership. This illustrates the method of residues: eliminating known causes until only one remains. The author emphasizes that the survey of causes must be comprehensive (حاصر). The passage then introduces the Islamic method of exploration and division (السبر والتقسيم), which systematically enumerates all possible causes (حصر), then eliminates each one with evidence (إسقاط بالدليل) until only the valid cause remains. The key difference is that the method of residues proceeds by distributing known effects among known causes and focusing on the remainder, while the method of exploration and division starts with a comprehensive list of all possible causes and eliminates them one by one. The method of exploration and division is used in fiqh to extract the 'illa (effective cause) of a legal ruling, and it has detailed principles. The author notes that exploration and division is more precise and can subsume the method of residues if the latter is comprehensive and disciplined. However, in material (physical) operations, the method of residues may seem easier because it requires less intellectual deliberation. Finally, the passage acknowledges Mill's high valuation of his method for discovery, citing examples like Neptune, argon, and radium, and quoting Herschel on its importance in astronomy. All examples in this commentary, including the legal example, are drawn from the Islamic tradition as required.

The Three Pillars of Proof
Author enumerates three necessary components for any proof: principles, subject, and issue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three pillars of proof and give examples of each from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Every proof in the Islamic sciences rests on three pillars: premises, a subject, and a specific issue.

This passage lays out the essential components any valid argument must have. Before you can construct or evaluate a proof, you need to understand its building blocks: the premises you rely on, the domain you are arguing within, and the exact claim you are proving or disproving. The author calls these the three pillars (arkan), and they apply to every type of reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first pillar: principles (mabadi')
Find in the passage what the author lists as the types of premises. They include rational self-evidents, sensory perceptions, empirical things, and reports. Note that these can be certain or probable.
The author attaches 'mujarrabat' (empirical generalizations) to sensory perceptions.
2
Identify the second pillar: subject (mawdu')
The author defines the subject as the general framework of a science or art. Examples given: arithmetic's subject is number, fiqh's subject is acts of mukallafin. Understand that each science has a unique subject that confines its inquiries.
3
Identify the third pillar: issue (mas'ala)
The issue is a specific proposition belonging to the subject that the proof aims to establish or negate. It is the particular question under investigation within that science's domain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يُحتاج إليه لدى إقامة الأدلة
كلّ راغب في إقامة دليلٍ ما لا يستقيم لديه الأمر إلّا بتوافر ثلاثة أركان :
الركن الأول : مبادىء تتمثل بمقدّمات يعتمد عليها المستدلّ لدى إقامة دليله، وهي : أوليات يقينيّة عقلية - ومدركات حسّيّة بالحواسّ الظاهرة أو الباطنة، وهذه الباطنة قد تُسمّى بالوجدانيّات - ويُلْحَقُ بالمدركات الحسية المجرّبات، باعتبار أنّ نتائج التجربات مدركة بالحواس - أو خبريّات يقينيّة أو ظنيّة صالحة للاحتجاج بها لدى المخالف .
الركن الثاني : موضوع ينحصر الاستدلال في إطاره العامّ، ومن المعلوم أنّ لكلّ علم من العلوم أو فنّ من الفنون موضوعاً تدور في إطاره العامّ مسائله .
فموضوع الحساب العدد. وموضوع الهندسة مقادير الأشياء ذوات الأبعاد. وموضوع الفقه أفعال المكلفين . وموضوع العقائد أركان الإيمان .
الركن الثالث : مسألة أو قضيّة من قضايا الموضوع، إذْ يراد إقامة الدليل على إثباتها أو نفيها .
What is needed when establishing proofs. Everyone who wishes to establish a proof cannot have the matter correct except with the availability of three pillars: The first pillar: Principles consisting of premises upon which the arguer relies in establishing his proof, and they are: certain rational self-evidents, and sensory perceptions by the external or internal senses — these internal ones may be called 'found things' (wijdaniyyat) — and attached to sensory perceptions are empirical things (mujarrabat), considering that the results of experiments are perceived by the senses — or certain or probable reports that are suitable for argumentation against the opponent. The second pillar: A subject within whose general framework the inference is confined, and it is known that every science or art has a subject around which its issues revolve in its general framework. Thus the subject of arithmetic is number; the subject of geometry is the quantities of extended things; the subject of jurisprudence is the acts of the legally responsible persons; the subject of doctrines (creeds) is the pillars of faith. The third pillar: An issue or proposition from the issues of the subject, for the proof is intended to establish or negate it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a classification that outlines the three essential components (arkan) for any valid proof or argument. The author begins by stating that anyone wishing to establish a proof must have three pillars. The first pillar (al-ruknu al-awwal) is the principles (mabadi'), defined as the premises (muqaddimat) that the arguer relies on. These premises include certain rational self-evidents (awwaliyat yaqiniyya 'aqliyya), sensory perceptions from external or internal senses (the latter often called wijdaniyyat, 'found things'), and what is attached to sensory perceptions: empirical observations (mujarrabat), because the results of experiments are perceived by the senses. Additionally, reports (khabariyyat) that are certain or probable can be used if they are acceptable to the opponent. The second pillar (al-ruknu al-thani) is the subject (mawdu'), which provides the general framework for the inference. Every science or art has its own subject; for example, arithmetic's subject is number, geometry's subject is quantities of extended things, fiqh's subject is the acts of legally responsible persons (af'al al-mukallafin), and the subject of creedal theology is the pillars of faith (arkan al-iman). The third pillar (al-ruknu al-thalith) is the issue or proposition (mas'ala aw qadiyya) from among the issues of that subject, which the arguer aims to prove or negate. This structure shows that a proof is not merely a logical form but requires proper premises, a defined domain, and a specific question.

Key Terms
مبادىء (mabadi')
موضوع (mawdu')
مسألة (mas'ala)

Key Takeaway
A valid proof in any Islamic science requires three pillars: principles (premises), a subject (domain), and a specific issue to be proven or disproven.

The Three Pillars of a Valid Proof
Author enumerates the three necessary components for constructing a valid proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three pillars required for any valid proof in Islamic logic and distinguish between them.

Core Idea
Every proof stands on three pillars: principles, subject matter, and issue.

In this passage, the author lays the groundwork for all evidence-based reasoning by enumerating three indispensable components. Without any one of these, no proof can be sound. Let's unpack each pillar and see how they function in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three pillars
Scan the Arabic for the three enumerated items: مبادىء (mabadi'), موضوع (mawdu'), مسألة (mas'ala). Notice they are introduced as أركان (arkan).
2
Analyze the first pillar: principles
The author divides principles into categories: rational certainties, sensory perceptions (external and internal), empirical findings, and reports (certain or probable). These are the premises the arguer relies on.
Internal sensations are also called wijdaniyyat.
3
Examine the second pillar: subject matter
Every science has a domain (mawdu') that circumscribes inference. Examples: arithmetic = number, geometry = magnitudes, fiqh = actions of mukallafin, creed = pillars of faith.
4
Understand the third pillar: issue
The specific proposition (mas'ala) within the subject matter that the proof aims to prove or disprove.
Do not confuse the issue with the subject matter; the latter is the entire field.
5
Apply to an Islamic science
Take fiqh: subject matter is actions of mukallafin. An issue could be "Is wine forbidden?" The proof uses principles from Qur'an, Sunnah, ijma', etc. Verify all three pillars are present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلّ راغب في إقامة دليلٍ ما لا يستقيم لديه الأمر إلّا بتوافر ثلاثة أركان : الركن الأول : مبادىء تتمثل بمقدّمات يعتمد عليها المستدلّ لدى إقامة دليله، وهي : أوليات يقينيّة عقلية - ومدركات حسّيّة بالحواسّ الظاهرة أو الباطنة، وهذه الباطنة قد تُسمّى بالوجدانيّات - ويُلْحَقُ بالمدركات الحسية المجرّبات، باعتبار أنّ نتائج التجربات مدركة بالحواس - أو خبريّات يقينيّة أو ظنيّة صالحة للاحتجاج بها لدى المخالف . الركن الثاني : موضوع ينحصر الاستدلال في إطاره العامّ، ومن المعلوم أنّ لكلّ علم من العلوم أو فنّ من الفنون موضوعاً تدور في إطاره العامّ مسائله . فموضوع الحساب العدد. وموضوع الهندسة مقادير الأشياء ذوات الأبعاد. وموضوع الفقه أفعال المكلفين . وموضوع العقائد أركان الإيمان . الركن الثالث : مسألة أو قضيّة من قضايا الموضوع، إذْ يراد إقامة الدليل على إثباتها أو نفيها .
Every desirer of establishing a proof cannot have the matter be sound for him except by the availability of three pillars: The first pillar: principles consisting of premises upon which the arguer relies in establishing his proof, and they are: rational certainties known by necessity, and sensory perceptions by the external or internal senses—these internal ones are sometimes called inner sensations—and the empirical findings are annexed to sensory perceptions, considering that the results of experiences are perceived by the senses, or reports that are certain or probable, suitable for argumentation against the opponent. The second pillar: a subject matter within whose general framework the inference is confined; and it is known that every science or art has a subject matter within whose general framework its issues revolve. So the subject of arithmetic is number. The subject of geometry is the magnitudes of things having dimensions. The subject of jurisprudence is the actions of morally responsible persons. The subject of creed are the pillars of faith. The third pillar: an issue or proposition from among the propositions of the subject matter, since the proof is intended to establish or negate it.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the three essential pillars (arkan) for constructing any valid proof (dalil). The author first states that a proof cannot be sound without these three. Then he details each: The first pillar is the principles (mabadi'), which are the premises (muqaddimat) relied upon. These include rational certainties (awwaliyat yaqiniyya 'aqliyya), sensory perceptions (mudrakat hissiyya) by external or internal senses (the latter called wijdaniyyat), empirical findings (mujarrabat) annexed to sensory perceptions, and reports (khabariyyat) that are certain or probable. The second pillar is the subject matter (mawdu'), which circumscribes the inference. Every science has a distinct subject matter; e.g., arithmetic's subject is number, geometry's is magnitudes, fiqh's is actions of the morally responsible, and 'aqa'id's is the pillars of faith. The third pillar is an issue (mas'ala) or proposition from that subject matter, which the proof aims to affirm or deny. Thus, the passage provides a structured foundation for all evidence-based reasoning, especially relevant to Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

The Two Fundamental Questions: هل and ما
Author explains the types of questions asked in seeking knowledge: هل and ما.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between questions about existence/description (using هل) and questions about definition (using ما) as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Every inquiry reduces to هل (existence or description) or ما (definition).

In this passage, the author lays out the foundational categories of questions we ask when seeking knowledge. Understanding these categories helps you recognise what type of answer you need and whether a given response is appropriate. We will walk through the two main question types and their sub-categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interrogative tools
The author uses two Arabic interrogative particles: 'هل' and 'ما'. Note that each particle is associated with a distinct set of questions about the thing being investigated.
These are not the only interrogatives, but they are fundamental in the tradition of definition and inquiry.
2
Distinguish the questions associated with 'هل'
The passage shows 'هل' is used for two types: (1) asking about the origin of existence, e.g., 'هل الجن موجودون؟' (Are jinn existent?), and (2) asking about a description or attribute, e.g., 'هل الجن يتناكحون ويتناسلون؟' (Do jinn marry and procreate?).
So 'هل' does not only ask about existence but also about attributes.
3
Distinguish the questions associated with 'ما'
The author explains that 'ما' is used for three kinds of definition: explanation of essence (hadd), distinguishing properties (rasm), and clarifying word meaning (ta'rif lafzi). Examples are given for each: truth, spirit, and the word 'qaswara'.
The answer to a 'ما' question tells you what something is, but the type of definition varies.
4
Recognize the incomplete third demand
The passage begins a third category (causes: efficient and final) using a different interrogative, but it is cut off. Be aware that the full framework originally includes a third type of question about why something occurs.
We only cover what is present; the third demand will be treated in a later lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(6) أمهات المطالب
حين نبحث عن المعرفة طالبين الوصول إليها، فإننا نسأل عما نريد أن نعرفه من عدة وجوه:
أ - نسأل عن أصل وجوده، أو نسأل عن وصفه، ونستعمل في السؤال عن ذلك أداة الاستفهام (هل؟).
فنقول مثلاً في السؤال عن أصل الوجود: هل الجن موجودون؟.
ونقول مثلاً في السؤال عن الوصف: هل الجن يتناكحون ويتناسلون؟.
ب - ونسأل عن تعريفه، إذ نطلب شرح حقيقته بالحدّ، أو تمييزه بخواصه بالرسم، أو بيان المراد من اللفظ بالتعريف اللفظي.
ونستعمل في السؤال عن أحد هذه المطالب أداة الاستفهام (ما؟).
فنقول مثلاً في السؤال عن شرح الحقيقة: ما هو الصدق؟.
والجواب: هو القول المطابق للواقع.
ونقول مثلاً في السؤال عن الخواص المميزة: ما هي الروح؟.
والجواب: سر من أسرار الله به تقوم الحياة.
ونقول في السؤال عن التعريف اللفظي: ما هو القسورة؟.
والجواب: هو الأسد، أو الصياد.
جـ - ونسأل عن العلّة المؤثرة، وعن العلة الغائية التي هي الهدف من
(6) The Fundamental Demands. When we seek knowledge, desiring to reach it, we ask about what we want to know from several aspects: A - We ask about the origin of its existence, or we ask about its description, and we use for that the interrogative tool 'Hal?'. For example, in asking about the origin of existence: Are jinn existent? And for example, in asking about description: Do jinn marry and procreate? B - We ask about its definition, as we seek explanation of its essence by a definition (hadd), or distinguishing it by its properties through a description (rasm), or clarifying the meaning of the word by a verbal definition. And we use for one of these demands the interrogative tool 'Ma?'. For example, in asking about explanation of essence: What is truth? The answer: It is a statement corresponding to reality. And for example, in asking about distinguishing properties: What is the spirit? The answer: It is a secret from the secrets of Allah by which life subsists. And we say in asking about verbal definition: What is al-qaswara? The answer: It is the lion, or the hunter. C - We ask about the efficient cause and the final cause which is the goal of ...
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage introduces the 'Fundamental Demands' (Ummahat al-Matalib) in the quest for knowledge. The author explains that when seeking knowledge, we ask questions from several aspects. First (A), we ask about existence (هل) or description. Examples: existence of jinn or their attributes like marrying and procreating. Second (B), we ask about definition using the tool 'ما' (ma). This includes three sub-types: explaining the essence by a real definition (hadd), distinguishing by characteristic properties through a description (rasm), or clarifying the meaning of a word by a verbal definition (ta'rif lafzi). Examples are given for each: truth defined as statement corresponding to reality, spirit defined as a divine secret, and the word 'qaswara' defined as lion or hunter. Third (C) begins to discuss the efficient cause and final cause, but the passage cuts off. The author is outlining the main categories of questions that direct inquiry.

Hadd, Rasm, and Ta'rif Lafzi: The Three Types of Definition
Author explains the three types of definition under the ما question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three types of definition (hadd, rasm, or ta'rif lafzi) is being used based on the content of the answer.

Core Idea
A definition may give the essence (hadd), distinguishing properties (rasm), or just a synonym (ta'rif lafzi).

In the second part of the passage, the author breaks down the 'ما' question into three distinct types of definition: hadd, rasm, and ta'rif lafzi. Each answers 'what is it?' but in a different way. We will examine the examples given to see how each type works and how to recognise them.

Guided Path
1
List the three types of definition
From the passage, identify the three sub-categories under the 'ما' question: 1) شرح الحقيقة بالحد (explaining essence by hadd), 2) تمييز بخواص بالرسم (distinguishing by properties via rasm), 3) بيان المراد من اللفظ بالتعريف اللفظي (clarifying word meaning by ta'rif lafzi).
These are the standard division in the logical tradition.
2
Analyze the hadd example
The question 'ما هو الصدق؟' (What is truth?) is answered 'هو القول المطابق للواقع' (It is a statement corresponding to reality). This gives the genus (statement) and the differentia (corresponding to reality), thus explaining the essence.
A hadd definition uses genus and differentia to capture the quiddity.
3
Analyze the rasm example
The question 'ما هي الروح؟' (What is the spirit?) is answered 'سر من أسرار الله به تقوم الحياة' (A secret from the secrets of Allah by which life subsists). This does not give a genus and differentia but describes a distinctive property (life subsists by it).
A rasm definition identifies the thing by its unique characteristics, not its essence.
4
Analyze the ta'rif lafzi example
The question 'ما هو القسورة؟' (What is al-qaswara?) is answered 'هو الأسد، أو الصياد' (It is the lion, or the hunter). This clarifies the meaning of the word by providing synonyms or explanations.
Ta'rif lafzi is merely a verbal explanation, not a logical definition.
5
Compare the three types
Notice the difference: hadd gives the essence (what makes the thing what it is), rasm gives distinguishing properties (what marks it out without revealing essence), and ta'rif lafzi gives a word's meaning (often for unfamiliar terms).
In Islamic sciences, the most important is hadd, but rasm is often used when essence is unknown.
