Distinguishing Logical and Usuli General/Specific Terms
Author distinguishes a'amm/akhass in logic from 'amm/khass in usul, then explains usuli entailment rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between the logical terms a'amm/akhass and the usuli terms 'amm/khass, and apply the correct rule of entailment for usuli 'amm/khass.

Core Idea
The logician's 'broader/narrower' is about concepts; the usuli's 'general/specific' is about words and their scope.

This lesson clarifies a crucial terminological distinction that prevents confusion when studying logic and usul al-fiqh. While both fields use terms for generality and specificity, they refer to different things and have different logical properties. You will learn how to identify each set and apply the correct rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sets of terms
Locate in the passage where the author introduces 'a'amm/akhass' for logic and ''amm/khass' for usul. Note that they are distinct technical terms.
2
Understand the definitions
In logic, a'amm means broader extension (e.g., animal includes man) and akhass means narrower (e.g., man). In usul, 'amm is a word covering all its individuals (e.g., 'men'), and khass is a word for a specific individual or limited set (e.g., 'Zayd').
The usuli definition is linguistic, not conceptual.
3
Examine the entailment rule for usuli terms
The author states: a judgment on the general ('amm) entails the judgment on every specific (khass), but a judgment on a specific does not entail the judgment on the general. This one-way entailment is key.
4
Apply the rule to examples
Consider the example: 'All land creatures need food' (general) applies to men, beasts, etc. (specifics). But 'Some people are greedy' (specific) does not force 'All people are greedy' (general).
This contrasts with logical a'amm/akhass where a property of the broader class does not necessarily transfer to the narrower (e.g., 'animal' can move, but 'man' must move? Actually in logic, all properties of akhass are not necessarily in a'amm, but a'amm's properties are in akhass? Wait: in logic, if a'amm is predicable of akhass, but not vice versa. However, the passage is about usuli entailment. Be careful: the logical relation is different. For clarity, the author is only explaining usuli entailment here. In logic, a'amm includes akhass extensionally, but a property of a'amm (e.g., 'animal' has sensation) applies to akhass ('man' has sensation). That is similar to usuli entailment? Actually, the author's point is that these are different fields. So stick to usuli rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ملاحظة :
الأعم والأخص في الاصطلاح عند المناطقة غير العام والخاص :
فالأعم والأخص هو ما شرحنا في مصطلح علماء المنطق . أما العام
والخاص فهما مصطلحان آخران معروفان عند علماء أصول الفقه وغيرهم .
ويعرف الأصوليون العام : بأنه اللفظ المستغرق لجميع ما يصلح له
بحسب وضع واحد، مثل : الرجال، والنساء، والدراهم، والدنانير، والناس .
ويعرفون الخاص بأنه اللفظ الدال على مسمى واحد، مثل زيد وعمرو
من الناس، ونحو ذلك .
فبالنسبة إلى العام والخاص نقول :
إن الحكم على العام (بمعنى الحكم على كل أفراد العام) يستلزم
الحكم على الخاص سواء أكان الحكم موجباً أو سالباً، لكن الحكم على
الخاص لا يستلزم الحكم على العام، لاحتمال أن يكون غير هذا الخاص من
أفراد العام لا ينطبق عليه هذا الحكم .
فإذا قلنا : إن الأحياء الأرضية تحتاج إلى الغذاء لتستمر حياتها، فإن هذا
الكلام ينطبق على الناس، والدواب، والأسماك، والطيور، والحشرات،
وغيرها، لأن كل هذه مشمولة بالحكم الذي قررناه، بمقتضى صيغة العموم .
لكن إذا حكمنا على فئة من الناس بالطمع والشره، فلا يلزم من ذلك
أن يكون كل الناس أهل طمع وشره .

القاعدة الرابعة : العلم يتبع المعلوم، وليس المعلوم هو الذي يتبع
العلم .
وهذه القاعدة تنطبق على علم المخلوقات، فما هو قابل لأن يُعلم يكون
أولاً حقيقة في ذاته، ثم يكون علم المخلوق به بعد ذلك .
Note:
The more general (a'amm) and the more specific (akhass) in the terminology of the logicians are different from the general ('amm) and the specific (khass):
For a'amm and akhass are what we have explained in the terminology of the logicians. As for 'amm and khass, they are two other terms known among the scholars of Usul al-Fiqh and others.
The usulis define 'amm (general) as: the word that encompasses all that it is suitable for according to a single usage, like: men, women, dirhams, dinars, and people.
And they define khass (specific) as: the word that indicates a single named thing, like Zayd and Amr from people, and the like.
So with respect to 'amm and khass we say:
Indeed, the judgment on the general (meaning the judgment on every individual of the general) entails the judgment on the specific, whether the judgment is affirmative or negative, but the judgment on the specific does not entail the judgment on the general, due to the possibility that other individuals of the general besides this specific one may not be covered by this judgment.
If we say: 'Land creatures need nourishment to continue their lives,' then this statement applies to people, beasts, fish, birds, insects, and others, because all of these are included in the judgment we have stated, by virtue of the general wording.
But if we judge a group of people as greedy and gluttonous, it does not necessarily follow that all people are greedy and gluttonous.

The fourth principle: Knowledge follows the known, and it is not the known that follows knowledge.
This principle applies to the knowledge of creatures: what is capable of being known is first a reality in itself, then the knowledge of the creature comes after that.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage first clarifies a terminological distinction: the terms 'a'amm' and 'akhass' used in logic are not the same as ''amm' and 'khass' used in usul al-fiqh. In logic, a'amm refers to a broader concept in extension (e.g., animal is broader than man), while akhass is narrower. In usul, 'amm is a word that covers all its instances by a single usage (e.g., 'men' includes all men), and khass is a word for a specific individual or limited set (e.g., 'Zayd'). The author then explains the logical relation in usul: a ruling on the general ('amm) applies to every specific instance, but a ruling on a specific instance does not necessarily apply to the general (since other instances may not share the ruling). An example is given: the statement that all land creatures need food applies to every creature, but a judgment that some people are greedy does not extend to all. Finally, the author introduces the fourth principle: 'Knowledge follows the known.' This is an epistemological principle stating that the object of knowledge has an independent reality prior to being known; the knower's knowledge conforms to the object, not the other way around. This applies to created beings: they exist as realities in themselves, and then knowledge of them occurs.

Key Terms
الأعم والأخص
العام
الخاص

Key Takeaway
The usuli terms 'amm and khass have a specific linguistic meaning and a one-way entailment rule: general implies specific, but not vice versa.

The Fourth Principle: Knowledge Follows the Known
Author introduces the fourth principle that knowledge follows the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological principle 'knowledge follows the known' and identify its implications in Islamic thought.

Core Idea
The known object has real existence independent of the knower; knowledge conforms to it, not the other way around.

This lesson introduces a foundational epistemological principle that underlies how Islamic scholars understand the relationship between the mind and reality. It is crucial for debates in kalam and usul about the nature of knowledge, ensuring that truth is based on objective reality rather than subjective perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement of the fourth principle
Find in the passage: 'The fourth principle: Knowledge follows the known, and it is not the known that follows knowledge.' This is the core assertion.
2
Analyze the meaning
The known object (ma'lum) has a reality in itself prior to being known. Knowledge is a representation or reflection of that reality. The principle affirms the primacy of existence over knowledge.
3
Contrast with alternative views
This rejects any form of idealism or subjectivism where reality depends on the mind. The principle says the object exists first, then knowledge of it occurs.
4
Consider the scope
The author says this applies to 'the knowledge of creatures' (al-makhluqat). This distinguishes created knowledge (dependent on the object) from divine knowledge (which is creative, not derivative).
This limitation is important in kalam discussions.
